Flowers in the Water

by Cassie_Bones

Summary

Agatha was born on land, but she was raised in the sea. As a mermaid, she had one very important rule: do not interact with humans.

But Agatha has never been very good at following rules.

Naturally, she gets caught up, literally, by a fishing boat and meets probably the most beautiful human she's ever laid eyes on.

Rio had always had an interest in sea life. Her father was a fisherman, so it was only natural that she follow in his footsteps, listening to his stories of the mythological creatures that live below the surface.

Her favorite stories are those of the mermaids though she knows that they're made up...or are they?

When her fishing boat catches a mysterious (and beautiful) woman miles from shore, she starts to believe that there may be some truth to her father's stories.

Notes

Well....here it is. Three months' worth of writing. I hope y'all enjoy it!

Chapter 1

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Prologue

The island was quiet. 

Or as quiet as an island can be when the tide is high. Water lapped at the sandy beaches, leaving foam in its wake as it retreated back to the sea, only to return, creeping higher and higher up the shore with each wave. There was a blood moon tonight, casting an eerie red glow over the water below.

By this time of night, any visitors to the island had taken leave, and only the locals remained. Some still milled about the town in the center of the island, closing up shops or catching one last drink at the few bars open this late on a Wednesday night, but most of the townspeople were already tucked away in their beds, soothed by the lullaby of the ocean waves lapping at the shore.

The beaches were empty.

Almost empty.

A long figure stumbled across the sand, whispering curses to herself as she clutched a bundle in her arms. The bundle made noises, too: whimpers and squeaks and tiny cries that only seemed to aggravate the figure more. 

“Hush!” the figure said. “You evil thing.” 

The figure squeezed her bundle, which let out a much louder cry in response. It was ignored as the figure hastened her steps toward the shore, stumbling further in the uneven sand. The long skirt of her bloodied nightgown didn’t help her much. She let out a growl of frustration, glaring at the bundle in her arms.

“You evil, evil thing!” she pinched a chubby arm reaching out for her and the bundle let out a cry. 

Finally, her feet reached the water and she breathed a sigh of relief: it would all be over soon. 

The tide retreated, leaving seafoam in its wake as the figure crouched down and laid the squirming bundle in the wet sand. The infant was fair-skinned and blue-eyed, with ten perfect little fingers and ten perfect little toes. She had a tuft of chestnut brown hair on her little head and a scrunched-up little face. Her cheeks were rosy and wet, but not from the sea. Not yet.

“You evil thing,” the figure said again as she left the newborn on the shore, wrapped only in a stained kitchen towel and nothing more. She glared at the infant, who continued to reach out for comfort as the brisk air washed over her, blowing through the little hairs on her head. The icy water flowed toward her fast as the tide came in, surrounding her in its frigid embrace. The baby let out a wail of discomfort as her body was dragged toward the sea. “You will hurt no one,” the figure swore, coldly. “Ever.” 

With that, she spun on her heel and stumbled back up the beach, certain that what she had just done was the right thing.

She did not watch as her newborn infant was swallowed up by the tide and carried fully out to sea. She did not witness the hands that plucked the child out of the water and tossed aside that old stained dish towel, instead wrapping her in a different type of cloth, one that surrounded her in warmth and security. She did not take notice of the child’s cries dying out, assuming that they had been silenced for good by the sea rather than by the soothing touch of another being just out of sight.

“Shh,” a voice soothed the infant, cradling her against her breast. “It’s all alright now. I’ve got you.”

The infant blinked blearily up at the new figure now holding her, with dripping brown curls and a kind smile, amber eyes shining with warmth. One finger stroked a downy cheek and a smaller one came out to grasp it.

“Ooh, strong one,” the figure cooed, pressing a kiss to the newborn’s forehead. “Intelligent, too. I can tell. I’m rarely wrong about these things, you know.” She chuckled as the baby’s eyes fluttered, sleep beginning to overtake her as she finally found safety in the arms of another. “That’s it, sweet girl,” the figure said, tucking the child into a garment wrapped around her frame, keeping her closely strapped to her chest, “sleep well.” She pulled something from a pocket on the garment and placed it in the child’s mouth: chupo, they called it. The infant took to it immediately as slits opened at the sides of her chubby little neck. 

The figure secured her and placed her lips against the little nose in a soothing peck before she began her descent into the ocean, leaving the blood moon behind them.

Chapter One

Thirty Years Later…

“Who can tell me the difference between a mermaid and a Siren?”

Only one hand went up. Agatha ignored it with a sigh.

“Come on, guys,” she said. “We’ve been over this a thousand times now! Sirens are…” She prompted with waving hands and widening eyes, looking all around the classroom at her unwilling participants until they breathed a collective sigh of irritation and responded.

“Seaborn mermaids who rescue abandoned infants from the shore and create new mermaids,” her class of seven droned, obviously bored out of their skulls. 

Agatha sighed, her shoulders drooping. “Don’t sound so excited,” she huffed. “And mermaids are…”

The students glanced at each other, clueless.

One hand went up again, and she brightened a little bit before frowning. 

“Billy, what did I say about raising your hand?” Agatha asked, tilting her head.

Billy’s hand slowly went down as he deflated. “To let others have a turn?”

“Mhmm,” Agatha said. “Like maybe…your brother!” She slapped her hand on his twin’s desk, and the boy jumped awake. “Are you guys not getting enough sleep or something? Why are you always so tired, Tobey?” She glared at the droopy-eyed boy as she asked this.

He just shrugged as he wiped sleep from his eyes. “Dunno,” he said. “And my name’s not Tobey. It’s—"

Agatha rolled her eyes and shook her head. “Don’t care. Class dismissed,” she said. “I am far too tired to deal with any of your shit right now. Get lost.”

She made her way back to the front of the room, sat on her boulder and pressed her face into the palms of her hands. She waited until it was completely quiet before raising her head, practically jumping out of her scales when Billy appeared.

“Poseidon!” she huffed. “How do you not make any noise when you swim?”

The teen shrugged his bony shoulders.

“What do you want, Billiam?”

“That’s not my—” At Agatha’s glare, he sighed. “Doesn’t matter.”

“Exactly,” Agatha said, pointing at him. “Now you’re getting it.”

“Why are you like this?” Billy muttered.

“What was that?”

“I said…why can’t I be a Siren?” he finished lamely. “Just because I wasn’t born underwater?”

“It’s not just because you weren’t born underwater,” Agatha said. “It’s also because you were born to humans.”

“So were you!”

“Yes, and…?” Agatha sneered. “I’m not a Siren. Nor have I ever wanted to be one.” That was a lie, but it was also none of Billiam’s business. “Most of us can’t be Sirens, but you know what?”

“What?” Billy huffed.

“You can make a Siren,” Agatha said, wiggling her brows. “Male mermaids are a hot commodity here; you know that. We have so few of them. Mate with a female mermaid and you could have your own little Siren to project all your hopes and dreams onto.”

“Um…” Billy flushed. “I don’t think that’s going to happen, actually, for…reasons.”

“Well, that’s disappointing,” Agatha said. Billy’s chin quivered. “For you,” she clarified. “Love whomever you love, kid. I don’t give a shit, but now you don’t get a Siren child. Maybe a Siren niece or nephew if there’s anybody dumb enough to mate with your dim-witted brother.”

“Are you supposed to talk about your students that way?” Billy asked.

“You gonna tell on me?” Agatha asked, raising an eyebrow at him.

Billy’s shoulders slumped. “No,” he said.

Agatha smiled, triumphantly. “Of course you aren’t,” she said, reaching out to pat his head. He had wild curls that floated around his head like a halo. “Now beat it. You have a week-long break from school. Go and enjoy it! And stop irritating me. You’re the only dweeb I know who would opt for more time in school.”

“I like learning!” Billy protested as Agatha started to poke and prod him out of her way, trying desperately to get him to leave her alone. “My mom said you were the same way!”

“Still am,” Agatha admitted. “But the difference between you and me is that I’m not asking some teacher for answers; I go out and find my own answers.”

“We’re not supposed to leave the Pod,” Billy reminded her like the goody-fucking-two-fins he was.

“It’s frowned upon,” Agatha corrected, “but not a hard and fast rule. I stay far below the surface, anyway. No boats are going to catch sight of me on the ocean floor, which is, coincidentally, where the best human artifacts are found.”

This wasn’t entirely true, but Billy didn’t need to know that.

“Can I come with you?” Billy asked, his big green eyes widening as he locked them on Agatha. “Please?”

“Not a chance,” Agatha huffed, shoving her hand into his face and pushing him away. She used the momentum from that shove to turn herself away from him, swimming quickly in the other direction. “I’ll see you in a week!” she called over her shoulder.

She heard him grumble something in return, but she paid it no mind. She was finally free.

Well, for the next week at least.

It wasn’t that Agatha disliked being a teacher. Not that much, anyway. She found genuine joy in sharing the knowledge that she had spent most of her life amassing with her often-discouraged curiosity, both about the mermaid world and the human world above the surface. She always wanted to learn more, wanted to know more. Her thirst for knowledge made her no better than Billy when it came to irritating her teachers and the other authority figures around her when she was his age.

It irritated her peers, too, which was probably why she had so few friends.

“Agatha!”

Except for at least one.

Agatha’s nose wrinkled as she turned to see Jen swimming straight at her, fast as a barracuda – but ten times as terrifyingly unwanted.

“What do you want, Jen?” she asked, still swimming away from the other woman, but a bit more slowly now.

“To celebrate the week off?” Jen huffed like it was obvious.

“Aren’t you still working?” Agatha asked. “You’re a healer.”

“Yeah, but at least I don’t have to teach this week. The young healers are starting to get on my nerves. They’re always asking questions that any child would know the answer to. Seriously, go ahead and ask Shuri what kind of seaweed is best for wrapping wounds. She’d give a more thoughtful and precise answer than these dolts three times her age.”

“That’s not a fair comparison – Shuri is a literal genius. She’s nearly finished with schooling entirely.”

“Well, Ramonda raised her well is all I’m saying,” Jen huffed. “Better than whomever raised this class of idiots.”

“The woman shaping our young healers, everybody,” Agatha said dryly to nobody in particular as she resumed swimming away, toward the edge of the Pod’s walls.

“Where are you going?” Jen asked, catching up to Agatha quickly then swimming in front of her backwards as if to show off. Jen had always been annoyingly good at swimming, even for a mermaid. Agatha blamed it on her freakishly long and wide tail, which propelled her forward at speeds that defied the properties of water – and physics, frankly.

“Nowhere,” Agatha replied. “Leave me alone.”

“You’re going to get yourself in trouble one of these days,” Jen warned, like a snitch. “If not with the leaders, then with the humans. And which group do you think is the less dangerous of the two?”

“I’ll tell you which group is the less annoying of the two,” Agatha huffed. “Spoiler: it’s the one that doesn’t have you in it.”

“Neither of them do!” Jen called after her.

Agatha ignored her.

“What about our celebration?”

“I’ll be back in a few hours,” Agatha called back. “We’ll celebrate then!”

“I’m holding you to it!” Jen said. Her voice was getting further away now, to Agatha’s relief. She didn’t even glance over her shoulder to make certain, too focused on reaching the end of the Pod and slipping past the guards that kept watch at the perimeter.

Ostensibly, they were there to guard the Pod from any outside threats, like orcas or sharks or other predators that might attempt to infiltrate the walls for a quick and easy snack.

Not that these creatures were really much of a threat to begin with. Agatha had encountered quite a few sharks in her travels outside of the Pod. They showed very little interest in eating or even interacting with her.

Except Scratchy.

Scratchy was a special kind of shark, smaller than average – unless the other sharks that Agatha was used to seeing were in fact larger than most – and way more affectionate, especially after Agatha had removed a large fishing hook from behind his dorsal fin the first time they met. He had growled a little bit and attempted to bite her, but once the hook was completely out and he was on his way to recovery, he instantly took to her, snuggling up to her side. After that, it seemed she couldn’t get rid of him.

She had to teach him to stay away from the Pod because the guards would kill him on sight and use his parts for food and tools. And, as much as Scratchy annoyed the hell out of her in those early days, Agatha didn’t want to see him get hurt. It was bad enough that he was covered in zigzagged scars from head-to-toe, which was where he got his name.

Scratchy emerged from the shadows the second Agatha was safe from the guards’ sight, as if he’d been waiting for her since the last time he saw her (less than 24 hours prior) and she greeted him with a long stroke down his back, careful of his more sensitive scarring. She could swear he purred at her. She pressed a kiss over the hook scar, scratching under his belly.

“How’s the best shark in the sea doing today?” she cooed, patting his nose as he swam around her. She laughed as he nudged at her belly then nosed at her bag, looking up at her with big, black, thoughtless eyes. No brain cells in sight.

Damn, she loved this stupid creature.

Agatha reached into her bag, pulling out her leftovers from dinner the night before – a thick piece of eel meat. She tossed it over his head and watched as Scratchy made a frankly ungraceful arc in the water to reach it before it hit the ocean floor and got all sandy and gritty.

He caught it and immediately tore through it, still happily munching as he caught up to Agatha as she swam away from the Pod. She didn’t quite know where she was headed yet, but she could always find her way back.

She glanced back in the direction of the Pod every so often, looking for the blinking light in the distance that shone from the highest point in the Pod down below. It was visible for miles, but it was still too deep for most human…what did they call them? Scoobies? Tubas?

Whatever they were called when they donned the ugly skins, the humans could not swim deep enough to even catch a glimpse of the Beacon that led back to the Pod. As long as Agatha could still see it, she knew that she was far enough away from the surface that she could also not be perceived by the humans.

This, of course, did not deter the naturally curious and knowledge-thirsty mermaid from getting closer and closer to the surface despite the fact that mermaids were expressly forbidden from leaving the water. Agatha reasoned (in her own head, mind you) that she would of course never break that rule. She had no interest in being spotted by the humans, captured, and experimented on, nor did she want to lead them to the Pod and expose the rest of her kind.

She just wanted to know a little more about the world in which she was born, about the people who abandoned her. She wanted to know more about her birth mother.

The Siren who had saved Agatha from drowning as an infant had disappeared shortly after her arrival. They said that she’d been captured on her next venture to the shore. The elders had even prepared for an imminent discovery from the humans – one that never came. They had mourned her, named the next seaborn in her honor, and celebrated her every year on the anniversary of her disappearance – two days after Agatha’s birthday.

Her name was Lily or something like that. Agatha had never gotten the chance to know her like most landborns knew the Sirens that rescued them from their most gruesome fates. Sirens were treated like aunts or grandmothers, celebrated and loved and showered with affection.

It was yet another thing that Agatha felt she’d missed out on: a mother and a Siren.

Of course, she was raised, like most mermaid children, by two mothers. Mothers that she was sure had loved her in her youth, when she was small and cute and still listened to everything they said. Mothers who became weary with her perpetual questions and the trouble she’d get herself into with her growing curiosity and thirst for more knowledge. She was in trouble constantly at school, and they were blamed for not keeping her in check. She presumed they might have been patient at first, for they never screamed at her when she was small, never gave her the silent treatment or told her that she was more trouble than she was worth.

But that, too, seemed to change as the trouble just kept coming and only got worse as Agatha got older.

It had been five years since the last time Agatha had seen her mothers. The Pod was luckily large enough that they never accidentally ran into each other. Others had stopped asking about them, too. Agatha stopped worrying that she might disappoint them – or anybody else – with her trips outside the Pod. She felt less and less shame the further she roamed with Scratchy at her side, his smooth body rubbing against her side as they swam toward the warmth of the sun, beams of light reaching her through the water. Agatha reached out her hand toward it, imagining how warm the sun would feel on her skin if she could just…

Suddenly, Scratchy was no longer at her side.

She felt the rough scrape of fish scales against her skin. Agatha looked down to see herself surrounded by smaller fish, all of them with their eyes and mouths open in panic as a net surrounded them, lifting them up – along with Agatha – and carrying them toward the surface. Agatha’s heart sank as she attempted to fight past them to escape, but it was already too late: the net surrounded her, too, trapping her inside.

“Scratchy!” she called out. Her faithful friend was already circling the net in obvious distress, lashing out at the net, biting it in an attempt to tear a hole big enough for Agatha to escape through, but whatever material it was made out of was strong enough to hold and just sharp enough to cause him pain. Agatha reached out, shoving his face away before he could add to his ever-growing collection of scars. “Don’t!” she commanded. At the same time, she used her other hand to do what she’d always been taught and reached for the necklace she wore, tugging as hard as she possibly could until she felt the chain break and the necklace go slack in her hand.

Then, everything went dark.
