Chapter 2

Chapter Summary

Rio was just having a normal day at sea....

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Chapter Two

She was floating.

Everything was warm.

She could see the bright light of the sun behind her eyelids.

Everything was peaceful, serene, muted…

 

“Dude, wake up!”

Rio grunted as she tumbled onto the floor of her boat, groaning as she blinked up at the sun, covering her eyes with one callused hand.

“What the fuck?” she grumbled, pushing herself to sit up and glaring at Alice, who had turned the seat to face her, leaning one arm against the steering wheel and smirking. “What’s wrong with you?”

“I’m not the one who fell asleep on the job,” Alice pointed out, “after you bitched me out for sleeping fifteen minutes past my alarm this morning.”

“The phone was literally right next to your head,” Rio said. “You need to get your hearing checked. Fuck me for being a concerned friend, I guess.” She pushed herself to her feet and stretched out sore limbs. As comfortable as the padded captain’s chair was, it wasn’t nearly as comfortable to sleep in as her bed at home.

It had still been mostly dark when Rio started to doze off, but now the sun was beating down on them, making the sweater she was wearing feel suffocating. She peeled it off and tossed it on her chair as she made her way to lower the anchor.

“Eddie,” she called over her shoulder to the teen she’d hired for the summer, “did you mend that net?”

“Aye, Captain!” Eddie retorted with an exaggerated pirate lilt. “That I did!”

Rio rolled her eyes. “Good,” she said. “Let’s see how well it holds up.”

“Hell yeah!” Eddie exclaimed, rushing over to the bow of the boat to retrieve the net he’d spent the last week mending. They’d been stuck using a smaller net since Rio’s knife had sliced through the larger one to free the tail of a pathetic-looking little lemon shark with scars already littering its body.

Rio had always had a soft spot for sharks of all kinds.

 

“Papi! Papi, mira! Sharks!”

“I see them, mi alma. Are you scared?”

“Scared? Why would I be scared? Sharks are cool.”

 

Rio was seven years old the first time her father brought her on one of his fishing trips. It was early June and school had just let out. Rio’s grades for the year had been good enough that her mother had agreed she could accompany her father on his trips once per week all summer so long as she kept her hands and feet inside the confines of the S. S. Esperanza the entire time.

Rio took that about as seriously as any seven-year-old could, of course. Her father was right there to supervise, along with the rest of his crew, and kept a watchful eye on her even as they pulled up full nets of fish and crab and the occasional lobster. He let her hold the fishing rod while he snapped pictures for her mother, capturing her wide, gap-toothed grin when she pulled up a ten-pound tuna. It wasn’t the biggest fish she’d ever catch, of course, but it was still a big deal to the scrawny little kid whose knees wobbled as she struggled to hold it still for the picture.

At one point, her Titi Mimi snapped a picture of her sitting in her father’s lap in his Captain’s chair, dozing against his chest with his sunglasses covering her eyes and a little sunburn on her cheeks from spending all day in the sun. Her father had one hand on the steering wheel and the other holding his baby close to his chest, his lips just brushing the crown of her head.

Rio kept that photo tucked in her wallet at all times.

Eddie handed her the mended net and Rio carefully looked over it, checking for weak areas where her knife had sliced through it just one week prior, but it held well through her tugging.

“Good job, Eduardo,” she said, handing it back.

“Not my name,” Eddie said, “but thanks.”

“Just go set it up. We don’t want to lose too much daylight.”

Eddie nodded and went about replacing the smaller net with his mended one, securing it and making sure nothing was tangled before using the hand crank to start lowering it into the water. Rio had to admit that hiring him to help for the summer had been a good move on her part.

Alice had thought he’d crap out in a week. Rio had won fifty bucks betting on him.

“It’s as far down as it can go, Cap,” he called back to Rio, who gave him a thumb’s up.

They’d check it in a couple of minutes. In the meantime, Rio took a seat and looked out at the water, inhaling the fresh sea air. She’d always loved being out at sea. When her father wasn’t away catching fish to sell, he’d bring her and her mother out for ‘picnics at sea’ watching dolphins swim in the distance and gazing at the clouds as they lay on a blanket on the deck floor. Sometimes, they’d even jump into the water to cool down for a little bit.

Well, Rio and her father would. Her mother, Marisol, wasn’t much of a swimmer. But she took pictures of them in the water and giggled when they’d splash her, kicking her feet to splash them back as she cooled them in the water.

Rio reached for her bag, pulled out her book and a pencil, and started sketching the horizon, adding clouds and seagulls in the sky and sharks in the water. She reached for the tumbler she’d filled with ice water that morning and took a sip. The tumbler had rainbow watercolor sharks painted on it from a ceramic painting studio she’d dragged Alice to for Rio’s last birthday. She’d labelled each individual shark as she painted them, spitting out random facts about sharks that she knew her best friend didn’t give a shit about.

Alice’s own mug had looked like Jackson Pollack personally threw up on it.

“Ready to pull the net up?” Rio blinked up at Alice. “It’s been fifteen minutes.”

“Already?” Rio asked, looking at the watch on her left wrist. “Damn.”

“Time flies when you’re…” Alice looked over the sketchbook in Rio’s hand, “drawing more sharks? Dude, I think you may have an addiction.”

“I can stop whenever I want to,” Rio huffed.

“That’s a thing that addicts say.”

“Shut up,” Rio grumbled, shutting her book and shoving it back into her bag before standing up. “Just come on.”

While the crank on the boat was fine for lowering the net into the ocean, it often failed when it came to lifting it back up again, full of heavy catch, requiring at least three people to turn it and reach for the net to pull it back into the boat.

That was honestly the real reason Rio had hired Eddie in the first place.

He was already waiting by the side of the ship, bouncing on the balls of his feet, way too enthusiastic for the back-breaking labor that was wrangling a bunch of squirming, smelly ocean-dwellers into a cooler.

To each their own.

Rio stood behind him to help with the crank while Alice stood across from them, ready to reach down and pull the net up when needed.

“Ready?” Eddie asked.

“Ready,” Rio and Alice chirped in unison.

Eddie grunted as he started to turn the crank, pulling the net closed and tugging it up toward the surface. His biceps strained as it got harder and harder to turn, even with Rio’s help. Alice reached over the side and tugged at the net as it came into view, pulling it into the boat and opening it to allow all the fish to spill across the tarp she’d laid across the deck floor.

“Not a bad first haul, huh?” Eddie said, grinning as he held onto the crank, locking it in place when he was sure it could withstand the weight of the slowly emptying net. “Just a couple more netfuls and we can probably call it a day, huh?”

“Don’t jinx us, Edmundo,” Alice said, starting to toss the fish into the cooler at the stern of the ship.

“Still not my name,” Eddie sighed.

By noon, they had cast the net five separate times, yielding fewer and fewer fish with each return. It was getting annoyingly easy to reel the net back up. Rio barely had to help with the crank anymore. She took this as a sign that she should actually eat lunch, which she rarely gave herself time to do most days despite Alice’s bitching at her.

She was a few bites into her tuna sandwich when she heard Eddie let out a louder than usual grunt. She glanced over to see him struggling with the crank, putting all his weight into attempting to turn it.

It did not budge.

Rio sighed and put down her sandwich, but Alice stopped her.

“Eat,” she said. “You need it. I’ll help.”

“You’re like 90 pounds, soaking wet,” Rio argued, wrapping her sandwich back up. “How the hell are you gonna move it?”

“Fuck off,” Alice huffed, placing her hands next to Eddie’s on the crank. She let out a grunt as well, and the crank moved a couple inches, to her credit. “I think the net might be stuck on something at the bottom,” she said. “Something heavy.”

“Do you think we caught a shark?” Eddie asked, excitedly, as he kept trying to turn the crank, his biceps flexing with the effort. Rio discarded her sandwich back into the food cooler and walked over to help them.

“I hope not,” Rio said. “Thing’s probably tearing up the net and chomping down our fish as we speak. Poor baby.”

“You’re the only one I know who coos at sharks,” Alice grunted.

“Shut up and push,” Rio said, putting her hands next to theirs as all three began to push the crank. It turned, begrudgingly, whatever it was stuck on giving way a little bit.

“When you can see the net,” Rio said, “lock the crank and Alice—” she looked at the smaller woman, thought better, “—Eddie and I will pull the net into the boat. If there’s a shark, we need to act fast to subdue it and get it back into the water.”

“And try not to get bit,” Eddie added.

“That, too,” Rio said, trying to blink sweat out of her eyes. She didn’t dare attempt to swipe it away lest the crank reverse and throw everything back into the ocean.

She internally rejoiced when she heard the sound of the net surfacing.

She and Eddie reached over the side of the boat and tugged at the fish-filled net, straining to keep a grip on it as they pulled it into the vessel while Alice kept hold on the crank, which, though locked, was still threatening to release.

Finally, the net was on the deck floor and they made quick work of opening it up, taking care that there might be a relatively large, very toothy creature under all the other fish they’d caught – one that could very well take out one of their extremities if they weren’t careful.

But there was no shark.

What lay on the deck floor amongst the floppy, slippery, gasping bodies of tuna, bass, and marlin was no sea creature at all, as far as any of them could tell. It was instead a woman.

A naked woman.

A naked woman with long, dark brown, tangled hair splayed around her body.

A naked woman who was not breathing.

“Oh shit,” Eddie said. “Is she dead?”

Rio got to her knees next to the woman and placed her hand on her throat, feeling for a pulse. The woman’s skin was cold and clammy. She didn’t look dead, though.

Rio started CPR, turning the woman onto her back and placing one hand on top of the other on the center of her chest and began to pump.

“Come on,” she breathed. “Come on…”

“Rio, give it up,” Alice said. “There’s no way she’s – holy shit!”

Alice nearly fell over the edge of the boat before Eddie caught her as the woman on the ground suddenly opened her eyes and began to cough and sputter. Rio helped her sit up and patted her back as she expelled water from her lungs all over the deck.

“Whoa,” Eddie said, eyes wide as he stared down at the woman. “Where the hell did she come from?”

“And how is she still alive?” Alice added, still clinging to the teen’s arm.

Rio ignored them as she continued to pat the woman’s back. She was still basically coughing up her lungs up along with the disgusting sea water.

“Are you okay?” she asked. “Are you hurt?”

The woman turned to her with bleary, ocean blue eyes squinting in the sun. She opened her mouth as if to speak, but before she could get a single word out, her eyes rolled back in her head and she fell against Rio’s body, out cold.

“Is she dead now?” Alice asked after a minute.

Chapter End Notes

damn Alice...that's cold...

Chapter 3

Chapter Summary

Agatha wakes up in a strange place...

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Chapter Three

 

“Where did you find her?”

“About an hour north of the island.”

“What on Earth was she doing out there?!”

“I didn’t ask.”

“Why not?”

“Because she passed out, Mami!”

“Why is she wearing your sweater?”

“Because she wasn’t wearing anything else when we found her.”

“She was naked? Ay, pobrecita.”

Agatha felt something on her forehead, pushing her hair away from her face. Weakly, she swatted at it with her own hand, but her arm felt heavy. She grunted a little and attempted to turn her head away from the annoyance.

“Oh, she’s waking up!” a voice said.

Too loudly. Agatha’s head hurt. There was light behind her eyelids.

“’op ‘espking,” Agatha groaned, then furrowed her brow.

What had she just said?

Agatha cleared her throat, but it felt…strange. In a way she couldn’t articulate. Her throat felt rough, like coral. She coughed.

“Get her some water.”

The next thing she knew, there was something pressing against her lips. Agatha felt something cold and opened her mouth reflexively. Whatever it was felt good as it entered her mouth.

Water, her tired mind supplied.

Agatha attempted to breathe it in, but started sputtering and choking as if she’d swallowed the bony part of a fish or some kind of sea urchin, only…it wasn’t spiky. There wasn’t anything in her throat except for the water. The pain wasn’t even coming from her throat: it was in her chest.

Why did her chest hurt?

She felt hands on her back urging her to rise. She felt heavier than she ever had and had to rely on the hands to support her as she leaned forward, expelling something from deep in her body.

When she finally opened her eyes, there was a…well, she didn’t quite have the word for what it was. It looked like something she’d found in the sand one day and brought back to her class to show them. It was a bright pink color and shaped sort of like a seashell. Inside the seashell was…water? It was clear like water, but Agatha had never seen so little of it. Had that come from her throat?

She glanced up to see the two other beings in the room with her, staring at her, both with concerned expressions. And confusion.

Agatha was also confused.

“Hoer yu?” she asked, her nose wrinkling at the unfamiliar sound that came from her mouth. She cleared her throat and tried again. “Hoo er yew?”

Her mouth felt strangely empty. And…dry? Like sand. But not…sandy? It just didn’t feel proper. Wrong.

“Let’s try that again, huh?” said the older being. She looked like any other mermaid, except her hair, which was tied up in a braid over her shoulder, did not float like Agatha’s did.

Like Agatha’s had, anyway. Agatha’s hair was also strangely stuck to her shoulders and face. She furiously attempted to push it away, but it didn’t go far. She let out a frustrated noise.

“Is she okay?” the younger being asked, out the side of her mouth. She didn’t look away from Agatha, though. Agatha didn’t really mind it. She had pretty eyes. They were brown, which Agatha rarely saw in the Pod.

“Um fin,” Agatha insisted, crossing her arms over her chest. Wait, what was on her chest? And her arms, as well? Agatha held them out, staring at the strange, green fabric that covered them. “Wathis?” she asked, not worrying how her speech sounded because that was honestly not the weirdest thing happening right now.

“That’s a sweater,” the older being said. “It’s meant to keep you warm.” She spoke slowly, like Agatha was an idiot.

Agatha wanted to be indignant about it, but then she realized something.

Oh. Oh. These were humans. These were full-fledged humans.

She looked down, and her eyes widened.

Oh, she was in trouble.

Because not only did they have legs, but Agatha also had legs.

That’s why everything felt strange.

That’s why she had choked on the water.

That’s why felt heavy and couldn’t speak.

She was no longer surrounded by water. She no longer had the gills to breathe it in. Humans could not breathe in water like she could.

Her eyes widened and her hands went to her throat, scratching it, feeling for her necklace, for the stone that had laid on her collarbones for her entire existence. It was stupid, she knew, because obviously if she had legs she was no longer wearing it, but her heart seized in her chest anyway because what the hell? Where did it go?!

“Relax,” the older human said, placing her hand on Agatha’s back. She rubbed between Agatha’s shoulder blades, and Agatha felt her breathing somehow get better. Like magic.

Were humans in possession of magic? She could not recall.

She was a damn teacher, for crying out loud! How could she not remember if humans possessed magic? She was supposed to know these kinds of things! It sure felt like this woman had magic in her touch.

“Everything’s going to be alright, querida,” the woman soothed. “You’ll be okay.” Her hand continued to rub up and down Agatha’s back. “Just breathe. In through your nose…” She paused, looking down at Agatha expectantly until she complied with her instructions. “Good,” she said. “Hold it in your lungs for 1…2…3…4…okay, now slowly breathe out through your mouth.”

Agatha did as she was told, her entire body seeming to relax as the air escaped her lungs. It felt strange but also very natural. She had never breathed air like that before – not that she could remember, anyway. Obviously, she had done it as a newborn, but she’d been brand-new when her Siren saved her and brought her down to the Pod. No more than a few hours old, if that.

The woman led Agatha through two or three more of those long breaths before letting her relax into the…what was this thing called? It was softer than the ground, for sure, but not by much. And it was elevated quite a distance. There was also some kind of material covering her legs.

She had legs.

Agatha still couldn’t believe it.

“Are you admitting her?” the younger human asked.

“I don’t see any reason to,” replied the elder. “She has no serious physical injuries. No broken bones, according to her X-rays. Her CT results came back clear. Her blood pressure and vitals are all normal. There’s no reason not to release her.”

“Except the fact that we have no idea who she is,” the younger one pointed out. “Also, she can’t really talk because she probably has a TBI.”

“Ah kin tah,” Agatha insisted, but her words were still nonsensical.

The older human furrowed her brow. She had the same eyes shape (and everything else really) as the younger, but her eyes were on the greener side. Were they related? Most humans that looked this alike must have been related. Or did all humans look like this?

No. That couldn’t be. Agatha was human-born, and she certainly didn’t look like them.

Still.

“Can you tell us your name?” the older one asked, tilting her head curiously.

“Agt-huh,” Agatha tried. It sounded the most like a real word so far. Agatha was starting to get used to this talking without water shit. She cleared her throat.

The younger human offered her the water again. It was in a strange-looking thing, a weird shade of pink with a smooth texture.

“Swallow this time,” the older one instructed as her offspring(?) placed the water against her lips. “Don’t breathe it in. I’d hate to have to hook you up to an I.V. if you can’t hydrate yourself.”

Agatha had no idea what that meant, but the woman was grimacing as she said it, so it could not have meant anything good.

She opened her lips for the pink thing, allowing the pretty, brown-eyed woman to tip the water into her mouth. Some of the water fell down her chin, but the majority of it slid into her mouth, filling it quickly.

“Swallow,” the older human instructed. “That’s it.”

Agatha felt her throat constrict and the water slid down it like food would, only this was considerably easier to swallow than food. It went down smooth and soothed the scratchiness of her throat almost instantly. Agatha actually found herself shutting her eyes in relief as she felt the cool temperature of the water spread through her chest. She let out a soft sigh as the small structure was pulled away from her lips.

Then she felt something warm on her jaw, and her eyes popped open.

Those pretty brown eyes were so close to hers now. The warmth on her jaw was caused by the younger human’s thumb rubbing away the spilled water. Her eyes were wide, too, as if surprised by her own body’s actions. Her tan cheeks were flushed, and she pulled her hand away after a second as if burned.

Agatha felt herself swallow again, but her throat was empty this time.

“Sorry,” the younger human said, stepping back, her face pink as a starfish.

“Better?” she asked.

Agatha cleared her throat and nodded. It did feel better.

“Can you try telling us your name again?”

Agatha nodded, took another breath, and tried again: “Agthe,” she said. She wrinkled her nose. “Agathhh.” She let out a growl.

“Slowly,” the older human said. “Take your time, querida. Go on.”

Agatha did another one of those long breaths. In through the nose, hold for a few seconds, then out through her mouth.

“Ah-gah-thuh,” she said, enunciating every syllable.

“Agatha?” the younger one asked.

Agatha nodded, smiling in relief. “Yah,” she said. “Agtha!”

She groaned again.

“That’s alright,” the younger one said, chuckling slightly. “We’ll work on it. My name is Rio. Rio Vidal. This is my mother, Dr. Marisol Vidal.”

Doctor. So the older human was a healer, like Jen. That was something Agatha could recognize, at least.

“Can you tell us your last name?” Rio Vidal asked. “Or try to?”

Last name?

Agatha wrinkled her nose and shook her head. She had no names prior to Agatha, as far as she knew. Nor did she, like Rio Vidal and Dr. Marisol Vidal, have a second name. Why did they have the same second name?

Was this a human thing then?

“You can’t remember?” Rio Vidal asked, frowning. “Shit, that sucks. We’ll figure it out.” She cleared her throat. “Can you at least tell us where you’re from?”

Agatha’s eyes widened and she shook her head, frantically.

As a child, it had been drilled into hers and all the other young mermaids’ heads that, if they were ever to go to the surface, the humans would not believe them if they told them where they were from because, to humans, the mermaids were mythological beings that they told stories about to children.

Humans, they said, did not believe in anything they could not see with their own eyes. Still, contact with them was forbidden.

Agatha was breaking a lot of rules right now, but Rio Vidal’s pretty brown eyes were making it feel a little bit like it was all worth it.

“You don’t know or you can’t tell us?” Rio Vidal asked, squinting her eyes at Agatha. She looked at her mother. “Do you think she’s from Cuba or something?”

“She doesn’t look Cuban to me, mi alma,” the older one – Dr. Marisol Vidal said. “Are you Cuban, querida? You can tell us. We can help you.”

Agatha had no idea what a Cuba was. But it was not the name of the Pod, so she shook her head.

“Do you know where you are now?” Rio Vidal asked.

“No,” Agatha said. It was the first word she’d said correctly. She sighed in relief.

“You’re in Isla Sirena,” Rio Vidal informed her.

Isla Sirena. Ironic.

What was she doing here? She reached again for her necklace, but remembered that she was no longer wearing it. She must have lost it at the bottom of the ocean. She vaguely remembered tearing it off when she was surrounded by that net and the fish. Her fingers scratched idly at her throat, her fingers brushing smooth skin where there once were gills.

Then there was a sound. One that startled Agatha, who glanced all around, frowning.

“Don’t worry,” Dr. Marisol Vidal said, walking to a large rectangle on one of the oddly smooth, flat walls. She twisted a piece of rounded metal on the rectangle and pulled it away from the rest of the wall, to Agatha’s shock. Then she stood blocking the slim opening it had created.

A door, Agatha realized. It was a door. It looked different from doors in the Pod. It was so flat and made out of different material than the coral that their doors were carved out of.

Dr. Marisol Vidal said something in a low voice to whomever was on the other side of the door before she turned back to Agatha.

“Agatha,” she said, “there is somebody here I believe can help you. Her name is Lilia Calderu, and she’s a social worker. She can give you a place to stay while you heal if that’s alright with you.”

“Aunt Lilia’s here?” Rio Vidal asked, smiling a bit. There was a tiny gap between her two front teeth that softened something inside of Agatha and made her throat go dry again.

“Yes, I am,” a musical voice said from the other side of the door. “May I come in?”

“That’s up to you, Agatha,” Dr. Marisol Vidal said, turning to her with a kind smile. “Are you alright with allowing Lilia to help you?”

Agatha swallowed thickly, wondering how many more humans she would have to allow to see her before she was finally able to make her escape back to the water.

She wondered if that would even be possible without her necklace. How far down would she have to swim, as a human, to find it? Would it even be possible, or was she stuck here, on land, forever? Would her students hear about what happened to her? Would she be a cautionary tale for them?

Or would she just be another mermaid gone missing?

It had happened before. Mermaids went missing rarely, but Agatha had heard of a few who just…never came back. Rumors circulated about sharks outside the Pod catching them for dinner, but Agatha no longer believed that was the truth. Maybe they, too, had lost their necklaces and got caught up on land, unable to return.

Now Agatha would befall the same fate. She could imagine the rumors that would circulate about her disappearance.

Too curious for her own good. It was only a matter of time before she got in over her head. This was always destined to happen to her. Her poor mothers.

Agatha doubted her mothers would even miss her. They hadn’t missed her in years, anyway.

Well, Agatha supposed, if there was no hope in her getting back to the Pod, anyway, why not allow more humans to see her? Maybe this Lilia could be of more help to her. Maybe she could even teach her how to speak properly.

She nodded to Dr. Marisol Vidal, who nodded back and opened the door wider, allowing the third human to enter the room.

And this human…Poseidon.

Aunt Lilia was the most colorful of the three humans.

Her hair was a lighter brown than Dr. Marisol Vidal’s or Rio Vidal’s, but it was also streaked with different shades of gray and was tied up and messy, curls springing out in every direction you could think of. She also had some kind of weird thing on her face, made of wire and glass. It magnified her amber eyes to three times their normal size. The material on her body is what really made her stand out, however. She had a long, tube-like stretch of fabric that stretched from her shoulders to around her…feet? That’s what they’re called, Agatha was certain. The fabric was covered in a rainbow of different colors, a couple that Agatha could swear she’d never seen before. And it was cinched at the waist, right around where her tail would have started if she had one. The material didn’t fall around her arms like Agatha’s sweater did, though. Instead, her arms were left bare and a little bit browner toward her hands and pinker around her shoulders.

Her feet were not bare; they were encased in a strange material that Agatha had certainly never seen before. There were pieces of them missing, held to her feet only by slim straps of the same strange material. They were practically molded to her feet, too, like they had been made specifically for her – which was quite common, actually, in the Pod, as well.

Mermaids didn’t wear clothes like the humans did. They had no real need to. They didn’t have to protect themselves from the sun or the sand. The water suited them just fine. No other fish wore clothing, so why should they? But there were some mermaids who tried to emulate the humans by making replicates of the clothes they wore on land, draping their torsos in fabric that sooner or later fell apart completely because they were not made to last long underwater.

It became part of fashion, though, like the way some of the younger mermaids decorated their hair with seashells and tied it with bits of rope and netting that fell from boats.

Agatha had done the same as a child. She still collected smaller pieces of twine to tie her hair when it wouldn’t stop floating in front of her face while she was teaching, and her own collection bag was made from the rough material she’d assumed fell from some fishing boat as well.

Wait.

Where was her bag?

She’d had it before she got caught, hadn’t she?

“Mah bug,” Agatha said, looking at Dr. Marisol Vidal before Lilia could even greet her. “Wehsh mah bug?”

“Bug?” asked the healer. “What bug?”

“No’ bug,” Agatha said in frustration. “Buuuuug.”

Shit. That didn’t sound different at all.

Agatha motioned for the water that was now sat on some flat surface. It seemed a bit odd to her that it did not move without being strapped down by something. Most of her items in her home had to be strapped down unless they were too heavy to float. Even then, they tended to shift.

This water in its pink container seemed content to stay perfectly still.

Rio Vidal followed Agatha’s pointed finger and retrieved the water for her, bringing it to Agatha’s lips, but Agatha grabbed the container from her hand and attempted to take a sip unaided.

The key word here is ‘attempted’ because try as she might to bring the cup to her lips at an acceptable speed and open her mouth normally to accept the liquid inside…well, that’s not exactly what happened.

Instead, all Agatha managed to do was splash water directly in her face. All of it. Every last drop. And the container even slipped from her fingers to add insult to injury – as well as another injury as the cup hit her right between the eyes, leaving Agatha (and everybody else in the room) completely stunned.

Agatha had never heard silence quite like this, her cheeks getting hot as the three humans stared at her, blinking in shock. The new woman, Aunt Lilia, had her lips pressed together like she was attempting not to laugh in Agatha’s face, which was just rude in her opinion.

“Oh dear,” Aunt Lilia said, her voice a bit choked up as she played with a few necklaces wrapped around her neck, all with differently shaped beads and stones. “Are you alright?” There was water at the corners of her eyes, which was odd. And she was biting the insides of her cheeks. Her nostrils were flaring.

Dr. Marisol Vidal nudged Aunt Lilia’s shoulder. She nudged back and put up a hand as she cleared her throat and continued to stare at Agatha.

“Wawa?” Agatha requested in a pathetic voice. Her voice felt scratchy again, but not because she was thirsty.

She felt like she was about to cry.

Rio Vidal plucked the pink container from the ground and brought it to the raised flat surface, a part that was shiny and smoothly carved into the surface. She twisted something round and a stream of water came out of a curved tube, falling directly into the container that Rio Vidal held underneath it. Agatha’s eyes popped wide open as she stared at it, her mouth gaping slightly.

When Rio Vidal turned back to her, holding out the container of water, she paused. “What?” she asked. “You wanted water, right?”

Agatha snapped out of it and nodded, holding out her hand.

Rio Vidal placed the container into her hand with a small, polite smile. “Slowly this time,” she said, gently. Her voice sent shivers down Agatha’s spine.

Agatha nodded and brought the container to her lips much more slowly than before, tipping the container slowly until she could feel the liquid hit her skin. She opened her lips like she had before and took a slow sip of the water, sighing in relief before she tipped the container back down.

“Better?” Dr. Marisol Vidal asked.

“Betra,” Agatha agreed.

“Well, she can learn new skills,” Aunt Lilia said. “That’s progress.”

“New skill?” Rio Vidal scoffed. “Hardly. She just drank some water. She definitely already knew how to do that.”

Wrong, Agatha thought, but she didn’t try to say it.

“TBI’s can be like a factory reset,” Aunt Lilia said. “Or so I’ve heard. I’m not a doctor, but this looks like it may be new for her damaged brain.”

Agatha frowned at her. Her brain was not damaged. She was just raised differently. Of course, they didn’t know that.

Still.

Very rude.

“Agatha, dear,” Dr. Marisol Vidal said, “this is Lilia Calderu, the social worker.”

“Retired social worker,” Lilia Calderu corrected, stepping past them and extending her hand toward Agatha, who pressed herself back as far from the colorful intruder as possible. To her credit, Lilia Calderu didn’t even let it faze her. Instead, she waved her hand, palm out toward Agatha, and gave her a toothy grin. “Nice to meet you, doll. I love the name Agatha. Did you know it means ‘good’ in Greek? Fun fact!”

“Aunt Lilia has a lot of fun facts,” Rio Vidal said, wrapping her arm around the much older woman’s waist. Lilia Calderu mirrored her action and pressed a kiss to Rio Vidal’s cheek.

“That’s because you always had some to share,” she said, “so I had to find some way to catch up!” She gave Rio Vidal’s other cheek a kiss, looking up at her with an even wider smile. “How have you been, my dear? How’s the fishing business?”

Rio Vidal shrugged her shoulders. “Can’t complain. Did you see my latest catch?” She glanced at Agatha, her cheeks turning pink as Dr. Marisol Vidal swatted her on the back of the head. “Sorry,” she said. “Bad joke.”

Agatha thought it was kind of funny, actually, but she was still confused by this third human’s appearance.

What the hell was a social worker, anyway?

“So,” Lilia Calderu/Aunt Lilia said, turning back to Agatha and clapping her hands, “Dr. Vidal tells me you may be in need of someplace to stay while you recover? Is that true?”

Agatha glanced at Dr. Marisol Vidal. Was that the same person as Dr. Vidal? Was the Marisol not actually important? It didn’t really matter, she thought, if she couldn’t get her mouth to form the words correctly.

Agatha nodded.

“Well then,” Lilia Calderu said, “you’re in luck! I know exactly where to bring you! Provided she’s ready to be discharged?” She looked back at Dr. Vidal.

“We’ve done all the usual tests,” Dr. Vidal replied. “They’ve all come back normal so far, including the blood tests and CT. We’ll have her come in soon for an MRI though, if that’s alright?” She looked between Agatha and Lilia for this.

Agatha had no clue what an ‘MRI’ was but it must have been important? Just based off of Rio Vidal’s grim expression, Agatha assumed it was somewhat dangerous. She frowned, looking to Lilia, whose gaze was steady on her now. She gave Agatha a subtle nod, never breaking eye contact.

Agatha nodded to Dr. Vidal.

“Okay,” the doctor said, walking over to some kind of…rock? Was it a rock? It was thin and rectangular and there was something clicky underneath it that Dr. Vidal tapped on really quickly. The rock emitted a glow, like magic.

(Okay, so humans did possess magic then?)

“I’ll print out her discharge papers and get her a wheelchair,” Dr. Vidal said. “And she should be all set to go if you have something she can wear out of here.”

That’s when Agatha noticed the bag hanging from Lilia Calderu’s shoulder. It was a sandy color, with strange shapes and colors all over it. They looked like some kind of fuzzy creatures, with big eyes and triangular noses. Lilia reached into the bag and pulled something out, unfolding it to hold out to Agatha.

“I hope you don’t mind dresses,” she said, “or loud patterns on dresses. This is all I’ve really got at home, but we can go shopping for more once you’re settled in.”

Loud patterns? Agatha didn’t hear anything.

She stared at the long piece of fabric. It was tubular like the one Lilia Calderu was wearing. Like Lilia Calderu’s, it was very colorful, with zig-zagged lines of reds and blues and yellows and greens all over it. Agatha wondered if she’d really have to wear that instead of the sweater that she was currently wearing.

She frowned at the ‘dresses’ and hugged her sweater, looking at both Dr. Vidal and Rio Vidal as if asking for help.

“Actually,” Rio Vidal said, catching on more quickly, “I think we could probably spare a pair of scrub pants if she’d prefer those. And she can keep the sweater for now. I have plenty more where that came from.” She offered Agatha an understanding smile.

“Would you like that, Agatha?” Dr. Vidal asked. “Scrub pants?”

Agatha had no idea what that meant, but she figured it was better than the fabric tube, so she nodded.

“I’ll go grab those for you,” Dr. Vidal said, “along with the discharge papers. Be right back.” She slipped out through the door, leaving Agatha alone with just Rio Vidal and Lilia Calderu.

“I hope you’re feeling better,” Rio Vidal said, “after this morning. I know this has to be very confusing for you.” She scratched the back of her neck and gave Agatha an awkward smile. “I hope you can start to get your memory and speech back, as well. I’m a physical therapist, so I can definitely help you with that if you need me to.”

Agatha did not know what that was, either, but if it meant spending more time with the pretty, brown-eyed human woman then…well, perhaps she did need help from a physical therapist.

Whatever that was.

She nodded enthusiastically, and Rio Vidal grinned. “Okay,” she said. “Well, I work mostly in the afternoons during the summer, so, um, Aunt Lilia can probably help you call my supervisor and set up an appointment. I have a couple of clients I need to run to meet right now, but I’m free tomorrow afternoon, around three.” She looked directly at Lilia Calderu as she said this. “I’ll let Sarah know to expect your call, alright?”

“You got it, kid,” Lilia Calderu said, leaning forward to give her another kiss on her cheek. “Now go on. I’ve got it from here.”

“Okay,” Rio Vidal said, shooting Agatha one more smile before making her way toward the door. “See you tomorrow, Agatha. Bye, Aunt Lilia.”

“Seeya later, kiddo,” Lilia Calderu said. “Don’t be late!”

Rio Vidal laughed as she disappeared, pulling the door closed behind her.

As soon as she was gone, Lilia Calderu turned back to Agatha.

“Okay,” she said, her voice lowered. “Now we need to figure out how to get you back to the Pod.”

Chapter End Notes

😳

Chapter 4

Chapter Summary

Lilia POV chapter yay!

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Chapter Four

Lilia Calderu had not always been her name.

Well, Lilia had been her name. The last name was relatively new. They didn’t have surnames in the Pod, for whatever reason. Maybe it was because each first name was unique enough on its own. There had been no other Lilias when she was a child.

There had been no Agathas there, either, which was why she’d chosen the name.

Agatha. Her last baby. Her last rescue.

A tiny girl she had named specifically to rebel against the heartless woman who had abandoned her.

 

“Welcome back, Lilia! Who do you have this time?”

“This…is Agatha.”

“Agatha? A lovely name.”

“Yes, I certainly think so.”

 

The name Agatha meant ‘good’; she’d hoped it would be a fitting name.

Agatha was a grown woman now. She had bright blue eyes and wild, dark brown hair, and her head was on a swivel as Lilia walked her the short distance from the tiny island hospital to her house at the end of the block, her eyes drinking in everything with childlike curiosity.

Lilia couldn’t really blame her, though: while Agatha had been born on land, she’d never gotten the chance to really appreciate the beauty of it. She’d never seen the sunrise or the sunset without the filter of the ocean above her head. She’d never gotten to smell the scents of the food from the marketplace, or watch as small children ran by, giggling as they chased each other. She never saw the birds flying high above her or watched the clouds slowly pass overhead. Everything was so new to her…

By the time they made it to Lilia’s house, Agatha had seen more on the short walk there than she’d probably seen in her entire lifetime under the sea. Her big blue eyes were wide with wonder as Lilia walked around her to unlock and shove open her front door before pushing Agatha’s chair inside.

“Weya we?” Agatha asked, looking around the sitting room.

“We’re at my home,” Lilia answered, walking through to the kitchen. “Would you like a cup of tea?”

“Wassat?” Agatha let out a growl. “Wa kin ah tah?”

“Because you’re still making room for the water,” Lilia said, filling up the electric kettle and placing it on its base. “Stop making room for the water.”

“Wud dah tha meen?” Agatha asked.

“Exactly what I said,” Lilia retorted, coming back into the living room. “When you were living underwater, you had to deal with the water moving through your mouth whenever it opened. It made you adapt to speaking with it in mind. Now that you don’t have that water constantly moving in and out of your mouth and through your gills, it’s throwing off your speech.” She came to stand in front of Agatha. “Look at my mouth,” she said. “Watch how my lips move.” She moved them slowly. “See how relaxed my jaw is when I speak? Relax your jaw a bit. Relax your whole face, in fact.”

Agatha took a deep breath, which Lilia was surprised to see came so easily for the younger woman. She closed her eyes and stretched her neck to the right and then to the left.

“Hah do yow—ugh!” Agatha clenched her fists as Lilia let out a soft chuckle.

“It’ll take some getting used to,” she said, “but you’ll get there, doll. Just keep practicing. I can understand you, either way.”

“Bah hah?” Agatha asked. “Hah do yaw—ahem, yoo undew stahn meh?”

“Ooh, that’s almost coherent,” Lilia said with a little clap. “Very good!” She retreated back into the kitchen and pulled out a tin of butter cookies. She opened it and sighed.

Sewing supplies.

She replaced the lid and put the cookies back, reaching for another.

Cookies.

She brought them back to the sitting room, placing them on her coffee table in front of Agatha.

“Hungry?” she asked.

“Answah mih kwestchan,” Agatha demanded. She took another deep breath, a bead of sweat rolling down her temple. “Hah do yew—”

“Because I’m a Siren,” Lilia said, lacking the patience to listen to her continue to struggle her way through a sentence. “Now have a cookie.”

She handed a round one to Agatha, who glared at the pale disc for a moment before snatching it out of her hand.

“Don’t choke,” Lilia warned. “Chew it like you would anything else. Then swallow it like you would any bit of seaweed or fish. Don’t breathe it in.”

“M’nah uh chiyal,” Agatha huffed. She took a tentative bite of the cookie and gasped. “Suh gew.” She shoved the whole thing in her mouth and chewed. “Suh suhft!”

“Well, it’s certainly not as tough as raw eel, I guess,” Lilia laughed. “And it melts in your mouth, huh?”

“Wassat? Mel?” Agatha reached for another one and Lilia pushed the tin closer to her. She was probably starving, the poor thing. And Lilia could always use more storage for her sewing supplies.

“Melt,” she said. “Like dissolve? It kind of breaks down in your mouth. It’s made of shortbread, which is almost entirely butter and sugar. It’s very good,” she said when Agatha opened her mouth to ask another question, “trust me.”

Agatha nodded and shoved another cookie into her mouth. She let out a sigh as she chewed happily on it and swallowed. Then she reached for another.

“Don’t go too quickly,” Lilia warned, “or you’ll get—”

“Hic!” Agatha squeaked, her eyes widening in alarm.

“—hiccups,” Lilia said. “Don’t worry,” she said. “Those are harmless. I’ll get you some water.”

Agatha continued to hiccup as Lilia retrieved a bottle of water from the fridge, unscrewing the top and bringing it out to her. Agatha stared at it for a second while she continued to squeak. She glanced up at Lilia.

“Put your mouth over the hole there,” Lilia said, pointing. “Then tip it back, carefully, and swallow the water. It helps if you also pinch your nose, like this.” She demonstrated. “Go on.”

Agatha did as she was told, tipping the water bottle into her mouth and leaning her head back to let the water spill into her mouth and down her throat. She swallowed a few times before she couldn’t take the lack of oxygen anymore and reflexively took a breath…

While the water was still spilling down her throat.

Like before, she started to sputter, the water bottle slipping from her fingers as she struggled to breathe.

Lilia plucked the bottle from the ground before too much could spill out and placed it on the table before going to Agatha’s aid. She urged her to lean forward while she patted her on the back as Agatha coughed.

“It’s alright,” she said. “You’re fine. You’re okay.”

“Whey—” Agatha coughed. “Wha—” She sputtered.

“You don’t have gills anymore,” Lilia said. “No more breathing water. Just oxygen for now, until I can make you another necklace.”

Agatha’s eyes widened as her head snapped up at that.

“Whuh?!” she gasped. “Yew hah—”

“Stones?” Lilia said. “Yes, I have them. They’re not too difficult to come by, actually. And I know a spell to make you a new necklace…if that’s what you want.”

“Yes!” Agatha gasped. “Ah do!”

“Well, look at that,” Lilia chuckled. “You’re getting better at this speaking thing after all.”

Agatha gave her a big, genuine smile. It was probably the first since Lilia had met her – probably the first since she landed on Rio’s fishing boat.

“It’s going to take some time, though,” Lilia warned. “Magic needs time to marinate, you know.”

Agatha furrowed her brow at that. “Whassat?”

“It means that it’s going to take at least a week before I can get you out of here, so we better make the most out of this visit while we can.”

The tea kettle began to sing, and Lilia hustled back into the kitchen to turn it off and pull out two mugs. She filled both with the hot water and reached for her tea drawer, pulling out some green tea bags.

“Have you ever had tea?” she asked Agatha, ripping the bags from their packaging and steeping them each in the mugs. “It’s lovely. Probably the best thing on this side of the water – besides music. They have such wonderful music on the land.”

She brought both mugs into the living room and placed them on the coffee table. Agatha just stared at them, tilting her head to the side as she watched the steam rise and disappear. She glanced up at Lilia, questioningly.

“It’s very hot,” Lilia warned. “So be careful. Let it cool first. But not too much. There’s nothing worse than lukewarm tea.”

She took a seat on the sofa, leaving Agatha in her chair. The younger woman squirmed a little, looking around the room at all the colorful arts and décor. Lilia favored mismatched furniture and artwork. Her rug was a faded robin’s egg blue with a sort of checkered, monochrome pattern on it. Her couch was mustard yellow with a green blanket she’d crocheted herself. Her end tables were both made of wood but completely different shades and colors. She had two easy chairs that were hot pink and pale yellow, respectively. The walls had floral wallpaper decorating them, but they weren’t lined up quite right, not that Lilia cared. She also had shelves of books and knickknacks all over the room.

She saw Agatha’s fingers flex as she stared at them, as if she wanted to get up and explore everything – but she couldn’t.

Agatha had attempted to get herself from the clinic’s cot to her wheelchair, but she’d been no sturdier on her feet than a newborn fawn. Lilia had taken most of her weight as she’d helped her into the chair and wheeled her out of the room.

After helping her into the scrub pants that Marisol had brought back for her, of course. Pants that Agatha looked at occasionally with a frown, as if she wanted to tear them right off of her own legs, but she resisted, to Lilia’s relief.

Before long, Agatha finally allowed herself to reach out for the mug of tea.

“Take it by the handle,” Lilia said. “The curved part sticking out.”

Agatha nodded, following her instruction. Her fingers wrapped around the handle of the hot mug, and she pulled away for a second, her eyes widening again.

“Hurts!” she said to Lilia.

“Hey, that was an actual word!” Lilia said. “Good job, doll!” Agatha preened a bit at the praise. “And yes, heat can hurt sometimes. You just have to be careful. Do it slowly.”

Agatha reached for the mug again, curling her fingers carefully around the handle. Lilia watched as she grimaced slightly, but did not let go of the mug, instead bringing it to her lips.

“Blow on it,” Lilia said.

Agatha wrinkled her nose in confusion. “Bow?”

Lilia made an ‘O’ with her lips and blew out some air to demonstrate. “Blow,” she said. “It will cool the tea down a bit.”

Agatha nodded and did the same as Lilia, blowing on the surface of the tea before tentatively placing her lips over the warmed rim. She slowly tilted the cup and opened her lips a little bit wider to allow the liquid into her mouth. Lilia watched as her brow furrowed and her throat worked, swallowing the warm liquid.

After a long sip, Agatha pulled the mug away from her mouth and wrinkled her nose, her lips puckering slightly. She touched her throat.

“Wam,” she said.

“Warm?” Lilia asked. “I told you it would be.”

“Bite,” Agatha said.

“Bitter? Would you like some sugar?”

“Shoogurr?” Agatha asked, wrinkling her nose further.

Lilia had a flash of memory from the night she first plucked Agatha from the tide. Her little face, her nose wrinkling in displeasure as she cried…her heart swelled with affection.

“Why ah yew smylin’?” Agatha asked. She wiped her mouth as if there was something there that Lilia may have been laughing at.

“You’re getting very good at this speaking on land thing,” Lilia said. “Your tongue and jaw are getting used to the empty space in your mouth. “Keep it up.” She gave Agatha another smile as she plucked her mug from the coffee table and took it into the kitchen for some sugar.

“Why ah yew sah weed?” Agatha called after her.

Lilia just snickered as she dropped two spoonfuls of sugar into the mug, stirring it into the cooling water before topping it off with a bit of the hot water left in the kettle to help it dissolve. She steeped the tea bag a little bit more before taking it out and tossing it into the trash.

When she brought it back into the living room, she found Agatha shoving two more cookies into her mouth. When she saw Lilia walk in, her shoulders hunched and her cheeks turned pink as if she were embarrassed at being caught like a naughty child stealing sweets.

“Have as many as you want,” Lilia said again. “You must be starving.” She handed Agatha back the mug.

Agatha plucked it back up, grimacing again at the renewed heat.

“Blow on it again,” Lilia advised.

Agatha did as told before she took another sip.

“Hmm,” she said. No wrinkled nose.

“Good?” Lilia asked.

Agatha nodded. She took another sip.

“That is green tea,” Lilia said. “Can you try to say it?”

Agatha cleared her throat and straightened her back.

“Geen tee,” she said, then sighed. “Gween tea.”

“R’s are hard,” Lilia said, “but you’ll get it.”

Agatha nodded with another sigh and took another long sip of her tea. She hummed happily and placed the mug back on the coffee table. Then she looked back up at Lilia.

“How…do…yewww…kn-know…me?” she asked, speaking very slowly and carefully, measuring her words.

“Look at that!” Lilia said, clapping happily. “That was a full coherent sentence!”

Agatha didn’t smile. Instead, one of her eyebrows rose, unamused.

“Tell me.”

Oh, she was actually improving in her speech.

Lilia took a deep breath and turned Agatha’s chair to face the pale-yellow easy chair so that they could face each other as she took a seat. Agatha reached for another cookie as she waited for Lilia to speak.

She didn’t have to wait long.

“About thirty years ago,” Lilia said, “I went on what turned out to be my last sojourn to the shore. I picked up one last child – a girl. As was typical back then. And still, I imagine.”

Agatha nodded, her eyes a little wider as she said nothing, simply listening as she nibbled at another shortbread cookie.

“I went on sojourns for hundreds of years,” Lilia said. “I’ve rescued thousands of infants from gruesome fates, from parents who did not want them. I put my own life on hold to rescue these children, to give them to parents who had the time to care for them, to love them as their own, to raise them in the water. But I knew, from the moment I prepared that night, that that was going to be my last sojourn.”

“Why?”

Agatha’s question was so clear now, her speech having drastically improved over the last hour since Lilia first laid eyes on her after thirty years in that hospital room. She wanted to comment on it, but there was a clear desperation in Agatha’s eyes to know. She needed to know. She had a hunger for knowledge, a curiosity that could not be sated.

That much was abundantly clear.

Lilia paused for a long moment, anyway, avoiding her gaze as she thought of her answer.

“It was just time,” she said. “Time to stop and settle down. Hundreds of years, remember? I was just…tired, and there were plenty of Sirens to take up the mantle.”

She looked Agatha in the eyes then.

“You were my last baby, Agatha,” she said. “You were the last infant I rescued from that shore. The moment I saw you, I knew you were supposed to be my child. I even named you before I got back to the Pod. I rarely named a child. After all, who could keep track of all the names in the Pod?” She chuckled humorlessly. “But I knew the perfect name for you. The perfect name for my child.” She reached out, cautiously, and cupped Agatha’s cheek.

To her shock, Agatha leaned into the touch. Lilia smiled at her.

“Seeing you now,” Lilia said, “and the woman you’ve become – curious, a bit rebellious, and determined – well, I wish I had stayed in the Pod and followed through with my original plans.”

“Why din’d you?” Agatha asked, pulling away out of Lilia’s touch.

Lilia frowned, her shoulders drooping.

“It’s complicated,” she said. “I…I couldn’t stay.”

“Buh why?” Agatha asked, tears started to fall from her eyes, which shocked her as she reached up to feel her face. She frowned at the water she found on her hand. “Wus dis?” she asked, looking back at Lilia.

“Tears,” Lilia informed her. “They happen when you cry, when you’re sad.”

Agatha frowned deeply. “No’ sad,” she insisted. “Jus’ confew.”

Lilia nodded in understanding as if she totally believed her.

“There was a mermaid,” she said, “when I returned to the Pod. She was…in trouble. I needed to help her. It meant leaving the Pod for good. I had to make a choice between my own desires to raise a child and helping her.”

It wasn’t the whole story, but Agatha wasn’t quite ready for that yet.

“You choth ‘er?” Agatha asked, another tear slipping down her cheek. She swiped it away, furiously, looking down at her lap. “O’er me?”

Lilia reached out again, tilting Agatha’s chin up so that their eyes met. “Not over you, dear,” she said. “I chose her over me. Over my own selfishness. I knew you were going to be okay. You would be adopted and live a safe and happy life in the Pod. But this girl…she needed to escape.” She swallowed thickly at the disbelief and anger in Agatha’s eyes. “I wish it could have been different.”

Agatha turned away from her. “M’tired,” she said. “Nee’ slee’.”

Lilia nodded. “I imagine you’ve had a long day,” she said. “I’ll take you to your room.” She reached for the cookie tin and placed it in Agatha’s lap. “Take these in case you’re still hungry,” she said. “I’ll bring you some water to drink.”

“More tea?” Agatha asked. “Wit’ shoogurr?”

Lilia smiled to herself. “Of course,” she said. “Anything you’d like.”

Before she could stop herself, she planted a kiss on the crown of Agatha’s head. If Agatha noticed, she said nothing about it.

Chapter End Notes

Poor Agatha struggling to communicate 😔

Chapter 5

Chapter Summary

Agatha starting to learn more about the human world...

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Chapter Five

Humans were strange creatures, Agatha decided.

For one thing, they slept on elevated surfaces. These elevated surfaces were soft and warm typically, yes, but why were they so high off the ground? Agatha’s bed back in the Pod was made of kelp and seaweed, like most of the beds in the Pod. It was comfortable enough, and the kelp wrapped her like a cocoon while she slept, keeping her relatively warm.

But it was nothing like the blankets that Lilia tucked her into that night.

Agatha had never felt such comfort before in her life. They were soft to the touch, almost as comforting as the sweater that she still wore from Rio Vidal. The scrub pants that she wore were exchanged for something Lilia called ‘leggings’ which clung to her legs like a second skin. They were soft, too.

Everything was so soft. It almost made up for the elevated sleeping surface.

Almost.

Agatha had peeked over the side of the bed when Lilia first helped her onto it (Lilia taking most of her weight since Agatha’s legs still would not fully cooperate) and looked up at Lilia with a question in her eyes that her tongue could not quite articulate.

“This is just how humans sleep,” Lilia had said. “In thirty years, I have heard many different reasons for why that is, but I’ve just learned to accept it.” She shrugged. “You’re not too far off the ground, though. A fall from this height would barely cause a bruise, except maybe to your ego. You’ll be fine. I’m just across the hall; call for me if you need the bathroom again.”

Ah, yes, the bathroom. Another strange human custom.

Why they needed a whole room just to relieve themselves was beyond Agatha, but Lilia had assured her that it was frowned upon to do it anywhere else. Including in their beds.

No matter how strange these things were to her – and the glowing structure next to her bed that filled the room she was in with light – Agatha fell asleep quite easily that night.

The soft rocks – pillows, Lilia had called them – cradled her head in such a way that Agatha felt like a small child again, held in her mothers’ arms as they rocked her to sleep, singing soft lullabies in her ear.

Her dreams were…strange that night.

She dreamt that she was back in the Pod, back in her classroom. She dreamt that her students were all in front of her, all raising their hands – which had never happened. She was delighted by their curiosity, by their hunger for knowledge.

She called on America, a student with a big smile and even bigger personality. She couldn’t even remember the question she had asked, but she knew whatever answer that came from America’s excited expression would be—

Gibberish. It was Gibberish.

Agatha cleaned out her ear with a single finger, frowning.

“Say that again?” she asked.

America happily repeated her answer…but it was entirely nonsensical. It was lacking vowels and the consonants all ran together like mush.

Agatha frowned, shaking her head.

She called on another student – Kamala, another lovely student who maybe spent too much time doodling while she was in class. She rarely raised her hand to answer questions, but this time she was practically floating out of her seat, her burgundy tail swishing around in her excitement.

“Kamala, go ahead,” Agatha said, a bit nervously.

Kamala opened her mouth and all that came out was a jumbled mess of sound. By the look on her face, however, she was quite obviously pleased with it.

“I’m sorry,” Agatha said. “What?”

Kamala repeated her mush.

Agatha’s heart was beginning to pound against her chest. Her head was on a swivel as she glanced around the room at her students, who were all staring at her in obvious confusion.

A third student raised his hand but didn’t wait to be called on. It was Tommy. He asked something in that infuriatingly deep and slow way he tended to speak.

It was incomprehensible – more so than usual, anyway.

“What’s happening?” Agatha asked. “Why can’t any of you speak clearly? What’s going on?”

Then she noticed that the room around her seemed…different.

It was still her same classroom. Still the same four walls and the doorway that led out to the other classrooms, where more students of different age groups were being taught different skills than the ones she was capable of teaching…or it should have been. The doorway was usually lit to make it easier for the students to find their way around.

Today, it was just…black.

And the room itself was…off.

Agatha tried to swim toward the door, but she found herself stuck in place.

“Wha…?” She looked down at her tail, but it wasn’t a tail. It was legs. Two human legs with feet at the end, like she’d seen in drawings.

No. She’d seen them in real life.

Where had she seen them?

It didn’t really matter where’d she seen them, she decided, because they were here now. They were in the place where her tail should have been. Pale, slightly hairy skin replaced iridescent purple and teal scales. Wiggly toes replaced swishy fins.

Agatha looked up, and suddenly she was alone. The slabs that her students typically sat at were gone, as were the students themselves. Agatha tried to turn around, but the seat she was sitting it did not budge. She craned her head to look behind her, but all she saw was a plain rock wall. The decorations she had put up at the beginning of the school term were gone. Her collection of human objects was gone.

So was the water.

The room was entirely empty.

Agatha attempted to push herself up but ended up tumbling to the ground. She looked down at her legs and found that her tail was back, flopping on the ground like a suffocating fish, no water to hydrate it. She gasped and turned toward the doorway, which was still black like the opening to a cave at the rock bottom of the sea.

“Help!” she cried out, dragging herself across a gritty, sandy floor. “Help!” She reached her hand out toward the doorway, praying that there was somebody there to reach back for her.

But, instead, the darkness began to radiate from the doorway.

Agatha gasped and reared back as tendrils of darkness crept across the floor toward her. She pushed herself back as it inched toward her, getting faster and faster as she tried to scramble away. Her tail acted more as an obstacle now, and Agatha found herself wishing for the legs and the feet. She may not have known quite how to use them, but at least they would have made getting away without water possible.

Wouldn’t they?

When she looked up again, the darkness was closer, the tendrils reaching out like splayed-open hands, wrapping themselves around her wrists and tugging, tugging, until…

Agatha sat up in bed, gasping. Her face was cold and wet – a feeling she was still unfamiliar with. She reached up and felt water on her forehead, her hair sticking to her temples. She pushed it all away, feeling hot. When she shoved her blankets away from her bottom half, she was almost relieved to see her legs. She wiggled her toes and sighed, her entire body relaxing as she leaned back on her hands.

She allowed herself to fall back to the pillows, sinking into their softness as she took a couple of deep breaths, her arms splayed at her sides.

She was safe. There was darkness, but not so much that she couldn’t see. There were lights outside her windows. Lilia said that they were streetlights: they came on when the sun went down because humans weren’t quite as adept at seeing in the dark as mermaids. She could hear the ocean, could hear water lapping against the shore. Lilia had explained these sounds, too.

Agatha wanted to go look out the window, to see if she could catch sight of the waves. She’d never seen them before, not from above.

She had also heard of lights in the sky that sparkled at night – stars.  And the moon. She’d never seen the moon before. She had no idea what it would even look like. She wanted to see it at least once before she had to return to the Pod.

Agatha pushed herself back up and bent her knees, pulling them against her chest like she used to do with her tail when she was young and feeling so small and alone in such a big ocean, when it was dark and quiet and she couldn’t fall asleep because her mind was so…loud.

The dream that had scared her into the conscious world was already fading from her memory, dripping slowly back into her subconscious. She couldn’t remember all the exact details, but she remembered the darkness reaching out for her.

Agatha had always hated the dark.

She leaned back against the wall behind her bed, keeping herself upright at an angle that made it easier to look outside. She held her folded legs close to her chest as she looked out onto the streets outside of Lilia’s house, which looked almost like they were underwater, as well. Everything looked kind of blue with the streetlights. She could see other houses, but no humans walking around just yet.

Everything was asleep.

Soon, so was Agatha.

“Wakey! Wakey!” a voice sang in Agatha’s ear as there was suddenly a blinding white light behind her eyelids. She groaned, turning over and pressing her face into the soft rock beneath her head. “No, no, no,” the voice sang. “No more sleeping! We have a lot to do before I drop you off at PT with Rio.”

Rio? The name rang an instant bell in Agatha’s head. She remembered deep brown eyes and a gap-toothed smile, and her heart fluttered.

She sat up, half of her hair plastered to her face.

“Figured that might get you up,” Lilia said with a crooked little grin. She waggled her eyebrows at Agatha as the younger woman pushed her hair away from her face. “Come on,” she said, “let’s get you to the bathroom.” She approached Agatha and helped her stand. “Let’s try this without the chair,” Lilia said. “Just one foot in front of the other.”

Agatha looked down, furrowing her brow. She looked back up at Lilia.

“Bend your knee,” Lilia instructed.

“Mah wah?” Agatha asked.

“Right,” Lilia sighed. She leaned down a bit and poked part of Agatha’s right leg. “This part,” she said. “Lift it.” She patted the back of Agatha’s leg.

She lifted it then looked at Lilia expectantly.

“Now bend it,” she said, helping Agatha to perform the action. “Brava!” she said. “Now, here’s a slightly scary part: you need to lean forward and quickly straighten the leg you just lifted as you fall onto it.”

“What?!” Agatha exclaimed. “No!”

“That’s how you walk, kid,” Lilia said. “You’ll catch on quickly, I promise. Besides, I’m right here to catch you if you need me. I’m not gonna let you fall.”

“Whuh shul ah truz yew?” Agatha demanded. “Yew leh me!”

“I know, doll,” Lilia said, reaching up to stroke her cheek. “I know. But I’m here now, and I’m trying to help you.” She took a deep breath. “Walking is scary. It’s a lot of trust to put in your own body – or somebody else – to catch you, but it’s necessary. Come on.”

She took a step back with little effort, as if to show off. Agatha frowned at her, but she leaned forward, straightening her leg in front of her and letting herself fall onto it.

It wasn’t a very long journey, all things considered. She was taller than Lilia by a few inches, but she still wasn’t that tall at all. Her foot hit the ground within seconds and she didn’t even fall over. Lilia was still holding her hands. She gave Agatha a bright smile.

“Look at that!” she said. “You did it! Now, shift your weight forward, like this –” she used one hand to shift Agatha’s hips toward herself, changing the weight distribution from her left leg to the right one, causing the back part of the left foot to lift off the ground. “Good,” Lilia said. “Now bend the knee and bring it forward like you just did with the other foot, but lean back a little as you do it, for balance.”

Agatha did as she was told and nearly fell back onto the bed, but Lilia kept a firm hold on her. For somebody so small, she was strong.

She helped Agatha make her way across the room and then across a narrow hallway to the bathroom, where Agatha learned about a few more human customs that fell on the grosser side. Lilia helped her as she explained that Agatha wasn’t the first mermaid that she’d had to help acclimate to the human world.

“It rarely takes more than a couple of days before they get the hang of it,” Lilia said.

“Th’ ‘ang o’ it?” Agatha asked.

“Walking, talking, using the bathroom…” Lilia explained. “You have all the muscles you need in your legs to help you out: they just haven’t been used this way yet. And talking…well, you know how to talk, obviously. You just need to get used to the no water thing. We’ll practice while we’re out shopping today.”

“Shawpin’?” Agatha asked, blinking at her. “Theh’s a mawkeh?

“Sort of,” Lilia said. “There’s a plaza about two blocks from here. A plaza,” she explained, “is like a market, but not everything is sold at stalls like in the Pod. Each seller typically has their own enclosed space to sell their wares. We’ll head to the thrift store first to buy you clothes that fit your style better than my leggings and Rio’s hoodie.”

“‘Ewdee’?” Agatha asked, looking down at the sweater she was still wearing. “Sweh-tah,” she said, pointing at it.

“Yes, that is a sweater,” Lilia sighed. “But it’s typically called a hoodie. Because it has this part,” she reached behind Agatha and pulled something over her head, covering Agatha’s eyes so that she was plunged into sudden darkness.

Agatha let out a squeak while Lilia chuckled, pushing the thing back over her eyes.

“Why?” Agatha asked. “Why do ‘ey hab dis?”

“To keep them dry from the rain?” Lilia shrugged. “I really have no clue. Rio used to wear hers with her hood up all the time when she was a moody teenager. She’s had that one for as long as I can remember. I’m surprised she even loaned it to you in the first place.”

Agatha flushed at that, a tiny thrill buzzing through her. In the Pod, personal possessions were sacred. Giving gifts was not unheard of, but giving somebody else your own possession, whether to have or to just borrow, was intimate. It was usually reserved for engagements or serious declarations.

Here, it seemed to be rather…ordinary.

“Don’t get all up in your head now,” Lilia said, guiding Agatha back toward her bedroom. “You two have only just met. I’m sure Rio didn’t mean it like that.”

“Ah nuh thah,” Agatha huffed, but her face was still hot as Lilia helped her back to the bed.

The older woman chuckled.

“This hoodie may be a little bit warm for today’s weather, though. I have a few shirts and tank tops that would probably be better suited.” She crossed the room to what she’d previously called a dresser, where she’d gotten the leggings from the night before. “They all say ‘Lilia’s Leggings’ on them, however,” she warned. “That’s my side hustle.”

Agatha opened her mouth, but Lilia beat her to it: “Just a way for me to make money. We trade in paper currency up here, though. No bartering.”

Agatha knew what money was. Enough coins and colored paper had fallen to the bottom of the ocean for her curiosity to be piqued. There were also Sirens who liked to talk about what lay above the surface. They were typically discouraged from speaking about what they saw on land by the elders, but few of them actually cared what those old geezers thought.

Still, most of their stories were whispered. One of them had slipped Agatha a faded, rectangular piece of green paper and a few coins of different sizes and said that it would probably be enough to buy her a nice meal on the surface, winking at her.

Agatha kind of wished she had it still. Like her bag.

“Mah bag,” she said. “Wheh ish mah bag?”

“You had a bag with you?” Lilia asked. Agatha nodded. “I’ll ask Rio and Marisol about it, but it’s likely that you lost it in the ocean, dear.” She gave Agatha a sympathetic frown.

“But I may that mahshel’,” Agatha moaned.

“I have no doubt of that,” Lilia said. “I’ll help you make a new one before you return. Hell, maybe you’ll even be able to find the one you lost on your way back.”

She pulled some pieces of fabric out of the dresser and walked over to sit next to Agatha on the bed.

“Take off the sweater, please,” she said. “We need to see how these fit.”

Agatha pouted at that and wrapped her arms around herself, holding the sweater more firmly to her body.

“You can put it back on later,” Lilia promised. “But, like I said, it may be too hot for that today. The last thing I want is for you to get overheated and land back in the hospital. Come on now: off.”

Agatha rolled her eyes and allowed Lilia to help her find her way out of the sweater. The older woman folded it and set it aside before handing Agatha one of the pieces of fabric.

“This is called a T-shirt,” Lilia said. “Can you try to say that? T-shirt?”

“T-shit,” Agatha said, then snorted. “Soory.”

Lilia cackled. “No worries, doll,” she retorted. “T-shirt. Try again.” She held up the light purple shirt, which said, “Lilia’s Leggings” in swoopy black writing.

“Teee,” Agatha enunciated. “Sherrrrt.”

“Very good!” Lilia said with a clap. “Faster now.”

“Tee shirt!” Agatha said, beaming with pride like one of her students.

“Fantastic!” Lilia praised. “You’re finally getting this speaking on dry land thing. Before you know it, you’ll be a regular William Shakespeare.” Agatha furrowed her brow. “No time for a history lesson. Let’s just say, he was very good at speaking. Put on the T-shirt.”

“Thih ith gew,” Agatha said, with a shrug.

“This is good,” Lilia corrected. “Say it.”

Agatha breathed in through her nose in annoyance. “Thissss,” she said, trying her hardest not to leave room for the water, “issss goo-duh.”

“Good!” Lilia praised. “Now, say it without the hissing.”

Agatha glared at her. Tried again. “This. Is. Good.”

“What,” Agatha said, speaking slowly and carefully so as not to be forced to repeat anything, “is…a…b-bath?”

“Yes?” Agatha said.

“Well, it’s for bathing, which is something humans do when they get dirty. I should have given you a bath yesterday, but you seemed tired and upset. You need one before we leave the house today, though. That hair…” She tutted and shook her head.

“Wha’s wrong wi’ mah hurr?” Agatha asked, pressing her lips together at the look Lilia gave her. “What,” she said, “is wrong…with…my hair?”

Speaking without the water was getting easier, but it still felt like a nuisance, especially when Lilia kept making her repeat everything she said wrong.

She felt the material, which was very soft on the outside. There were a few places where it was thinner than the rest of the fabric, but she liked that.

She wanted to put it back on, immediately, but Lilia had told her to get undressed, so she probably wouldn’t be able to keep it on for very long, anyway.

Still, Agatha was determined to wear it for the rest of the day.

Agatha was dying.

Heat was not something that she was accustomed to. The bottom of the ocean was not what anybody would consider warm, by any means. There were spots that were warmer, of course, but the lack of sun and many volcanoes nearby meant that it was relatively cool – which was just fine. Mermaids didn’t tend to feel the cold, anyway.

Lilia said it was because mermaids, like most fish, were cold-blooded, even the ones that were born on land.

“It keeps us from freezing to death down there,” Lilia had explained when she’d helped Agatha into the hot water of the bath. “Humans, on the other hand, appreciate the warmth.”

Agatha had not appreciated the heat of the bathwater at first. But, oddly, her body had acclimated to it rather quickly as she sank down into the deep tub. The water rose to nearly her shoulders and she felt her muscles relax all over. It felt…good.

Agatha was learning so many new words as Lilia washed her hair, her fingers working through the strands with gentle patience, making Agatha relax more and more.

Her singing voice was rather nice, as well.

Then she helped Agatha clean her body, which was a slightly awkward experience as she guided her on which parts she was meant to really scrub down on. By the time the bath was nearly finished, the water had turned a slightly different color that made Agatha wrinkle her nose.

While the water disappeared down a small hole at the bottom of the bathtub, Lilia had Agatha stand and reached for something on the wall that caused water to spray from above. Warm water, thankfully, as Agatha had found that the cool water around her was not quite as pleasant as warm water.

“Baths aren’t as clean as people would like to think,” Lilia had explained as she rinsed off Agatha’s body from head to toe. “At some point, you’re just bathing in your own filth.”

Agatha had wrinkled her nose at that, looking down as the dirty water slowly mixed with the fresh, clean water from the tap. She watched as all of it disappeared into the hole.
