Chapter 8

Chapter Summary

Jen POV!

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Chapter Eight

It had been four days since Jen saw Agatha, and she was really starting to panic.

(She had honestly started to panic that first night, when Agatha hadn’t shown up to celebrate, but she knew that Agatha would be annoyed and call her dramatic for it.)

She had gone to Agatha’s home the morning after being stood up, right before reporting for work, to ask her what the hell happened to her the night before – and maybe even guilt her into treating Jen that night – but…she wasn’t home.

She wasn’t anywhere in the Pod.

Jen spent the next several days searching for her everywhere.

When she wasn’t working, that is. Because while Agatha’s primary job was teaching and she had the week off to do whatever she pleased (including, apparently, disappear) Jen still had to see patients and treat their ailments, which left her little time to search for her friend.

‘Friend’ at times felt like a loose term for what she and Agatha were.

They had grown up in the Pod together, having been brought there, separately, just days apart. They both had the same Siren – or so they’d been told. Neither of them had ever actually met her because she’d disappeared almost immediately after bringing Agatha to the Pod, but they’d both grown up in the same class group with the knowledge that they were there because of her.

Like Agatha, Jen was curious about the world outside their Pod.

Unlike Agatha, Jen rarely left the Pod to go out and explore. She was too cognizant of what it might mean if she got caught. She had worked hard to become a healer and she didn’t want to jeopardize her standing in the community like that.

Perhaps she was a coward, like Agatha often accused her of being.

But now…maybe it was the smart thing to never have ventured outside because, as her search for Agatha around the Pod came up empty, it was becoming obvious that something had happened to her friend.

On the fifth day since she’d last seen Agatha, Jen decided that she was going to look for her outside the Pod. She’d been outside before, with Agatha leading her to all her favorite places to find ‘little treasures’ from the human world, but she had never gone alone. Her curiosity was sated by the information they already had on the human world – information passed down by generations of Sirens who had been there and experienced walking amongst them in their efforts to save infants from parents who did not want them.

Jen didn’t feel she needed any more than that.

Agatha had always been hungry for more knowledge than they were fed, though. She could never be satisfied.

And that curiosity may have just cost Agatha her life.

Jen tried not to think about it as she prepared a pack for herself. She had the day off from her healing duties, so she was going outside to search for her. It was certainly possible that Agatha had decided to spend her waking hours outside the Pod, searching high and low for new ‘little treasures’ and had completely forgotten about everything outside of that, including Jen.

It wouldn’t be the first time that had happened. She’d even known Agatha to spend the nights in some shipwreck, sleeping on whatever soft materials the humans used in those ships so that she could wake up and continue her exploration. She didn’t even care if she ran into a shark or a giant squid or any other predators that might cut her life short.

Agatha insisted that the leaders of the Pod were fearmongering when it came to dangerous creatures like sharks. They only wanted to keep the mermaids in line, to keep them from leaving the Pod…to keep them compliant.

Jen just figured Agatha was being reckless.

Now, she knew she was being stupid.

As she put food and a knife and healing supplies into her bag, Jen began to fear what she might find out there. Agatha wasn’t her only friend in the Pod, but she was the closest Jen had to a sister. They’d been adopted by different families. Jen had two mothers and two brothers, all of whom had been loving and affectionate toward Jen her entire life, but Agatha had two mothers who treated her like a chore they had to do rather than a daughter that they loved and adored.

Agatha had been treated similarly by most of their teachers and other elders. She’d been told that her curiosity was too much and wrong and was going to get her killed one day. They ignored the discoveries Agatha made on her trips outside the Pod. They didn’t care for the artifacts she found from the human world or the information she had gleaned from them.

But Jen did (even when she didn’t).

Agatha and Jen might have acted like they annoyed the living shit out of each other, but they understood each other – or tried to, at the very least – the way others didn’t. Jen did not judge Agatha the way the others did. She didn’t try to put her down or shove her into a box. And Agatha, as much as she acted otherwise, was empathetic and a good listener and actually a pretty good friend when it came down to it.

And Jen would do just about anything to bring her back to the Pod safely.

She didn’t want to sound the alarm about Agatha’s disappearance just yet. She still had a few days left until Agatha would be expected back to teach her students. Until then, the only person who seemed at all concerned about her was Jen.

And Jen was going to do her best to make sure that Agatha made it back, unharmed.

She just hoped that was still possible.

By the time she made it past the guards to the outside of the Pod, it was already late morning. There was little light this deep in the ocean – far out of reach of any humans – but it was enough for her to see the paths forward.

She avoided those paths, of course, because that’s typically where a guard’s eye would go first if they saw any kind of movement. Jen was technically within her rights to leave the Pod, but it was discouraged. Some of the leaders, she heard, had attempted to get it banned outright, but there was a lot of public agreement that mermaids should have the freedom to leave as they saw fit.

They weren’t prisoners, after all.

Still, there was a stigma attached to being someone who would leave the safety of the Pod. There were also repercussions though they found other reasons to discipline those who went against the leaders’ express wishes. Jen had to be careful.

She snuck around coral and rock formations until she was certain that she was out of sight of the guards, then she swam out into open ocean, feeling oddly…free. She hadn’t noticed how trapped it felt to be stuck in the Pod, feeling the eyes of everyone around her – especially the leaders and elders. There was always someone there to judge you, to keep you in line and doing what you were expected to do.

Maybe this was why Agatha preferred to be away from there.

Jen basked in it and the warmth that came with swimming higher, toward the surface where the sun could more easily reach her through the water. She’d rarely felt this warm before – only when Agatha dragged her out to do her ‘exploring’ – but it was nice. She liked it.

When she heard a growl, however, her heart stuttered in her chest.

Jen spun around to see…

“Scratchy,” Jen sighed in relief. “Thank Poseidon, it’s only you.” She ran her hand over his scarred back as he circled her. “Have you seen Agatha?”

Jen had met Scratchy once or twice when she joined Agatha on her little treasure hunts. The lemon shark loved Agatha like he was her child, and she loved him, baby-talking him and feeding him leftover bits of eel straight from her hand. He never bit her or so much as growled in her direction.

Scratchy pressed his nose against Jen’s side insistently, letting out little grunts and whines as he circled her again and again, keeping her almost trapped.

“What is up with you?” Jen huffed, attempting to escape his orbit.

Scratchy did not relent, pressing insistently against her belly, hips, shoulders…anything he could, really.

“Scratchy, quit it!” Jen said, batting him away gently. “What’s wrong?” She eyed him, warily, searching his body for any evidence of a new injury. He had probably gotten a piece of coral or a hook stuck in him again. He was always finding new and creative ways to injure himself. This time, at least Jen had the materials to help him.

But there were no new scars. Every injury on his body had long since healed. Jen ran her hands over his form just to check, but she couldn’t find anything wrong…

Until she reached his mouth. There, she paused. There, she found a familiar object stuck between his rows of teeth.

Agatha’s necklace.

Jen carefully extracted the purple gemstone necklace from Scratchy’s mouth, stroking his head as she did so. The chain was broken, but more from tension than from anything sharp. Agatha had broken it herself, clearly, from pulling it off her own neck.

Jen’s heart dropped into her stomach at that.

The only reason for a landborn mermaid to tug off their own necklace while still underwater was to keep from being found out by humans if they were about to be captured by them.

Agatha had been discovered.

She was in trouble.

“Scratchy,” Jen said, holding up the purple gemstone, “can you take me to Agatha? Do you know where she is?”

Scratchy let out a growl and swam a circle around Jen before swimming away from her in a straight line. Jen followed, Agatha’s necklace clutched tightly in her hand.

Chapter End Notes

they act like they hate each other sometimes, but you just know that Agatha and Jen are Ride or Die for each other 😏

Chapter 9

Chapter Summary

Agatha and Rio get closer...

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Chapter Nine

“Harkness?” Rio hummed, testing the word on her tongue. “Harkness. Agatha Harkness…”

“Why do you keep saying it like that?” Agatha asked, doing her sit-ups on the gym floor while Rio held her feet, her hands warm even through the material of Agatha’s sneakers.

It was her fifth day of rehab. They were back in the gym once again, and Rio had insisted that Agatha do a few stretches and exercises that she assured her would help to lessen the ache in her calves while she walked. Agatha really didn’t need the parallel bars anymore, but Rio wanted her to stay in the gym for the time being. If she could work out for thirty minutes straight today without resting, she said, they could spend their next session walking around the plaza.

Agatha was already starting to break a sweat and all she was really doing was sitting up over and over again. The effort made her stomach hurt, but she was determined not to need a rest until she’d done thirty of them.

In the meantime, she had told Rio her new last name.

Or, as far as Rio knew, the name that she’d only just remembered yesterday. Agatha and Lilia had spent much of the previous evening coming up with false memories about her life before she got to their little island.

“It’s just an interesting name,” Rio said, flushing slightly. “And you were a teacher?”

“On sabbatical,” Agatha confirmed, “after my mother passed a couple of months ago.” She might have felt bad about lying about her mother’s death if she thought that any one of the three she had gave a shit about her. “I went on a cruise to, uh…” Shit. She couldn’t remember the words Lilia had given her. “I went on a cruise to…” she shook her head, grimacing, trying to remember.

“Clear your head?” Rio offered.

That wasn’t what Lilia said, but it sounded correct, so Agatha nodded as she came up for another sit-up, grunting, “Yup!” before falling back down to the ground.

“Slower,” Rio coached. “You don’t want to injure your back, Miss Harkness.”

Agatha felt her face flush. “You don’t need to start using it all the time now,” she murmured, taking a breath as she lifted herself up again, careful to keep her arms tucked behind her head as Rio had instructed. This was becoming harder to do as the ache in her stomach – as well as the one in her lower back –grew.

“I just like the sound of it,” Rio said. “Sounds like an abstract noun. Like you have Harkness. It fits.”

“I’m not sure if I should take that as a compliment,” Agatha admitted. There was sweat on her brow as she lowered herself back down, more slowly this time. It was getting in her eyes. She used one hand to swipe it away. “How many was that?”

“Oh.” Rio blinked down at her. “I lost count a while ago.”

“Seriously?” Agatha practically whined. “When?”

“At eighteen, I think?” Rio said. “Like, two or three minutes ago?” She grimaced, peeking down at Agatha over the curve of her knees. “You probably passed thirty by now.”

“So, I can rest?” Agatha asked. It was more of a plea. She did not like sit-ups.

“Yes,” Rio said, letting go of her feet and moving to sit beside her. “You can rest now.” Agatha let her feet slide forward until her legs lie flat on the ground. “For a couple of minutes,” Rio said. “Then we’re hopping on the treadmill.”

Agatha let out a groan. “My stomach hurts,” she complained.

“That’s just your six-pack forming,” Rio said, poking her in her stomach, which was still soft and, apparently, ticklish. Agatha’s body curled forward in reflex as she let out an involuntary noise that was somewhat a mix between a laugh and a squeak. “Aww, like the Pillsbury Dough Boy,” Rio cooed.

“The what?” Agatha huffed, her face flaming as her hands protected her belly from being poked a second time.

“You’ve never heard of the Pillsbury Dough Boy?” Rio asked. “Or you just don’t remember it?”

“Is it something that a lot of people know about?” Agatha asked. She sat up, turning to look at Rio, whose eyes were wide and so brown and warm Agatha felt like she could get lost in them.

Why did the Pod have so few brown eyes?

“The Pillsbury Dough Boy is the mascot for a cookie dough brand, basically,” Rio explained, patient as ever. “I mean, not just cookie dough, but that’s what I tend to associate him with. Lilia would always use Pillsbury dough whenever she babysat me and we baked cookies together.” She smiled fondly, as if remembering. “There’s this thing that the people in the commercials do where they poke him in the belly and he makes this little sound, like ‘hoo-hoo!’” She flushed when Agatha let out a little laugh at the unexpectedly adorable sound. “That’s just what that reminded me of, I guess.”

“That’s not the sound I made!” Agatha insisted, trying to look annoyed.

“It’s pretty damn close,” Rio said, leaning back on her hands. “Your sound was cuter, though.”

Agatha felt her entire body go hot at that, her eyes flicking to Rio’s lips. For her part, Rio’s eyes widened like she was shocked at her own words, her lips parting a bit in surprise. They widened further when they caught Agatha’s gaze flicker down.

Then, before Agatha even knew what was happening, Rio had jumped to her feet and was practically on the other side of the gym, standing next to the treadmill.

“Okay!” Rio said, clapping. “Let’s do this. Thirty minutes, no breaks.”

She motioned toward the treadmill, urging Agatha to get on.

Agatha took a deep breath and pushed herself to her feet. Her midsection ached as she walked stiffly to the treadmill. Rio offered her a hand to step onto the strange machine. Agatha had been on it before, but not for very long. The first time, she’d nearly fallen off.

Rio had been quick to catch her, nearly twisting in a complete circle and falling over with Agatha half in her arms.

“Are you alright?” she’d asked, one hand holding Agatha’s thigh and the other supporting her shoulders. Their faces were impossibly close together, noses practically brushing. Rio’s hot breath had fanned across Agatha’s face. She felt her face heat up at that. She had searched Rio’s deep brown eyes, had felt herself lean in closer…

Only to be practically dropped back onto her ass as Rio hastened to pull away.

Not literally. Rio had made certain that Agatha was on solid ground, that her feet were steady and planted on the ground before she let go and moved back a few feet, her hands behind her back as she cleared her throat, face coral pink.

Then she’d helped Agatha back onto the treadmill and encouraged her to walk at a steady pace while not even daring to place her hands anywhere near Agatha’s body, as if she might be burned or something.

Agatha was certain that the attraction she felt for the other woman was mutual. Rio was not the first to look at Agatha with that hunger in her eyes. She wasn’t the first to allow her gaze to flicker down to Agatha’s lips. Agatha had done the same, however involuntarily, over the last few days.

Rio was very attractive. Her arms were muscular, as were her legs and abdomen and…well, pretty much every part of her was muscular. She had mentioned that, somewhat by accident, to Lilia, who gave her a knowing smile and responded, “Well, she should be: she’s been fishing recreationally since she was just a little girl.”

Right. Agatha had almost forgotten that the only reason she was in this mess was because of Rio and her fishing net. It was almost worth it, though, to be able to spend this much time in a place she’d always dreamed of but never thought she’d actually get to experience – at least not while she stuck so closely to the Pod, which Lilia had informed her was at least a half-day’s swim away.

Agatha took Rio’s hand, which was strong and warm and rough with calluses, and stepped onto the treadmill, clutching the bars on either side of her body to steady herself as it began to move.

“We’ll start you off slow,” Rio said, clipping the little red safety line to her shirt so that it would shut off if she happened to fall and pressing the up arrow on the little screen in front of Agatha, invading her space a little as she did so. “Let’s say…two miles per hour to start?”

Agatha nodded, and Rio set the pace.

“Thirty minutes starts now,” Rio said, pressing something on her watch as she stepped back from the treadmill.

There was a long moment of silence as Agatha focused on her breathing and putting one foot in front of the other as she’d been taught.

“So,” Rio said, “what was your mother like?”

Shit. Lilia had warned her that this was a probability: Rio asking questions.

“The girl can’t stay quiet for too long,” she’d said to Agatha the night prior, over a dinner of chicken and rice with vegetables. Agatha didn’t like the little green things in her rice. She tried picking them out, but Lilia reached over to smack her hand. “Eat those,” she said. “They’re good for you.” Agatha pouted at her, but Lilia just moved right along. “She used to follow me around asking questions incessantly about anything and everything should could think of.”

“That sounds adorable,” Agatha said, picturing a smaller Rio, with the same big brown eyes and tooth gap, following around Lilia as she went about her chores, chattering on and on the entire time.

“Of course it does,” Lilia snorted, “to you. But you’re a teacher. How would you feel if your kids just asked incessant questions without letting you get a word in, edgewise?”

“You presume that they don’t already do that,” Agatha shot back.

They didn’t. They barely interacted with the lessons she did try to engage them with. But Lilia didn’t need to know that.

“Well, then you’ll be well-prepared,” Lilia said, spearing a piece of chicken with her fork and using it to gesture, “for all the questions she’s bound to ask you about your past.”

Agatha thought about her mothers – all three of them.

The first one, she didn’t know. She had asked Lilia about her, but it was Lilia’s insistence that Agatha didn’t want to know about her, that she wasn’t a pleasant woman in the least. She had given Agatha up just hours – if not moments – after she was born, and that was all Agatha needed to know, really.

As for her other others…

Naia had been the primary comfort-giver. She’d been the one who stayed up with Agatha when she didn’t feel well, telling her stories about the human world and how strange they were. She’d never seen it herself, she said, but all the tales about them were similar enough to one another that they must have been true.. She also sang songs to Agatha, rocking her back and forth until Agatha fell asleep against her shoulder. She was a healer by trade, so she had taken care of all of the aches and pains Agatha had acquired due to her curiosity.

Her other mother was Ola, who was more of a disciplinarian. She was the one who made sure that Agatha got up for her lessons and did all her chores before she was let out to go ‘exploring,’ but she was also the one who answered all of Agatha’s questions, which only ever led to Agatha asking more questions. She had initially encouraged Agatha’s curiosity, hoping that it would lead her to being a historian or a teacher like herself – which it did, in a roundabout way. She had congratulated Agatha when she initially got her placement as a teacher, but when she found out that Agatha was sneaking out of the Pod to go exploring…

Well, needless to say, she disapproved. They both did. And they both chastised her about it, telling her that she was taking her curiosity too far and that they couldn’t protect her if she kept going like she was, that it was bound to catch up to her.

Agatha had tried to defend herself, had insisted that she was being safe, that she knew what she was doing, that she never went anywhere near the surface…but they never wanted to listen. They just saw her as that same curious, reckless little girl that they used to take care of. To them, she was immature, stupid, naïve, and too curious for her own good.

And they refused to support that lifestyle.

Before she left their home for the last time five years prior, Naia looked her right in the eye and said, “We are so disappointed in you.”

Agatha felt as though her heart had been shredded into a million tiny pieces.

“She was…tough,” Agatha said, after probably too long. Rio was still listening intently, though. She could tell by the way her eyes were still focused on Agatha, long after the question had been posed. “She was strict, but loving. She made sure I got my homework done and took care of me when I was sick. She cared, I think.”

“Sure sounds like she did,” Rio said.

Agatha shook her head. “Our relationship was…complicated,” she admitted. “I haven’t spoken to the-her in five years.”

“Oh,” Rio said.

There was so much sympathy in that one syllable. Agatha hated it. She pressed the up arrow a few times, making the treadmill go faster until she was at 2.5 mph. As if she could run away from the conversation even though she wasn’t great at running yet. She was barely able to walk as quickly as she set the treadmill.

She really shouldn’t have done that.

“Her death must have come as a surprise,” Rio said. “My dad’s did, too.”

Agatha’s eyes widened at that. She’d forgotten that Rio had had a second parent at one time. And that it was a man. Lilia had told her little about Rio’s father, and Rio had told her even less. She just knew that he was a fisher and Rio took after him in that respect.

“What was he like?” Agatha asked, needing the distraction as her calves began to burn.

Rio smiled softly. “Goofy,” she said. “He was always joking and trying make me and Mami laugh. He liked puns a lot.” She motioned to her own shirt, which had a picture of a shark and words that said, ‘I’m a little Sharkastic.’

Agatha hadn’t even noticed it when she walked in that day. Rio’s biceps, which were bared by the rolled-up sleeves, took up most of her vision. She laughed at the joke now, snorting a little bit.

Oh. That was embarrassing. Agatha had learned that she made that sound when she laughed a little too hard and that it sounded kind of like an animal Lilia called a pig. Lilia had insisted that it was cute, but Agatha disagreed, her face going hot every time the sound left her nostrils.

She cleared her throat when she saw Rio’s amused grin, turning forward to avoid her gaze. Rio, thankfully, was able to take a hint.

“Anyway,” she said, “I guess I got my sense of humor from him. He used to take me fishing all the time, too. He knew how much I loved sharks and how much I wanted to study them when I got older.”

Agatha furrowed her brow at that, turning back to Rio. “You want to study sharks?” she asked.

“Wanted to,” Rio corrected. “When I was little, I used to read every book I could find on sharks. I used to draw them, too. I got pretty good at it. My dad used to hang my pictures up in the cabin on his boat and on our fridge at home. He said he was saving them to frame them for my office when I became a ‘world-famous marine biologist’.” Her grin widened, but her eyes filled with tears. “I still have most of them.”

“Where are they?” Agatha asked.

“Most are still in the cabin on the boat,” Rio said. “I don’t go in there a lot, but, when I do, they’re all still right where I left them.” She cleared her throat. “Where he left them.”

There was another long moment of silence.

“How did he die?” Agatha asked, her voice quiet, cautious.

“He was very sick,” Rio said, her voice just as quiet. “He didn’t tell us until it was almost too late, though.” She took a deep breath. “Just before I graduated from undergrad.”

Agatha didn’t know what that meant.

“That must have been rough,” she said, anyway.

“Yeah, well…” Rio shrugged. “I had to be there for my mom, so…”

“What does that mean?” Agatha asked. “Having to be there for your mom.”

“Nothing,” Rio insisted. “Just that, I dunno…my feelings kind of didn’t matter then.”

“What?” Agatha nearly stopped walking, tripping a bit on the treadmill. Rio instinctively reached out to steady her, but she batted her hands away. “Of course they matter!”

“Well, I know that now,” Rio assured her. “After, like, two years of therapy and some distance. But, back then…my mom could barely get out of bed for six months following his death. Lilia and I had to take care of her. I dropped out of my grad program.”

“I thought you said undergrad.”

“I got into a grad program right before I got my Bachelor’s. Mom insisted that I go, that she’d be fine.”

“But she wasn’t?”

Rio shook her head, her lips pressed firmly together.

“Aunt Lilia called before we even got to Halloween,” she said, her voice flat. “She said that Mami wasn’t doing well, that she wasn’t eating or bathing, that Lilia had to call into work for her to extend her leave. She was thinking about admitting her into a psych ward if she didn’t get better soon.” She took a deep breath, but it was uneven, shuddery. “She didn’t tell me I needed to come back right away, but she implied that it would be for the best if I was here for her.” Rio sniffled, swiping at her eyes. “So, I came back. I left my program.”

“You left your dream?” Agatha asked.

Rio snorted. “I can pick up that dream whenever I want to,” she said. “I’m not even thirty yet; I have time. My Mami needed me.”

“Does she still need you?” Agatha asked. She peered over her shoulder at Rio, who was tidying up other exercise equipment now, obviously trying to look busy. “She seemed alright the last time I saw her.”

That was two days ago, at Agatha’s MRI and follow-up check-up. Dr. Vidal – Marisol, she preferred Agatha to call her – had done a few small tests to check Agatha’s vitals. Agatha was still relying on the wheelchair to take her longer distances, but she’d been able to walk across the room, which Marisol found quite impressive.

She hadn’t looked sick or sad. She had curves and a healthy overall look to her, so obviously she’d been eating and drinking. And she was at work. Her little office, which Agatha and Lilia had sat in with her, was cluttered and filled with personal items and decorations all over, so she’d obviously been there for a while.

“She’s doing a lot better now,” Rio agreed. “It’s been a few years since Papi passed. Almost a decade, actually.” She took a breath. “Still, I don’t want to go too far, and the program I was in…it’s pretty far away.”

“How far away?” Agatha asked.

“A plane ride.”

“Lilia said everything is a plane ride away from here,” Agatha pointed out.

“This one is a long plane ride,” Rio said. “It took forever to get back here last time, so I just…stayed. There was a DPT program at the local college, so I stayed, worked, fished…” She shrugged. “I can study sharks on my own time.”

“So you settled,” Agatha said.

“What?” Rio asked, a slight edge to her voice. She furrowed her brow at Agatha, who had already turned back around.

“You settled,” Agatha said. “You took the simpler route.”

“You think being a PT is easy?” Rio huffed.

“No,” Agatha said. “That’s not what I said.” She pressed the down arrow on the treadmill to give herself a little room to breathe. “I said it was simpler. It was a simple solution to a struggle you were having about leaving your mother behind. It was certainly easier than leaving her behind to pursue your dreams, even if your mother was fine and didn’t need you to be physically here with her.”

Rio opened her mouth to say something, but then she closed it again, allowing Agatha to continue.

“I don’t think physical therapy is easy at all. You’ve said that while I have been progressing rather quickly from my brain injury, others aren’t quite as lucky. You said they take months, and even years, to recover. That doesn’t sound easy at all. It must have taken a lot of learning.”

Rio nodded. “It did.”

“And to have done all that while helping your mother must have been difficult. Were you also fishing at the time?”

“Yes,” Rio said. “We needed the money. That was actually how it started – the fishing. My dad’s customers were pretty loyal, so whenever I brought in fish, they paid me the same as what they paid him. It kept us afloat until Mami felt ready to go back to work, which wasn’t too long after that, really.”

“That’s good,” Agatha said, her voice gentle, understanding. “I’m glad she’s doing better now. She…she’s a very nice woman. I imagine you had a happy childhood with her.”

Rio smiled. “I did,” she confirmed. “She’s the best.” She grimaced. “Not to rub it in, sorry.”

Agatha let out a surprised laugh at that. “It’s okay,” she said. “Mine may not have been the best, but I think I turned out just fine. Don’t you?” She gave Rio a grin over her shoulder, and found Rio already grinning back at her, a little lopsidedly, her tongue pressing into her cheek again.

There were more flutters in Agatha’s stomach, but now they extended up into her chest. She turned away, looking at the display on the treadmill. It had already been fifteen minutes since she began walking. Just fifteen left to go.

“You know,” Rio said, “despite the fact that you couldn’t string a sentence together, like, four days ago, your speech has certainly improved quickly.”

“That’s because I’m exceptional,” Agatha said, turning up her nose. She couldn’t fight her little grin as Rio burst into laughter next to her.

“You definitely are that,” Rio agreed.

Agatha felt the flush creep up her neck and kept her face turned away from Rio for the remainder of her walk as Rio continued to walk around and tidy the gym up from her last client.

By the time Agatha reached the thirty-minute mark, the room around her was clean and Rio was look down at her phone, her thumb rubbing the screen, probably flicking through whatever ‘app’ she was using.

Lilia had attempted to explain the concept of phones to her, but Agatha couldn’t quite grasp how a communication device had turned into something that people stared at for hours on end and used for pretty much every aspect of their lives. The phone Lilia had given her was mostly just for phone calls and texts, which took forever for Agatha to tap out using the letters on the screen, which weren’t even in alphabetical order.

She preferred phone calls.

“I’m done,” Agatha called out to her.

Rio blinked up at her. “Hmm?” she said.

Agatha pointed to the display on the treadmill, which now read 31:02, the time steadily increasing.

“Oh,” Rio said, her cheeks pink as she quickly tucked her phone back into her pocket, “sorry.” She sidled up next to Agatha, reaching over her and pressing a button labeled ‘Cool Down’ which gradually started lowering the speed on the treadmill – not that she was even going that quickly to begin with. “How do you feel?” Rio asked, after the treadmill had stopped completely and Agatha was able to step down, her legs a little achy, but not all that worse for wear.

“I’m okay,” Agatha said, wiping the sweat off her brow with a towel that Rio handed her. “What were you looking at on your phone?”

“Alice was texting me,” Rio said. “You remember my friend from the boat?”

“Not at all,” Agatha said. The only person she remembered from the boat was Rio. Hers was really the only face she’d seen before passing out.

Not that she was complaining.

“Is she your girlfriend?”

Rio’s face instantly wrinkled like she smelled or tasted something bitter. It was a face of pure disgust, but not entirely malicious. “Ew, no,” she said. “Alice is like my annoying little sister. I love her, but not like that. Besides, I’m too short for her, she said.” Rio rolled her eyes, but her face unpuckered and the previously disgusted look was replaced by an amused grin.

“Is she particularly tall?” Agatha asked.

“Ha!” Rio said. “No. She’s, like, 5 foot even. But she prefers ‘Amazonian’ women. She likes to be thrown around like a ragdoll.”

“That seems…unhealthy,” Agatha said. “Is she okay?”

“She’s fine,” Rio snorted. “She just has her preferences, like the rest of us.”

Agatha nodded. “What are your preferences?” she asked.

Rio’s eyes went almost comically wide at that question. Her face got even more red. Her lips gaped open a little bit.

But, oddly, she didn’t answer the question.

Agatha waved a hand in front of her face. “Hello?” she said. “Are you okay?”

Rio blinked and cleared her throat, swallowing thickly as she stepped back from Agatha, even though they weren’t really standing that closely together.

“Yeah,” she said. “I just…that’s not really an appropriate topic of conversation, you know? You’re my patient. It would be…unprofessional to share my romantic or sexual preferences with a patient.”

Patient. Right.

Agatha knew that.

“I thought we were friends, as well,” Agatha said, frowning. “Sorry. My mistake.”

“No!” Rio said. “I mean…um, we are friends. Technically. I guess. I just…ahem.” She righted herself, standing ramrod straight. “Currently, while you’re still my patient, we have to keep everything professional. At least until your PT is finished.”

“When is that?” Agatha asked, tilting her head to the side in curiosity. “How do I know even when it’s done?”

Rio gave her a tiny, lopsided grin. “Tomorrow is officially our last session. You don’t need my help relearning to walk anymore. You apparently never needed my help to talk. Lilia worked her own magic with that.”

“I’m also a teacher,” Agatha pointed out. “Talking was my whole profession.”

“Right,” Rio laughed. “The brain can be weird, though. Especially after a trauma – like falling off a cruise ship.”

Right. Another reason to keep things professional: Agatha’s lies.

“Yes,” Agatha said, nodding, “but I guess it wasn’t as bad as we originally thought.”

“Maybe not.”

They stood there for a long moment, just looking at one another, quiet and cautious.

Agatha cleared her throat. “What was Alice texting you about?” she asked.

“Oh!” Rio said, reaching for her phone in her pocket. “Nothing, really. Just…there’s this fair that comes through the island every summer. They’re setting up right now, and Alice was asked to DJ for it.”

“Deejay?” Agatha asked, furrowing her brow. Was that something she was supposed to know the meaning of.

“Yeah,” Rio said, oblivious to the reason for her confusion, thankfully. “Alice’s mom is this famous singer, Lorna Wu. Have you heard of her?”

Agatha shook her head. At least this she could be honest about. Music wasn’t very common in the Pod. Materials weren’t wasted on instruments, though Agatha had found plenty of human instruments in shipwrecks. She had taught herself how to play the drums she found for her class, how to make beats that sounded almost like real music.

“She has a band that Alice used to travel around with when she was a kid, before they settled down here in elementary school: Lorna Wu and the Coral Shore. Alice grew up around music and instruments. She learned how to play a bunch of them while her mom was off doing shows. She has her own band, and they play most of the weddings and events here on the island – and a few on some of our neighboring islands – but she’s also started to DJ when the demand for that increased. This is the first year she’ll be DJing for the fair. She’s really psyched about it.”

“Lucky her,” Agatha said. She had no idea what a ‘fair’ was, but it must have been something good if Rio’s friend was so excited about it. “I’m sure she’ll be great.”

“Oh, she totally will be,” Rio replied, nodding. “We’ll probably see her tomorrow, on our walk. They’re setting up the plaza for the event right now. She has her first soundcheck tomorrow evening.”

“When is the fair?” Agatha asked. It sounded weird to ask about something for which she had very little context.

“This weekend,” Rio said. “It’s mostly a bunch of food stands and a couple of small kiddie rides – a merry-go-round, a tiny train, some spinny thing – and a Ferris Wheel, which is a bit bigger. There’s also games and a few stalls set up by local artists selling their crafts.” She shrugged. “I always drop way too much on funnels cakes and punny pins, but it’s fun.”

“What’s a funnel cake?” Agatha asked.

Rio’s eyes widened, and Agatha suddenly wanted to take it back. Oh shit, was she supposed to know what a funnel cake was?

“You’ve never had a funnel cake?” Rio asked.

“Um…no,” Agatha said, giving her an awkward smile. “I’ve also never been to a fair.”

“Where did you grow up?” Rio asked. “Alcatraz?”

Okay, Agatha knew what that was. Lilia had told her about it when she read the name in a book that had given her zero context. It was a prison, which is a place where they kept those who broke very serious rules. Alcatraz was on another island, but it no longer operated. Not in over sixty years.

Agatha was not yet that old.

“Of course not!” she huffed. “I just…grew up kind of sheltered.”

That was the line Lilia gave her in case anybody questioned why she didn’t share common knowledge. She said it should be an accepted answer.

“Well,” Rio said, “I guess that means that we’re definitely checking it out tomorrow, on our walk. It’s going to be very fun, trust me.”

Agatha felt those familiar flutters in her stomach as she nodded, trying to act all nonchalant with a shrug.

“If you say so,” she said.

Alice sent a selfie of her pouting at the camera. Her big-ass headphones were around her shoulders and her hair was up in a ponytail, her red and orange dyed bangs hanging over her eyes. Her big brown eyes looked glossy with tears, but Rio wasn’t dumb enough to fall for that: she knew how good Alice was at faking tears.

She sent back a selfie of her serious face.

Rio: Not falling for it.

Al: Meanie

Rio sent back a middle finger emoji.

Alice was silent for a couple minutes, and Rio took the moment of silence to start getting dressed.

She never made any appointments until one PM even on days she didn’t plan to go fishing. It made keeping a routine easier.

Today, her only appointment was her walk around the plaza with Agatha.

Rio tried not to think too hard about that as she slipped into a pair of cargo shorts and grabbed the green carabiner with her apartment keys. Then she slipped on her green sports bra and a light blue tee that said ‘Anything is Fossible’ with a stegosaurus skeleton on it. She looked at the shirt in the mirror and chuckled at herself.

She was fucking hilarious. Fuck anybody who thought otherwise.

Rio still had a couple of hours until she had to meet Agatha outside the hospital and her stomach was starting to growl at her, so she figured she might as well feed it something.

As she gathered the ingredients for a tuna fish sandwich, her phone vibrated on the countertop. She wiped her hands on a towel before reaching for it and rolling her eyes at the picture Alice sent her of her DJ setup.

Al: Look at what you’d be missing if you skipped sound check. We’re even taking song requests for the first hour or so. Don’t be a loser!

Rio: Wow. Calling me a loser is SUCH a great way to get me to show up

Rio: You’re a marketing genius, Al

Al: I know I am, but what are you?

Rio: …

Rio: be honest

Rio: Did Lorna drop you on your head as an infant?

Al: fuck off

Al: Come to soundcheck

Al: Or I’ll find you

Al: I know where you live

Rio: You can’t even reach where I keep the spare key

Al: Good thing I have my own copy

Al: :)

Rio: creep

Al: see you soon <3

Rio: I’m blocking you

Rio, obviously, did not block her best friend, but she did mute the conversation as she finished making and then eating her tuna fish sandwich, staring out her living room window as she did so.

Her apartment wasn’t big, but it wasn’t the tiniest on the island, either. It was open-concept with separate rooms for her bedroom and bathroom, which were across the hall from each other. She had a few wide windows that were perfect for her many, many plants. They caught the sunlight for the vast majority of the day while she was out working to give them all the water, plant food, and plant siblings they could want.

Rio was seriously just one step away from giving each of them individual names. Well, they already had names, but those were based on their scientific names, not like Helen or something – that would be ridiculous.

“Here you go, Drac,” she cooed, pouring what was left of her cup of ice water into her snake plant’s pot. “Don’t worry, Aurora,” she said to her pothos, “I’ll get you some, too.” She filled the lime green watering can in the sink and returned to pour a little water into each one of the planters, stroking their bright green leaves with the pads of her fingers before returning to the kitchen (really just the area between her kitchen island and stove) to rinse out the watering can and wash her dishes.

When she looked at the time again, it was a quarter past one. She still had well over an hour before she had to meet Agatha. If she weren’t already dressed, she would go back to bed and doomscroll for a bit, but she’d made it a rule that she never got into bed with her street clothes, nor did she undress just for the sake of getting back into bed when the sun was still out – not unless she had a really good reason.

She just needed something to do.

After scrolling on her phone for a bit, Rio was starting to get antsy. She hated being bored more than anything else in the world. Being bored meant that she had nothing to do, which meant that she had time to think about things she’d rather not think about.

Like her Papi’s face the last time she saw him in person. He looked so small and frail in that hospital bed. His eyes were so filled with sadness and fear despite the smile on his lips. His hand felt weak in hers for the first time in her life. She wished she could have taken away his pain at that moment, could have given her some of her own youth and good health.

She wished she could have done something.

Rio stood up from the couch, slipped on her sneakers, and headed toward the door, making sure she had her phone, wallet, and keys as she beelined toward the door. She could not do this right now. She had too much to do today.

Not right now, but later. And she could not spiral when she had shit to do later. It would take far longer than the amount of free time she currently had.

As she walked down the path from her building to the sidewalk, she tied up her hair and started to pick up her pace. She hadn’t been on a good run in a bit. What with the fishing, her appointments, and her slight phone addiction, she hadn’t really given it much thought. But now was as good a time as any to catch up on her cardio, right?

Rio looked down at her phone. It still wasn’t even a quarter to two yet, which meant that she had over an hour until she had to meet Agatha at the hospital – plenty of time for a quick five-mile sprint. Maybe even six or seven miles. She’d decide on the way.

When she reached the sidewalk, Rio immediately started to run, focusing on her heartbeat and how it synchronized with the pounding of her feet on the pavement. She breathed evenly as she ran, like she used to when she did track in high school. Running wasn’t her favorite sport in the world or anything, but it was a good way to clear her mind of all the things she just didn’t want to think about right now. All she could really think about was her breathing, her heartbeat, and her feet not tripping her up.

That was all she needed to think about right now. Nothing else mattered.

She avoided the plaza as she ran, taking all the residential roads surrounding it so that she wouldn’t be stopped by all the people she’d grown up with – or Alice, who knew why Rio ran in the first place. She didn’t want to deal with the concern in those big brown eyes or the questions that were bound to follow.

She just wanted to run. That was all.

That was all.

In the end, Rio ran nearly eight miles before she had to find her way back to her apartment before meeting Lilia and Agatha, and she didn’t want to show up all sweaty and smelly.

It was ten to three when she returned to her apartment and ran straight to the bathroom, filling her sink with cold water and dunking her entire head into it. She gave herself until the count of ten to bask in the refreshing chill before she pulled her head out and looked at herself in the mirror, looking like a waterlogged chihuahua.

Rio reached blindly for the towel that hung perpetually on the back of her bathroom door and made quick work of wiping herself down. She untied her hair and tried what little had gotten wet, brushed out the knots that her ponytail and sweat had left her with, and retied it into a messy bun, letting a few loose tendrils hang down in front of her face, like she didn’t have a care in the world.

She made a pit stop in her bedroom to refresh her spray-on deodorant (not bothering to change the slightly sweaty shirt because she was about to go on an hour-long walk anyway) and stretched out her muscles before she headed back toward the front door once again.

On her way out for the second time that day, Rio stopped by the mirror her mami had hung there when she first moved in (“Everyone needs a good hall mirror, mi sol.”) and did a once-over before she left.

Why? She did not know.

(Deep down, she knew.)

She left the apartment the second time with five minutes to spare.

She only needed three to make it to the front door of the hospital.

Agatha and Lilia were already sitting there, waiting for her.

“Wow, you guys are early, huh?” Rio said, approaching casually, with her hands in her pocket to keep them from shaking. “How long have you been out here?”

“About ninety seconds,” Lilia said, standing up to press a kiss to Rio’s cheek in greeting. “We were almost earlier, but I saw a couple of folks I knew and got caught up talking to them.”

“You know everybody in this town,” Rio laughed.

“Leaving the house with her involves a lot of standing around while she asks after everybody they know,” Agatha said, rolling her eyes, but there was affection there – Rio could see it, clearly. She wondered if Agatha wouldn’t miss Lilia when she was gone. She wondered if she wouldn’t miss Rio, too.

Rio would certainly miss Agatha.

She swallowed that thought down like saliva and offered Agatha an easy smile. “I always thought the same thing,” she admitted, “when I was a little kid and she was babysitting me. Couldn’t go ten feet without somebody calling out to talk to her.”

“You’re so dramatic,” Lilia huffed. It’s was two hundred feet, minimum.” It was her turn to roll her eyes now. “Anyway, here you go.” She handed a twenty to Rio. “You girls buy yourselves some ice cream or something from the vendors in the plaza. I’m heading out toward the beach to get a little sun in. Feels like forever since I was down there.”

Lilia had loved the beach for as long as Rio had known her. She used to love going over to Lilia’s on days when her mami had school and her papi was out on his fishing boat. They always ended up at the beach, even when the winter chill made it too cold to go into the water. The sun was still high in the sky. Lilia would lay out on a towel and Rio would make sand castles as she watched the waves roll in and shark fins appear in the distance.

She’d think about her father’s mermaid stories. She’d wonder if there were any mermaids out there, watching her, or if they were told stories about little girls with legs instead of tails, with feet instead of fins. Did they believe in her the way she believed in them?

She would make up little make-believe scenarios of herself being washed out to sea and saved by a mermaid the way her father had been, all those years ago. Then Lilia would inevitably call out to her that it was time to go home, so she’d pack up all of her beach toys in her bag, take her Aunt Lilia’s hand, and beg her to stop for ice cream on the way home.

“You know I make my own money now, right?” Rio snorted, taking the money that Lilia offered her anyway. “I can buy my own ice cream. I can even treat Agatha.”

“You’re already helping her out pro bono,” Lilia pointed out. “Let me at least buy you a cone on your last day. Make sure it’s two scoops, too.”

“Two?” Rio gasped, feeling genuinely psyched. Aunt Lilia had only ever let her get one scoop because her mami would be upset if she had too much sugar.

“You’re old enough now, I think,” Lilia said. “Thirty this year, my big girl.” She pinched Rio’s cheek, eliciting a flush as Rio side-eyed Agatha, who was smirking at her.

“Aunt Lilia…” Rio grumbled, pulling back.

“Don’t make me take that money back,” Lilia warned. “I will.”

“No, you won’t,” Rio said, pressing a kiss to Lilia’s nose, then cackling as she back away from Lilia’s batting hands.

“Be good,” Lilia said to Agatha. “Don’t push yourself.”

“I’m always good,” Agatha huffed, her face flushing slightly. “Didn’t you say that’s what my name means?”

“Mmhmm,” Lilia said. “I’ll be on the beach if you need me. Enjoy the soundcheck, girls!” She turned, waving at them over her shoulder as she made her way in the direction of the shore.

Rio tucked the money into her pocket and turned toward Agatha. “You ready to walk?” she asked.

“I’ve been walking just fine for the last couple of days,” Agatha huffed. “I should be fine.”

“Okay,” Rio said, putting her hands up in surrender. “I get it. This is probably starting to get annoying, seeing my dumb face every day, huh?”

“That’s not the part I have the issue with,” Agatha said, casually, as she started toward the sidewalk.

Rio felt the blush creep up from her neck at that as she followed, easily catching up. Agatha’s gait was better now; she took perfectly even steps as they made their way up the sidewalk. She was just a little bit slower than Rio, but Rio matched her pace as they got closer to the plaza.

People were already milling about. It was a sunny Thursday afternoon, typical for the summer months. There were kids chasing each other and giggling, their eyes wide at all the rides that were still in the process of being put up, probably anticipating standing in line for them the next day. Their parents stood around with other adults, chatting amongst themselves with drinks in their hands. Somebody was grilling hot dogs and corn on the cob, selling them for cheap, while tables and chairs were set up by the stage. There was a small dance floor where a few couples were dancing like they were in junior high. Little kids ran around them, as well.

Rio watched Agatha’s eyes widen as she took everything in. She probably hadn’t seen that many people in one place in the entire week she’d been on the island. It wasn’t like this often, to be fair. Most people had jobs to go to, and most of the kids either stayed in the air conditioning during the summer or lounged by pools or swam in the ocean.

It was unusual to see so many of the island’s residents – and even a few tourists – congregated together like this.

That was why Rio liked it when the fair came through.

She liked being surrounded by people sometimes.

The deeper in the plaza they went, the more crowded it became. Rio instinctively reached for Agatha’s hand, latching on and tugging slightly to pull her closer. “Wouldn’t want to get separated,” she said, by way of explanation, when Agatha turned to look at her with wide eyes. Rio flashed her a crooked grin as they continued through the crowd toward the stage.

Alice was fiddling with the microphone, trying to get it down to her level, but it ended up at her forehead every time. She frowned at it, trying to twist the stand and bring it down further, but she had no luck.

Then she spotted Rio.

“Rio!” she shouted into the mic, which was apparently on already. Rio’s name reverberated through the speakers, which echoed.

This is what they had soundchecks for.

“Rio!” Alice said, stepping away from the mic. “Get up here and help me! The mic isn’t cooperating!” She pouted like a petulant child, and Rio rolled her eyes.

“I’ll be right back,” she told Agatha, squeezing her hand before letting go and climbing onto the stage. It was only a foot and a half tall, thankfully, so it took little more effort that raising her knee a little higher than usual. “Stay there!” Rio called to Agatha, pointing at her.

Agatha gave her a thumbs up and Rio snorted, turning back to Alice.

“Why do you always have such issues with microphones?” she asked. “Didn’t you grow up around them or something?”

“Fuck off,” Alice huffed. “This one is literally being mean to me.”

“Yeah, yeah,” Rio snorted. “Everyone and everything is mean to Alice Wu-Gulliver.”

“Yes! Exactly!” Alice said, simply. “I’m glad you finally understand. Now fix it. Please.”

Rio rolled her eyes and twisted the mic stand, grunting a bit as she pushed the stubborn thing down to about Alice’s height, just below where her chin should be. She tightened it back up and stepped away.

“It’s too low now,” Alice complained, looking at Rio.

Rio gave her a soft glare and reached for the microphone, simply angling it up a bit so that the grille was pointed toward her lips.

“Happy?” she asked.

“Yes,” Alice said, smiling brightly. “Thanks, Ri Ri!” She wrapped her arms around Rio’s shoulders – or tried to, anyway. In reality, her short stature had her arms actually wrapping themselves around Rio’s arms, trapping them against her sides as Alice gave her an exaggerated kiss on the cheek, which Rio groaned as she attempted to moved away from her, making a disgusted face that elicited a few chuckles from the crowd, especially from the children.

“Why?” Rio huffed, pushing her away.

Alice pouted at her. “Why don’t you love me?” she asked.

“Quit it,” Rio snorted. Alice continued to pout at her, and Rio rolled her eyes as she pulled her back in for a hug and a kiss at the crown of her head. “Happy?” she asked for the second time in as many minutes.

“We shall marry at sunset,” Alice said, teasingly.

Rio shoved her away while she snickered. “Get on with your soundcheck,” she said. “People are waiting to dance.”

“They’ve been dancing for, like, half an hour,” Alice said. “With no music. They’re all kooks.”

“Is that how you talk about your fans?” Rio tutted. “For shame, Alice.”

“Fuck off,” Alice snorted. Then she lowered her voice. “So,” she said, eyeing Agatha, who was still waiting for Rio to return as her eyes roamed over the plaza, “still not a date?”

Rio’s eyes widened and she tugged her further away from the mic, in case it was still hot. It wasn’t. She couldn’t hear their words echoing around them anymore, thankfully.

Still.

“It’s not,” she insisted.

“Please!” Alice scoffed. “You were holding hands!”

“Will you please be quiet!” she huffed, glancing toward Agatha, who was still in the same spot where Rio had left her but was talking to a woman who’d approached her. Rio vaguely recognized her. There was a toddler hanging off her arm that she barely seemed to notice. Agatha smiled at the small child, giving her a tiny wave.

The child was giving her an odd look, her eyes wide with wonder as if Agatha were some kind of unicorn or other mythical creature.

Rio understood that.

“Hellooooo?” Alice said, snapping her fingers in front of her face. “Earth to Rio?” Rio blinked, turning back to Alice, who had a smug smirk on her face. “You’ve got it bad, huh?”

“Shut up,” Rio huffed. “She’s my patient, remember? I don’t date patients.”

“But you want to date this one, don’t you?” Alice teased. “Besides, she’s only your patient for, like,” she looked at her watch, “fifty-two more minutes. And you were holding hands with her, like, a second ago!”

“So that she wouldn’t get lost in the crowd!” Rio whisper-screamed. “That’s all!”

“Mmhmm,” Alice said, nodding sagely. “I’m sure.”

Rio shoved her again. “Shut up,” she grumbled. “Get on with your stupid soundcheck.”

“Stupid!” Alice mock-gasped. “That’s it! I want my friendship bracelet back!”

“Oh, I threw that out years ago,” Rio said, walking away from her as she hopped down from the stage.

“What?!” Alice squeaked. “Rio!”

Alice’s voice actually sounded hurt now, like she was close to tears. Rio rolled her eyes and reached for the carabiner hidden under the hem of her t-shirt, pulling up the retractable part for Alice to see as she turned her body slightly, raising one eyebrow. At the end of the chain was the bracelet that Alice had made for her the summer after they graduated high school, right before Rio was about to leave for college in Hawaii.

“This is in case anybody thinks they can replace me as your best friend.”

“You are weirdly possessive.”

“You belong to me, Rio. Forever and ever.”

“Freak.”

“You love me.”

“God only knows why.”

The second she saw it, Alice’s face split into a wide grin. She made a heart with her hands as Rio snorted out a laugh and let the bracelet fall back to her waist, tucking it safely back under her shirt.

Rio approached Agatha again, smiling at the small group of women with children who had inexplicably gathered around her, all talking to Agatha about the difficulties they were having with their small children. Meanwhile, all the small children in the group were staring at her like she was glowing with some kind of fairy dust.

Rio joined the conversation just as one woman said, “I don’t know what I was thinking having twins at my age! As a single mother, too! I just don’t know how to handle them!” She had two little girls, no older than three, tugging insistently at her hand.

Rio frowned. “Hey,” she said, “let’s not say that in front of the twins, huh? That’s fu—ahem, mean.” She smiled kindly at the little girls, who both had big green eyes and crooked brunette pigtails. She crouched down. “You’re not giving your mama any trouble, are you?”

They shook their heads in tandem, only of them giving Rio a shy little smile with her hands behind her back.

“You better not,” Rio said, warningly. “Because I know Santa Claus. And he doesn’t like it when children don’t listen to their mamas. You’re not gonna get very good toys if you keep it up, and Christmas is gonna be here before you know it!”

Her eyes widened further and she pouted.

“Just be good, okay? Can you do that for me?” The little girls nodded. Rio held up her hand for high-fives. They both slapped her hand with their little ones. “Awesome!” she said, standing up. She glared at their mother as she took Agatha’s hand and led them away from the crowd of moms.

“What was that all about?” Agatha asked as Rio led her in the direction of the ice cream shop. “You know Santa?” It sounded almost genuine, as if she believed Santa was an actual person.

“Oh,” Rio said, flushing, “yeah. Sorta. I do this character every Christmas called ‘The Christmas Witch’ who is meant to be Santa Claus’s long-lost sister. Basically, I just visit the kids on Christmas morning and throw presents at them. Some of them chase me. It’s a lot of fun.”

Agatha just blinked at her. She looked like she had so many questions, but she didn’t know how to ask them.

Rio’s blushed deepened. “I know,” she said. “It’s a little ridiculous, but the kids love it, and we don’t really have anybody volunteering to play Santa Claus, so I’m the best we’ve got.”

“So you dress up,” Agatha said, slowly, as they entered the ice cream shop, “and pretend to be…a witch?”

Rio shrugged. “Basically,” she said. “It’s a character I created while I was in high school, when the last guy who played Santa,” she glanced around, making sure no tiny ears were around, “died. They needed somebody to play a part for Christmas, and I didn’t want to put on a huge belly, so I made up the Christmas Witch persona. It’s just a white wig, some makeup, and a red witch outfit. No big deal.”

“That is…oddly sweet,” Agatha decided. “To do for the kids, I mean.”

“I’ve never had an issue with kids,” Rio said, shrugging one shoulder as she perused the ice cream flavors as if she wasn’t going to get the same thing she always got. “They’re pretty cool. Kinda wish I had siblings or cousins growing up.”

“You didn’t?” Agatha asked. She was also looking at the ice cream.

“I mean…technically I did,” Rio said. “But they lived on other islands, so I rarely saw them. And they were all older than me. My cousins, I mean. I never had any siblings. Mami said they tried, but it wasn’t meant to be or whatever.” She shrugged. “What’s your flavor?”

“My flavor?” Agatha asked, furrowing her brow.

“Yeah?” Rio snorted. “Which flavors are you gonna get?”

“Oh.” Agatha’s cheeks went pink. “Right. Um…I like mint chocolate,” she said. “Lilia bought me a mint chocolate shake the first day we went out together, after we went shopping. It was so good.”

Rio’s nose wrinkled automatically, but she fixed her face so as not to be rude. Everybody had different tastes, after all.

“Okay, so toothpaste flavored,” she said. “What else?”

“Else?” Agatha asked, confused. “What do you mean?”

“You get two scoops,” Rio reminded her. “The first one is mint chocolate chip,” she pointed to the flavor in the display case. “What’s the other?”

“Mint chocolate chip?” Agatha said, as if it were obvious.

“You can’t get two of the same flavor,” Rio said, “it’s illegal.”

Agatha pouted. “But it’s the only flavor I like,” she said. Rio could swear she saw tears in the other woman’s eyes and she immediately started to panic.

“Whoa,” she said. “I’m just kidding! You can get whatever flavor you want. Mint chocolate chip isn’t my cup of tea, but if you like it, get two scoops. Hell, get three if you want! On Lilia.” She held up the twenty.

Agatha rolled her eyes, but there was a tiny grin on her face as she turned to look at the flavors again. Her eyes widened and zeroed in on something in the display. Rio tried to follow her eyes, but the flavor she was looking at…no…surely not that one. Rio must have been imagining things.

“Would you like to try any flavors while you wait?” a lanky teen boy asked, holding up a couple of the bright pink tester spoons in his hand.

“Yes,” Agatha said. “Could I try this one?”

There was no mistaking it now: Agatha pointed directly at the oyster-flavored ice cream. It looked like just plain vanilla for the most part, but there was a large, plastic oyster shell sticking out of it to differentiate it from the actual vanilla.

Rio knew for a fact that it didn’t always used to be there.

She shuddered at the memory.

The teen didn’t even give her a weird look as he dipped the pink spoon into the oyster flavor, handing it over to Agatha with a friendly smile. She mirrored the smile as she took the spoon, mouthing a silent ‘thank you’ as she stuck the ice cream right into her mouth.

Rio ignored her gag reflex as she watched Agatha taste the white ice cream. Agatha smacked her lips a couple of times, humming to herself, then her eyes widened and a tiny grin appeared on her lips.

“That’s so good!” she said. “I think I might get one scoop of this, as well!”

She tossed the tester spoon in the recycling bin and smiled at Rio, who tried to force a smile back at her.

“It’s really that good, huh?” Rio asked, shakily.

Agatha nodded. “What, you never tried it?” Agatha asked.

“I did,” Rio said. “Once.” She swallowed the bile threatening to rise in her throat. “Lilia likes that flavor. She offered me a lick once. I thought it was vanilla.” She tried to suppress a gag.

“I’m guessing you didn’t like it,” Agatha asked, obviously not oblivious to her poorly-disguised disgust.

“I low-key have something akin to ARFID,” Rio said. “Certain flavors just…don’t mesh well with my taste buds.” She took a deep breath. “Oyster is one of those flavors.”

“Too bad. I think it tastes great,” Agatha said, shrugging.

“My mom thought so, too,” Rio said, grimacing. “It’s just not my thing, I guess.”

“What is your thing?” Agatha asked.

“Mountain Dew Dorito,” Rio said. “It’s the best flavor here.”

“You’re literally the only one that gets it, freak,” a voice behind the counter said.

Rio turned to glare in the direction of one of her old classmates, Scott, as he gave her a wide, goofy grin. She flipped him off, and he clutched a set of invisible pearls at his chest.

“Can you believe this?” he asked Agatha. “The audacity.”

Rio rolled her eyes, but Agatha snorted, shaking her head.

“I take it you know each other?” she asked.

“She only cheated off of me for the entirety of our AP Calc class senior year,” Scott said.

“I did not!” Rio looked at Agatha. “I didn’t,” she insisted.

“I believe you,” Agatha said, simply.

“You shouldn’t,” Scott said. “Because she did totally copy me.”

“I did not!” Rio argued.

“Then how did we happen to have all the same answers?”

“Because we were in the same class?” Rio huffed. “We didn’t even sit near each other, Scott. You were always on the other side of the room from me!”

“A likely story,” Scott said, narrowing his eyes suspiciously at her. He looked at Agatha, his face shifting into that big goofy grin once again. “Anyway, what can I get for ya?”

“I’d like to try whatever Rio’s favorite flavor is,” Agatha said, “before I make my final decision.”

“Are you sure about that?” Scott asked, already grabbing a new tester spoon. “I wasn’t lying when I said that she was the only one that ordered it. Well, her and a few local teenage boys and neckbeards.”

“Screw you, Lang,” Rio huffed. “It’s a good flavor.”

“I’m sure,” Agatha said.

Scott shrugged and dipped the spoon into the Mountain Dew Dorito ice cream, making sure he got a decent amount before handing it over to Agatha with a tiny salute.

“Good luck,” he said. “May God have mercy on you.”

“You’re so dramatic,” Rio huffed.

“I have good reason to be,” he retorted, watching as Agatha stuffed the whole bite of ice cream into her mouth.

She chewed thoughtfully as she had done for the oyster-flavored ice cream. Her face quickly shifted from thoughtful to…Rio didn’t even know what to call the expression Agatha was making. It wasn’t exactly disgust, but she could tell that Agatha wasn’t pleased by what she was tasting.

She looked at Rio. “This is your favorite flavor?” she asked.

“Yes,” Rio chirped.

“What is wrong with you?”

Rio frowned deeply. “You don’t like it?” she asked.

“That is an understatement,” Agatha admitted. “No offense, but…ew.”

She tossed the spoon and looked at Scott. “I’ll have one scoop of mint chocolate chip and one of oyster, please.”

“Coming right up!” Scott said, cheerily. He gave Rio a knowing smirk as he reached for the scoop. “Cup or cone?” he asked.

“Oh,” Agatha said, her eyes going a little wide as she looked back at Rio, blinking as though she didn’t know what the answer to that question could possibly be.

“I’d go for the cone,” Rio advised. “They’re pretty good here.”

Agatha nodded, turning back to Scott. “I’ll have a cone,” she said. “Please.”

Scott grabbed a cone then dipped the ice cream scooper into a bowl of warm water and scooped the mint chocolate chip ice cream into the cone. He did the same with the oyster scoop, creating a visually perfect double-scoop cone.

“Looks like you found your calling, Lang,” Rio said, grinning. “What did you need all that AP Calc for anyway?”

He ignored her. “Any toppings, Miss?” He asked.

“Um…” Agatha eyed the toppings on the shelf above the ice cream. “That,” she said, pointing to the rainbow sprinkles.

“Good choice,” Scott said, using a plastic spoon to shake coat the double-scoop onto the ice cream. “Rainbow sprinkles are always a win.” He gave her another big smile as he handed the cone over. “Here you go. Enjoy!”

“Thanks,” Agatha said, taking a somewhat tentative lick of her ice cream. She smacked her lips for a moment before her eyes widened and the corners of her lips turned up. “That’s very good,” she said.

“Thank you kindly!” he said, chipperly, his face falling as he turned back to Vidal. “Your usual?” he asked.

“Yes, please,” Rio said, making her voice sound sweet. “With the crunchies and extra cherries on top.”

“There’s something wrong with you,” Scott said. “Psychologically.”

Rio gave him her widest, most unhinged smile and nodded. Scott shivered and dipped the scooper into the Mountain Dew Dorito, wrinkling his nose as he did so. Then he scooped the coconut ice cream on top.

“Here’s your abomination,” Scott said, after he’d coated it in crunchies and stuck a few maraschino cherries on top. He handed the cone to Rio, who took it with no small amount of glee, immediately licking off a cherry.

“Thank you!” she sing-songed, handing over the twenty. “Keep the change, Lang! You look like you need it.”

“I don’t need your charity, Vidal,” Scott huffed.

“Can I have it?” the gangly teen boy behind him asked.

Scott sighed, handing him the two dollars in change. “Here you go, Peter,” he said as Rio tugged Agatha out of the ice cream shop.

When they made it back to the plaza, Alice was midway through a performance of one of her mother’s most famous songs: The Witches’ Road. It was a cover of an old folk song, but Lorna had put her own spin on it decades ago and made it a hit. There were people on the dance floor jamming out to it, a couple of teen boys being goofs and playing air guitar and drums while women who would have been teenage girls at the time it came out were singing along at the top of their lungs, nearly drowning out Alice and her bandmates.

Rio guided Agatha over to a table to sit and watch as they ate their ice cream.

“I thought we were supposed to be walking,” Agatha said, as she took a seat next to Rio on the bench of a picnic table. She licked her ice cream, humming happily at the strange flavor of her oyster ice cream. Rio tried not to show her disgust. If Agatha liked it, that was none of her business.

“We have plenty of time to walk,” Rio said. “I promised Alice I’d watch her soundcheck and I’ve already missed most of it.” She took a lick of her own ice cream, eating another cherry. They were nice and tart – her favorite.

There was a smattering of applause as Alice finished her song. She took a bow and gestured to her bandmates, who all bowed as well, waving as the crowd cheered for them.

“Thank you, thank you!” Alice said. “Alright, I’m gonna let these guys go spend time with their friends and families and relax for the remainder of the night. You did great, guys!” she said, smiling at her bandmates. “I’ll see you all tomorrow!” She turned back to the crowd. “We are Coven, and we will be performing tomorrow afternoon from four to six at El Festival de Isla Sirena. After that, I will be DJing from six-thirty until ten. This will be our daily schedule for all three nights of the festival. Feel free to come out and support us and your local businesses, who will have vendor booths open for the duration of the festival. Some of them have opened a day early, so go on and give them your business.”

She clapped, motioning toward the few vendors who were already selling their wares.

“Amazing,” Alice said. “Now, for the last bit of our soundcheck, I’m going to play a few songs from my DJ booth just to make sure all the equipment is working. I have a whole list of requested songs to test it out on, so thank you for that. I, unfortunately, will only play a few tonight, but come back tomorrow and you may just hear the song you requested if you don’t hear it right now.” Another smattering of applause and cheers. “Awesome!” Alice said. “Thank you all!”

She stepped away from the mic and inserted herself into her DJ booth, putting on her headphones and starting up her computer. She pushed a few buttons, pulled and pushed some levers, and twisted a few dials as her switchboard came to life, a rainbow of colors glowing around her.

Rio watched in amusement as a few young children stopped running just to stare at the bright flashing colors that surrounded Alice as she spun a few records, techno music filling the air.

Then ‘Let it Go’ started playing and they lost their fucking minds.

Rio snickered as a bunch of kids swarmed the dance floor and more or less began to mosh, their little bodies bouncing around as parents pulled out their phones to take pictures and videos. Alice laughed as she grabbed the mic and started leading a sing-along. The little kids all began to scream sing with her, jumping up and down with their little arms waving around wildly.

“Wow,” Agatha said. “They are very intense.”

“Yep,” Rio said. “You must be used to this kind of thing, though, right? Being a teacher and all.”

“I teach older kids,” Agatha said. “Teenagers.”

“High school?” Rio asked.

“Yes,” Agatha confirmed. “High School.”

She licked her melting ice cream cone, her brows knotting together as it began to drip over her fingers. Rio snorted and handed her a couple of napkins. Agatha thanked her, wiping up the ice cream in frustration.

“This is so messy,” she said. “How do people deal with this?”

“You could have gotten a cup,” Rio pointed out, licking her ice cream from the back of her hand. “It comes with a spoon.”

“You told me to get the cone!” Agatha complained.

“No, I said that they had really good cones there,” Rio retorted.

“Same thing,” Agatha huffed. She continued to lick around the ice cream cone, practically slurping as it continued to melt.

Rio watched her in amusement and fascination as she struggled to keep up. Before long, the top scoop of oyster ice cream was gone and Agatha was fighting a losing battle with the mint chocolate chip. She let out little huffs and growls of frustration every once in a while, and Rio polished off her own cone as she watched, letting out a soft belch when she was finished.

Agatha glared at her, sticking out her bottom lip in a pout. Rio swallowed thickly as she thought about what she might like to do to that bottom lip.

She looked at the time on her phone. It was a quarter to four.

She cleared her throat and got up from the table. “Let me go get you some water,” she said, “to rinse that off.”

Before Agatha could say anything, Rio was off like a shot, leaving her confused.

Chapter End Notes

don't run from your feelings, Rio!

Chapter 11

Chapter Summary

*in tour guide voice* and to your left, you'll see our title song

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Chapter Eleven

Agatha didn’t like the feeling of melting ice cream between her fingers. She tried her best to clean up with the napkins, but that didn’t help much. Pieces of the napkins tore away and stuck to her fingers, making it even worse. She wrinkled her nose at her hands, wishing that Rio would hurry up and get back with the water that she’d promised to get.

She was acting so strangely in the last few minutes. She had just watched as Agatha struggled with her melting ice cream, then she’d run off while Agatha had made a mess of her hands. She’d even looked terrified by something Agatha either couldn’t see or…it was her.

But that was ridiculous. Why would Rio be scared of her? She hadn’t done anything that could have warranted such fear, had she? Rio had run like she was on fire. Her face had been flushed and her eyes wide, but not really with fear, but with…

Agatha felt her own face flush as the realization hit.

Did Rio…like her?

She certainly liked Rio. She’d known it from the first moment they met.

Okay, maybe not from the first moment. Agatha had been half-drowned and disoriented, terrified and suddenly surrounded by the very beings she’d been warned not to interact with for the vast majority of her life.

But the second time she awoke on land, when she was wearing Rio’s hoodie and had a blanket over her bare lower half, she had looked into those gorgeous brown eyes and felt the flutters in her stomach. The stupid flutters that never really stopped when she was in Rio’s presence.

In fact, those flutters got stronger in Rio’s presence, if anything.

She hadn’t wanted to say anything to Lilia about the flutters, but the older woman was annoyingly observant. She had noticed the way Agatha looked at Rio since the first time she saw them in the same room together. She would nudge Agatha every time she dropped her off or picked her up from physical therapy. She never said anything explicitly about the way Agatha looked or acted or blushed around Rio, though, so Agatha hadn’t either.

She also never said anything about the way Rio looked at Agatha, either.

Agatha had noticed the way Rio’s eyes sometimes flitted down to her lips, but she’d thought that was just because of…well, honestly, Agatha couldn’t remember any of the excuses that she’d made for why Rio was looking at her lips. She couldn’t think of anything right now. She’d just known that she made them and that they’d seemed logical at the time because there was no way that she and Rio could be together. Rio was a human, born and raised on land. And, while Agatha had been born on land, she was raised in the water. She had a life in the water, waiting for her.

She had students who would expect her back to teaching on Monday morning. She had Jen, who was surely wondering where she’d gotten off to.

She had Scratchy…

Her poor friend must have been terrified. He had watched Agatha get tangled up in the net and hadn’t been able to do anything about it but watch her get pulled out of sight. She wondered if he was still out there, looking for her, panicking…

She had to get back to him. Now was not the time to start a relationship with a human, no matter how much her stomach fluttered and her face flushed and her heart leapt and…

Poseidon, Agatha felt pathetic. To be fair, though, she had never had such a reaction to anybody like this before. She was no virgin by any stretch of the imagination, but feelings weren’t something that Agatha dealt in very often. She didn’t feel this way about anybody. Not even when she was an adolescent.

It was embarrassing.

And it was even worse that it was a human because she knew that relationship could end in nothing more than heartbreak.

Still, she couldn’t help the way her heart skipped when Rio returned with a bottle of water and more napkins, offering them to Agatha as she took her seat back at the table.

“What do I do with this?” Agatha asked. Her fingertips were sticky and it felt uncomfortable to hold the bottle and napkins with sticky fingers. She wrinkled her nose.

“Right,” Rio said, taking the bottle back. “Sorry, I should have considered that.” She twisted off the cap of the bottle. “Hold out your hands,” she said. “Cup them a little bit.”

Agatha did as she was told, though she didn’t know the exact meaning of what Rio was telling her to do. She made her best guess and was apparently correct as Rio poured the water directly into her hands.
