The water gathered in the rounded shape she’d made them into, a tiny bit of it leaking between her fingertips. Rio ignored that and plucked up a napkin, dipping it into the water, soaking it fully, then used it to start scrubbing at the melted bits of ice cream on Agatha’s hands, holding them with her free hand.

Her fingers were strong and warm as she worked diligently to get all of the sticky mess off of Agatha’s hands, dipping the napkin back into the water twice more before it started to dissolve.

Then she reached for another.

Agatha’s hands were pretty much clean by then, but she didn’t stop Rio from scrubbing what little was left in the webbing between her fingers. The majority of the water was also soaked up by the napkins, but neither of them seemed to notice or care until it was all gone.

Even after all that, Rio held Agatha’s hands in her own. Agatha found herself staring at their joined hands, was even shocked when her thumb started idly stroking one of the calluses on Rio’s middle finger, feeling the roughness that she’d become accustomed to as Rio helped her learn to walk.

She felt Rio shiver, but it took several seconds for her to pull away, clearing her throat.

“I’m just gonna toss these,” she said, standing up quickly and gathering up the piles of wet napkin. The garbage can was only a few feet away away, but Rio seemed to be taking her sweet time with it. She tossed the napkins and flexed her fingers, looking down at her hands for a few moments as Agatha watched, transfixed.

Then Rio returned to the table and offered Agatha a somewhat shy smile.

“So,” she said, “your PT is officially over.”

“What?” Agatha asked, blinking at her.

Rio showed her the screen of her phone. It was 4:02.

Her last session with Rio had already ended.

“We barely did any walking,” she protested. “All we did was get ice cream and sit.”

“You feel like you didn’t get your money’s worth?” Rio teased. “Even though you paid zero dollars?”

“That’s not excuse for you to not do your job,” Agatha pointed out.

Rio snorted at that, but then she shrugged and nodded. “That’s true,” she said. “So what? You wanna walk around a little bit more?” The music suddenly shifted behind her, from one song that Agatha didn’t know to another that seemed a bit faster, and Rio’s eyes widened as her smile spread across her face. Agatha’s heart did a stupid little flip at the sight of her gap. “Or how about a dance?” she said. “That should make up for the lack of walking.”

“A dance?” Agatha asked, her heart clenching as her cheeks went hot. “I…I don’t know how to dance.”

“You don’t need to know much,” Rio said, standing up once again. “Not for this song, anyway. It’s Irish.”

“I don’t know what that means,” Agatha said as Rio walked around the table, reaching for her hand.

“It’s fast-paced and it’s just the kind of song you bounce around to. There’s no real skill to it. It’ll be fun!”

“Uh…” Agatha stared at Rio’s offered hand, at the callouses on it.

“Come on,” she said. “Do you trust me?”

Agatha looked up into Rio’s eyes at the question. They were a little wider than usual, a little more intense, but no less warm. She took a deep breath and nodded.

“Of course,” she said, and it was the truth: she did trust Rio. Rio was kind and funny, and she had never let Agatha fall. Agatha couldn’t imagine it would be any different now. “Of course I do,” she said, finally slipping her hand into Rio’s and allowing herself to be tugged to her feet.

Rio started laughing as she tugged Agatha in the direction of the dance floor. Agatha struggled a bit to keep up, but then, before she knew it, she was bumping into Rio’s front as the other woman turned back toward her with a wide grin, her brown eyes crinkling at the corner. Agatha felt her mouth go dry as one of Rio’s hands landed on her waist while the other held Agatha’s left hand at about shoulder-level, their fingers tangling slightly.

“Is this okay?” Rio asked, her voice low as she leaned in closer to Agatha’s ear. Agatha shivered a little bit and swallowed thickly, nodding.

“It’s alright,” she said, feeling her cheeks go hot. Rio’s grin widened and her eyes flashed with something that Agatha couldn’t name as she took a quick step back, pulling Agatha along with her.

Agatha had no other option than to follow her lead.

Rio guided her around the dance floor, her steps quick and bouncy as she laughed and sang along to the voice floating through the speakers around them.

I met her in the summer

Down by the golden bay

Like flowers in the water

I can’t forget her face.

No wonder, she’s a stunner

And takes my breath away

Like flowers in the water

She dances in the rain.

Agatha found herself laughing at the goofy faces that Rio was making as they danced to the music that swelled around them. Well, Rio was doing most of the dancing while Agatha was following her lead and struggling not to step on her feet the entire time. Rio held her tight and close, her fingers squeezing Agatha’s as they bounced around the dance floor with the few other couples and more than a few children who were more or less spinning around in circles.

Agatha pressed her face into Rio’s shoulder to hide her laughter as Rio moved faster, singing a little bit off-key to the music, likely with the express goal to make Agatha laugh.

She felt Rio’s hand shift on her hip, moving to wrap around her waist as she picked up her speed. Agatha let out an involuntarily shriek as Rio practically picked her up, bouncing around with her even more and tugging Agatha’s hand to wrap around her shoulder. Agatha held on for dear life as Rio continued to spin them around the dance floor, just barely registering the claps and shouts around them as they spun.

Rio laughed as Agatha clung to her, her feet fumbling slightly until Rio fully picked her up, spinning them around in circles with Agatha holding onto her shoulders as she pressed her face against Rio’s shoulder, laughing hysterically. Rio continued to sing and laugh at the same time as the song started to wind down.

Just like the waves,

I wish that she’d come back around

And stay with me

In this old town.

I met her in the summer

Down by the golden bay

Like flowers in the water

I can’t forget her face.

No wonder, she’s a stunner

And takes my breath away

Like flowers in the water

She dances in the rain.

Rio placed her back on the ground after spinning her in the air for a bit, then took both of Agatha’s hands and started to turn in a circle, their arms extended, holding onto each other’s hands as they more or less ran around in the circle, laughing at one another as the song came to a close, shifting to another one.

“That was amazing!” Alice said into the microphone. “Everybody give it up for Rio and Agatha! Round of applause.”

Rio and Agatha stopped spinning around just to see the crowd of people who had gathered around them, clapping for them. Agatha felt her face flush hot as she waved at some of the people around them, including a few little girls who were bouncing on their toes as their eyes glittered up at her. She gave them waves too, and they cheered harder.

Agatha smiled at them.

She looked up at Rio, who was already smiling down at her.

They were still holding hands.

“Okay,” Alice was saying, “one last song to round out our soundcheck. Many of you will hopefully recognize this classic. If not, then I’m almost certain you’ll enjoy it, nonetheless. It’s a slower one, so grab a partner and just sway.”

When marimba rhythm starts to play

Dance with me, make me sway

Like a lazy ocean hugs the shore

Hold me close, sway me more

Rio flushed bright red for some reason, her hold on Agatha’s hand starting to loosen. Instinctively, Agatha tightened her hold on the other woman’s hand, and Rio’s eyes widened.

“You want to dance to this one?” she asked.

Agatha shrugged. “Why not?” she asked. “I like the rhythm.”

It was true. And whomever was singing the song had a pleasing voice that sent shivers down her spine. She found her hips started to move without her permission. There were more couples on the floor now, spinning around almost slowly as they moved to the flow of the song.

Rio swallowed thickly, but she didn’t resist as Agatha moved closer, trying to place herself in the same position as many of the dancers around them. Rio placed her hand back on Agatha’s hip and took her hand, much like she’d done the first time, only now their bodies were closer together and, when they began to move, they weren’t going quite as quickly as with the prior song.

Their faces were a little closer now, too, which made Agatha’s face feel very hot, but she didn’t want to move away.

Like a flower bending in the breeze,

Bend with me, sway with me

When we dance, you have a way with me

Sway with me, sway with me

Her heart was pounding in her chest as they started to move. Her feet moved more surely, keeping pace with Rio’s even as she did slip up and step on her toes from time to time. Rio’s hand clenched a little bit on her back, but she didn’t complain or move away. She just started to sing in her ear again.

“Other dancers may be on the floor

Dear, but my eyes will see only you

Only you have that magic technique

When we sway, I go weak.”

Her voice was smoother now as she sang, deeper. Agatha felt almost hypnotized by it as their bodies moved together. Her feet caught onto the rhythm, no longer tripping over Rio’s as they moved together. Her eyes closed as she pressed her face against Rio’s shoulder and felt Rio’s cheek resting against her head. Their fingers linked at their sides and Agatha felt her entire body relax against Rio’s as they just…swayed.

I can hear the sounds of violins

Long before it begins

Make me thrill as only you know how

Sway me smooth, sway me now

Agatha wondered, idly, why they didn’t have music in the Pod. Or, at least, why they didn’t have any such thing as music. She had asked Lilia a couple of nights earlier about this, but she couldn’t really come up with an answer. She had simply…shrugged, even as she danced around to the radio as she cooked them both dinner.

“Sirens have songs,” Lilia had said while they ate. “But our songs are laced with magic. I don’t think we’ve very capable of singing without putting somebody under a spell.” She had waggled her fingers at Agatha. “So I try to avoid it as much as possible.”

“What if I sang?” Agatha asked. “Do you think I could put a human under a spell just with my voice?”

“I can’t imagine so,” Lilia had admitted. “You were born human, too. Just being raised as a mermaid shouldn’t endow you with any specific powers like those of a Siren. You could certainly try, though.” She had chuckled at that as she took a sip of her wine.

Agatha didn’t really even know where to start with singing. It sounded different than speaking, obviously, but she didn’t know how to really go about it. Rio, however, seemed very skilled at it. It seemed to come almost as second nature to her as she swayed around with Agatha in her arms, lulling her into a sense of security as they danced, warm in Rio’s embrace.

The soothing rhythm of the song didn’t hurt, either.

Other dancers may be on the floor

Dear, but my eyes will see only you

Only you have that magic technique

When we sway, I go weak

Agatha pulled away slightly to look up into Rio’s eyes, which were half-lidded as though she were putting even herself into a trance. Their noses brushed as Agatha found herself leaning in. Her eyes flickered down to Rio’s lips, feeling drawn in by them even as they continued to sing.

“I can hear the sounds of violins

Long before it begins

Make me thrill as only you know how

Sway me smooth, sway me now.”

Rio leaned in closer, too. Agatha was certain that she could feel the rhythm of her heart pounding away in her chest as it was pressed directly to Rio’s. Her mouth and throat had both gone dry. She licked her lips in an attempt to…she didn’t even know what she was attempting to do. She had lost her train of thought as she stared into Rio’s eyes, captured by her gaze, which seemed to be almost glowing as their faces got closer, as Rio’s mouth continued to move seamlessly with the words of the song, as the skin of their lips brushed, just slightly and—

“Mind if I step in,” a voice said, breaking whatever spell it was that they were under. Rio stepped back immediately, her eyes going wide as she turned and saw Lilia standing behind them, smiling innocently at them like she hadn’t just very clearly interrupted something.

Rio cleared her throat and let go of Agatha’s hand, placing both of hers behind her body. “Sure,” she said. “No problem.” She cleared her throat as she stepped away from Agatha, making enough space for her to take Agatha’s hand and place the other on her waist.

Agatha could only allow herself to be swayed away from Rio as the other woman stood there, frowning at her in disappointment with her hands behind her back.

“Why did you do that?” Agatha hissed at Lilia as she danced her closer and closer to the edge of the dance floor, leaving Rio alone in the middle of it.

“Do what?” Lilia retorted, acting dumb. Agatha knew she had to be acting because there was no way Lilia was that blind. She knew exactly what she had just interrupted.

Agatha didn’t really know how to answer that question, however, so she just pressed her lips together, stubbornly.

“Exactly,” Lilia said. “If you’re going to play with my niece’s heart like that, you better be damn sure that you mean it.”

“I wasn’t—”

“Agatha,” Lilia said, firmly. “You are going home soon, are you not?” Agatha didn’t answer, avoiding Lilia’s gaze. “Or have you decided to stay?”

“I don’t know,” Agatha admitted as the music continued over their heads. “I…I can’t stay…can I?”

She looked over Lilia’s shoulder at Rio, who had moved to sit on the stage by Alice’s booth. Alice was leaning over it, hand over the mic to talk to her without broadcasting their conversation to the whole plaza. Rio just shrugged, but she kept sparing Lilia and Agatha forlorn looks that made Agatha’s chest ache.

If it were allowed, she would make it work with Rio. She would travel whenever she could outside the Pod to meet her, to be with her. If only she could tell her…

“How did that one mermaid fall in love with a human?” she asked.

“Hmm?” Lilia asked, pulling back a little bit to look her more closely in the eye.

“You said that the reason you left the Pod is because you were trying to help a mermaid who had fallen in love and gotten impregnated by a human,” Agatha reminded her. “How did she…do that?”

“Do you really want me to explain the birds and the bees to you right now?” Lilia huffed.

“What do birds and bees have to do with this?” Agatha asked, her voice sounding a bit louder as the music came to its natural conclusion. A few people around them turned to look in their direction, but Agatha ignored them and lowered her volume. “I’m asking about how somebody got pregnant.”

“And I thought you would have already learned how that happens. Aren’t you a teacher?” Lilia hissed, dragging her off the dance floor.

“Yes, but how did that happen between a human and a mermaid?” Agatha asked. “We were always told—”

“Lies,” Lilia huffed. “You were told lies.”

“What?” Agatha asked, furrowing her brow. “What are you talking about?” She looked around, realizing that Lilia was dragging her in the direction of the shore. “Where are we going? What’s happening right now?” She tried to tug her fingers out of Lilia’s grasp, but the old woman was surprisingly strong for her multi-centennial age.

“I have something to show you,” Lilia said, glancing over her shoulder.

“What?” Agatha asked, still trying to resist. She looked back over her shoulder at the dance floor, where Rio was still looking in their direction, frowning. She wanted to slip out of Lilia’s grip so badly, to run back to Rio, to continue what they had so obviously been about to do before Lilia rudely interrupted…

“Stop resisting,” Lilia huffed as their shoes left the smooth sidewalk for the rough wooden ramp that went down into the sand. “You’re going to want to see this, trust me.”

Agatha turned back forward, frowning, but she allowed her steps to match pace with Lilia’s, quickening as they went down the ramp and stepped down onto the uneven sandy beach.

There were very few people on the beach, oddly enough. Agatha figured it was due to the soundcheck and the majority of the citizens on the island gathering in the plaza. But then she saw some people further down the beach, sitting on blankets and laughing together.

None of them were anywhere near the dock that Lilia was guiding her toward, however. They weren’t even looking in that direction, almost like there was some kind of force making them oblivious as she was dragged down the beach.

Agatha looked back at Lilia’s face, but the older woman was singularly focused on getting to wherever they were going. Agatha had no clue what she could possibly want her to—

“Jen?!” Agatha exclaimed when she saw who was sitting on the shore, a pair of deep brown legs that matched the rest of her pressed up to her bare chest and one of Lilia’s colorful shawls wrapped around her shoulders.

“Agatha!” Jen exclaimed. “Oh, thank Poseidon! I’ve been looking for you for days! What are you doing here?”

“Me?” Agatha huffed. “What are you doing here?”

Lilia finally let go of her hand, allowing Agatha to rush forward and drop to her knees in the sand right next to Jen’s body. She didn’t even try to fight it as Jen wrapped her arms around Agatha’s shoulders in an uncharacteristic hug. Agatha hugged her back, feeling her eyes well up with tears and her chest clench.

She hadn’t even realized just how much she’d missed the other woman until she saw her face again.

Agatha pulled back to look Jen in the eyes, cupping her cheeks with her hands. “I can’t believe you’re here!” she exclaimed. “How…how did you even know where to find me?”

“Your pet shark,” Jen said, pointing out to the water, where Agatha could clearly see a shark fin sticking out of the shallows. “I figured you were outside of the Pod and that you’d gotten yourself in some kind of trouble. I didn’t know that you were actually breaking laws, you dumbass!” She shoved Agatha into the sand. “What the fuck is wrong with you? Do you know how much trouble we’ll be in if any of the leaders figure out where we’ve been?”

“You didn’t have to come up on land, you know,” Agatha huffed, brushing sand off of herself.

“Like hell I didn’t!” Jen said. “How else was I going to drag your ass back home?”

“You can’t,” Agatha said. “I don’t have my—”

Jen held something up in her hand that Agatha immediately recognized.

“My necklace!” she gasped, reaching for it. “Where did you find this?”

“Scratchy had it stuck between his teeth,” Jen informed her, handing over the necklace.

“My baby,” Agatha said, grinning. “What a smart boy.”

She looked back out at the shore, grinning. Scratchy’s fin moved back and forth in the water, somewhat nervously, like he was pacing.

“Yes, he’s a genius. Can we go now? I’m freezing out here!”

“You can go back in the water, you know,” Agatha huffed. “You didn’t have to…how the fuck are you speaking right now?”

“What do you mean?” Jen asked. “I learned to talk the same as you when we were little.”

“But you…out of water…it took me days to figure out how to speak without water in my mouth!” Agatha said. “How did you learn in less than an hour?!”

“What, like it’s hard?” Jen huffed. “Also, I’ve been here longer than an hour.”

“I swear to Poseidon if you can already walk…” Agatha grumbled.

“Don’t you worry about that,” Jen sighed. “I can’t.” She looked down at her legs, which were long and lanky. “These things are so weird,” she said. “Who designed them? They’re also so boring and devoid of color, like for what?”

“They were made for function, Jennifer,” Lilia said, “not fashion.”

“Well, they’re not doing much of either,” Jen said. “Please.” She looked at Agatha. “Can we go?”

“I…uh…” Agatha looked down at the necklace in her hand. “I don’t…” Her heart suddenly felt heavy.

Lilia was making her a new one. She had promised that it would be done by Saturday morning, at the latest, which would have given them enough time to get Agatha home to the Pod before her class on Monday morning.

Hopefully. Agatha still wasn’t too sure how far the swim was to get to the Pod. She figured Lilia was correct in her estimation, however, considering she’d had to make that swim probably thousands of times back in her hay day.

But Agatha had planned for Sunday to be the day she returned. She had prepared herself for that.

Today was only Thursday. She had just completed her PT. She wasn’t quite ready to go back to the Pod just yet, for this particular journey to end. She wasn’t ready to say goodbye to Lilia.

Or to Rio.

“Can we wait a little?” she asked Jen.

“Wait?” Jen huffed. “For what?”

“There’s a fair tomorrow that I was excited to go to,” Agatha said, trying to find any excuse.

“A what?” Jen asked. “What the hell is a fair?”

“It’s hard to explain,” Agatha said, “but there’s going to be music and dancing and a lot of people selling things.”

“Music?” Jen asked, her eyes widening slightly. She looked between Agatha and Lilia. “They have music up here?”

“Yes!” Agatha said. “And the food up here is amazing! You should really try some before you leave, Jen! It’s warm and delicious and there are so many different flavors! Even the cold food is great! You’ve never had ice cream! They have some that tastes exactly like fresh oysters!”

“Wow,” Jen said, “you really like it up here, don’t you?”

Agatha paused, pressing her lips together as her cheeks warmed. “I do,” she confirmed. “I feel more welcome up here than I’ve felt my entire life in the Pod. I can see now why some decided to leave there for here.” She looked at Lilia. “I understand why you never went back.”

“Are you considering not going back?” Jen asked before Lilia could respond. There was a tinge of fear in her voice. “Are you going to stay up here? Abandon your students?”

“I don’t know,” Agatha admitted. “A few days ago, I would have said no. That would have been an absurd thought…”

“But now…?” Lilia asked.

Agatha turned to look at her again. “There is so much I still want to explore up here. And now I can walk and talk and dance…”

“Dance?” Jen asked. “When did you dance?”

“Just now,” Agatha said, thumbing over her shoulder. “It’s really not that hard. You kind of just bounce around on your toes or sway as you hold onto your partner.”

“You found a mate up here?!” Jen spluttered. “Agatha, that’s illegal!”

“I didn’t find a mate!” Agatha defended. “I just…found a dance partner.” Her face went red. “But what would it even matter if I found a mate, anyway? It’s not like I haven’t had mates before, right? And Rio is…wonderful. She doesn’t think my questions are stupid or that I’m too curious or anything like that. If anyway, she encourages my curiosity. So does Lilia!”

She felt Lilia’s hand land on her shoulder, warming her. She turned to look at the other woman, whose face wasn’t as happy or as soft as she had been expecting. Instead, it was grim.

“We should talk,” Lilia said. “At home, if you’re coming back. Jennifer,” she looked at Jen, “you can come, too, if you’d like.”

“How do you expect to get me there if I’m naked and unable to walk like the rest of the humans? Wouldn’t I look out of place?”

“Yes,” Lilia said. “You would. Luckily, I have a few tricks up my sleeve. I’ve called for reinforcements. They should be here any moment, if you’re interested.”

Jen looked between Agatha and Lilia, then shrugged. “Why not?” she sighed. “Nobody’s going to be looking for me.”

“Your parents would,” Agatha pointed out. “And your brothers.”

“Nah, they’d just think I was at work,” Jen said. “My students, however…” She shook her head. “I probably wouldn’t make it for work tomorrow anyway. I’ll stay for a day or two. Maybe I can show Agatha the way back so you don’t have to,” she said to Lilia.

“Sounds like a plan, doll,” Lilia said, smiling, though it didn’t quite meet her eyes. “Agatha, why don’t you go say hello to your little friend out there before he makes a whirlpool and draws too much attention to himself. I swear, if Rio sees a shark, she’s going to be out here before you can even blink twice.”

Agatha snorted, pushing herself up from the sand, kicking off her shoes and socks, and running out into the water.

“How did you learn how to do that?” Jen called after her. “Teach me!”

Agatha ignored her in favor of wading into the water. Scratchy’s fin made a line straight toward her, slicing through the water with a quickness that might have alarmed any other human. Agatha didn’t even blink as he bumped against her knees and circled around her while she laughed, stroking his scarred back.

“My boy!” she exclaimed. “My good little sharky boy!” She leaned down to pull his face closer to hers for a kiss on his snout. “What a smart boy you are! Leading Jen right to me! And bringing her my necklace, too! You’re a genius!”

Scratchy let out a strangled sound that sounded like music to Agatha’s ears. She had missed those strange sounds he made. They sounded like purrs to her ears, even though she knew that wasn’t possible. Agatha laid a few more kisses on his snout, stroking her hands all over his body for a few more moments as he swam around her.

“Okay, buddy,” she said, “I have to go back to shore, but I’ll come out and visit you again, okay? I love you, Scratchy. So much.” She kissed his snout once before turning and heading back to shore.

She could see that another figure had arrived in her absence, and she was helping Lilia dress Jen and get her into a wheelchair. There was something familiar about the figure, though Agatha couldn’t see the other woman’s face. The long, dark hair that had been twisted into a braid down her back was oddly familiar.

It wasn’t until she had turned back around to say something to Lilia that Agatha realized who the other woman was.

“Dr. Vidal?”

Chapter End Notes

show of hands, who guessed it?

Chapter 12

Chapter Summary

I wanna preface this by apologizing to Jen for what she's about to learn.

TW for child kidnapping(?) ig

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Chapter Twelve

Lilia shushed her the second the words were out of her mouth, urging Agatha to follow her as she wheeled Jen up the beach.

“Thank you for your help, Marisol,” she said. “We’ll talk later. I think Rio has spotted you.”

She motioned to Rio, who was walking down the sidewalk toward them, her head tilted in curiosity as she approached. Marisol shot Agatha a pleading look before painting on a smile and pointing it toward her daughter.

“Mami?” Rio said. “I thought you had a lot of work today. What are you doing here?”

“Hola, mijita,” Marisol greeted. “I was just helping Lilia with something. No big deal. How was the soundcheck?”

“It was good,” Rio said, glancing toward Agatha. They smiled shyly at each other. Agatha felt her heart swell with affection.

Marisol looked between them, smiling knowingly. “You don’t say…”

Rio’s cheeks reddened and she shot her mother a warning look, pouting at her. Marisol just snorted and embraced her child, pressing a kiss to her forehead, which she had to stand on tip-toes just to reach. Rio didn’t fight the embrace for even a second, which made something in Agatha’s chest ache. She couldn’t even remember the last time one of her mothers had held her like that.

Rio gave her mom an embarrassed smile and an eyeroll, but she didn’t try to squirm out of the embrace as her mother cupped her cheeks and squished her face. Not until she glanced in Agatha’s direction again, at least.

Then she must have murmured something about Agatha because suddenly Agatha was on the receiving end of a pair of wide, hazel eyes that looked her up and down very quickly before she stepped away from her daughter.

“Hey,” Rio said. “What were you doing on the beach with Lilia?”

“Oh, nothing,” Agatha said. “She just wanted to introduce me to…” She turned to motion to Jen, who suddenly was no longer there. Lilia was several feet away, walking away as she pushed her in the direction of her home. “Doesn’t matter,” Agatha said, clearing her throat as she turned back to Rio, whose eyes were locked on her. “Sorry we just abandoned you back there on the dance floor. Lilia can be kind of…”

“Kooky?” Rio finished for her, snorting.

“That’s your word,” Agatha said, holding up her hands, “not mine.”

“Yeah, she’s been that way my entire life,” Rio said. “Likely well before it, too.”

Very likely, Agatha thought to herself.

“Anyway,” Rio said, sticking her hands into her pockets and looking down at the toes of her sneakers, “I was wondering…” She trailed off, clearing her throat as her cheeks darkened.

“Yeah?” Agatha asked, feeling a bit nervous.

“Wouldyouliketogotothefairtomorrow?”

Agatha’s brows twisted and her nose wrinkled.

“What?” she asked.

“I know it’s probably unprofessional,” Rio rushed out, “but you’re technically not my patient anymore, and I really liked hanging out with you today, and I just thought that we might have a little more fun tomorrow when the fair is actually in full swing and we can –”

“Rio!” Agatha said, reaching out to place her hand on Rio’s shoulder, which caused any further words to die on her tongue as her eyes widened, nearly popping out of her skull. “Slow down. Please. I have no idea what you just said to me. Could you please repeat yourself?”

“Oh,” Rio said, clearing her throat. “Right. Yeah. Of course.” She took a deep breath in through her nose, then out through her mouth, shutting her eyes for a couple of moments before opening them again and locking them on Agatha with what looked like resolve. She cleared her throat for good measure. “Would you,” she said, more slowly this time, “like to go to the fair tomorrow? With me.” She bit her lip, nervously as she waited for Agatha’s response.

Agatha, for her part, suddenly felt just as nervous as Rio looked, like she was a teenager again, being asked by a cute classmate if she wanted to go together to get something to eat between classes. But this was different. Because Rio wasn’t just a cute classmate. Rio was a cute human. The cute human. The cute human who had taught her how to walk and had been so patient about it, too.

The cute human that Agatha would likely be leaving after the weekend was over.

Agatha’s chest ached at that thought, but she found herself unable to look away from Rio’s face, which was so full of hope as she waited for Agatha’s answer. Agatha felt those flutters in her stomach again. They were stronger this time as she was close enough to Rio to just lean in and…

She finally looked away, staring down at her own feet as she contemplated what her answer should be.

 Lilia’s words were echoing in her ear: “If you’re going to play with my niece’s heart like that…” Agatha didn’t want to play with her heart. She didn’t want to get Rio’s hopes up, only to disappear on her.

It wouldn’t be fair.

But when Agatha looked up again to tell her no, Rio’s eyes captured hers again with their depth. She was chewing on her bottom lip nervously, her cheeks pink and her face full of hope. Agatha couldn’t bring herself to say no.

“Of course,” she said, offering a soft smile. “I’d love to go to the fair with you.”

The smile that Rio gave her in response took Agatha’s breath away. It was wider than any other smile she’d seen from her before. The little gap between her front teeth was on full display. Her brown eyes were crinkly and adorable. Agatha felt those flutters in her stomach increasing to an almost painful degree. Her throat and mouth were suddenly very dry again.

“Okay,” Rio said, after what felt like a millennium of the two of them just staring at each other, smiling and blushing like idiots. “Um, I’ll see you tomorrow then.” She spun around and took a few steps away from Agatha before suddenly spinning back around before Agatha could even turn in the direction of Lilia’s house. “Oh! Um, should I pick you up at Lilia’s, or do you want to meet in the plaza?”

“Whatever you prefer,” Agatha replied. Then, thinking better. “I’ll meet you there, actually. I think Lilia also wants to check it out, so I’ll probably head over with her.”

Rio nodded, still giving her that impossibly wide grin. “Right,” she said. “Cool. Okay.”

Once again, she twisted her body to start walking away, but instead ended up doing a full three-sixty turn, ending up facing Agatha once again.

“What time do you want to meet?” she asked.

“Oh,” Agatha said, her face warm because, honestly, she had no idea.

Time worked differently in the Pod than it did on land. She had no idea what a ‘good’ time would be to meet for something like this.

“You pick,” Agatha said. “What would be a good time for you? You have the job, after all.”

“Oh,” Rio said, her face going even more pink. “Yeah. Um. Well, I have a couple appointments, but I should be ready to go around six?”

“Is that a question?” Agatha asked, teasingly, one eyebrow raised.

“Six,” Rio said, more decisively. “We’ll meet at six. By the…” Rio turned to look toward the plaza, “stage.” She turned back to Agatha. “That sound good to you?”

“It sounds great,” Agatha said, her voice soft and her chest aching just slightly. “I’ll see you then.”

“Yeah,” Rio said, looking a little dazed with her goofy little grin as she took a couple steps back toward the plaza. “See you.”

Agatha turned away, making her way toward Lilia’s house. She caught sight of Dr. Vidal standing nearby, out of sight of her daughter, and slowed her pace a little bit as the older woman studied her with narrowed eyes.

“Hello, Dr. Vidal,” Agatha said, coming to a stop a few feet away from the older woman. “Were you waiting for me?”

“Yes,” Dr. Vidal replied. “Yes, I was.”

There was a long moment of silence as the two women simply stared at one another. Agatha cleared her throat, suddenly nervous from the woman’s gaze on her. Rio looked strikingly like her mother: they had almost identical faces, except for the hazel eyes that were  more green than brown on the doctor, and the lack of a gap between the older woman’s front teeth. And the nose was a bit slimmer on Dr. Vidal.

Besides those few things, however, Rio could have been her mother’s twin. She was significantly taller than the other woman, too. She wondered if that had happened gradually or if, one day, she had just suddenly been taller.

Did she still see Rio as a small child in need of protection? Did she still see her as her baby? Was Agatha somebody she needed protection from?

There was a look of suspicion in Dr. Vidal’s eyes that made every cell in Agatha’s body stand on edge.

“What are your intentions with my daughter?” the doctor demanded, her arms crossing over her chest as she widened her stance.

Agatha’s gaze flew up to meet hers as her eyes widened.

“Excuse me?” she asked. “Wh-what do you mean?”

“I think you know exactly what I mean,” Dr. Vidal said. “What are you trying to do with my daughter? Are you playing with her heart? Because, if you are, I swear to Poseidon…”

“Poseidon?” Agatha echoed, her brows furrowing. She had not heard a single human mention Poseidon once since she’d been on land. As far as she knew, they didn’t even know of the sea god’s existence. “How do you know of…”

Then it dawned on her. It was so clear to her. So obvious. How she hadn’t seen it sooner was beyond Agatha. Especially when she’d shown up on the shore to help Jen just five minutes ago. Her eyes…so much lighter than Rio’s.

“You’re a mer—”

“Shh!” Dr. Vidal said, slapping a hand over her mouth and looking over her shoulder before she turned back to Agatha. “What is wrong with you?” she hissed. “Do you not have a brain between those ears?” She swatted Agatha on the side of the head. “Tonta!”

“Ouch!” Agatha complained. “What was that for?”

Dr. Vidal rolled her eyes and wrapped her fingers around Agatha’s wrist, tugging her in the direction of Lilia’s house. Agatha didn’t attempt to resist, mainly because for a woman so petite, Dr. Marisol Vidal was incredibly strong and partly because she was afraid of what else the woman might do to her.

Lilia had gotten Jen situated on the couch by the time Marisol burst into the house, holding Agatha by the wrist. She let go once they were past the threshold.

Agatha pouted as she started to massage where Marisol had been clutching her wrist in her vice-like grip. She frowned at Lilia, who just shook her head and patted her on the shoulder, motioning for her to sit next to Jen on the couch.

Jen was wearing a pair of hot pink scrubs and Lilia’s shawl over her shoulders still. She also had a strange thing on her head that looked like it was made of the same yarn that many of Lilia’s scarves were comprised of. It, too, was pink, with a little bit of orange and white mixed in, oddly enough.

“What’s going on?” Jen whispered to Agatha as she sat down.

“I have no clue,” Agatha retorted, “but I think Dr. Vidal might be a mermaid.”

“Of course I’m a mermaid!” Marisol huffed, throwing her arms up. “It’s obvious!” She turned to Lilia. “Where did you find this girl? I thought you said she was very smart.”

“She is,” Lilia said. “But, in her defense, it’s not common to assume that the humans walking around you are actually mermaids in disguise. And, well, technically dear, you’re not a mermaid. I took you from the surface when you were only a few years old like I did with them.”

“What?” Agatha and Jen gasped in unison.

“A few years old?” Agatha asked. “I thought you only took infants.”

“Marisol was a special case,” Lilia replied. “She wasn’t abandoned; she was orphaned.”

Agatha’s eyes widened as she turned her gaze to Marisol, who evaded her gaze. Her cheeks were pink and her head was lowered slightly. Lilia noticed, too. She reached out, stroking Marisol’s back and cupping the younger woman’s cheek.

“My girl,” she said. “You were so young. I should have never taken you like that. I should have brought you back to your island.”

Marisol shrugged. “There’s nothing to be done about it now,” she said. “It happened. And, because of it, I have the life that I have now. I have Rio.” She gave Lilia a soft smile. “I have you.” She leaned down, wrapping her arms around Lilia’s shoulders in a warm, tight hug. “Thank you for saving me.”

“Anytime, hun,” Lilia said before pulling away. “But you need to stop calling my girl stupid. She’s very bright, actually.”

“Yes, I am!” Agatha added. “I’m a teacher!”

“A teacher!” Marisol scoffed. “Do you teach your students to play with hearts?”

“I’m not playing with Rio’s heart,” Agatha replied. “I swear.”

“Then why would you say yes to going with her to the fair?” Marisol demanded. “Why would you get her hopes up just to dash them like that?”

“Rio knows that I’m not staying!” Agatha defended. “She thinks I’m from a completely different place, far away from here! And she’s a grown woman, anyway. She’s nearly thirty years old!”

Agatha paused, her eyes wide.

“She’s nearly thirty years old…” She looked at Lilia, who was already staring at her, as if waiting for her to come to her own conclusion. Her gaze was steady on Agatha’s, her chin raised, waiting…

“Marisol is the reason you left the Pod, isn’t she?” she asked.

“No,” Lilia said. “She’s not the reason. I left for…personal reasons. She just gave me the courage I needed to leave. But there were many reasons why I felt the need to get out of there. And why I believe neither of you should go back.”

“What?” Jen exclaimed, sitting up straighter.

“What?” Agatha asked, a little more quietly. “What are you talking about?”

Lilia and Marisol shared a look, then Lilia took a deep breath and motioned with her arm for Marisol to sit on one of the easy chairs she had in the living room while she took the other, the two women on the couch looking between them like they were waiting for either of them to start speaking, but they didn’t know who would be the first to do so.

Lilia started.

“As all of you already know,” she said, “I was born in the sea, which meant that I was endowed with certain…powers.” She took a deep breath. “I never really understood how Sirens got our powers. We were taught that they were bestowed on us from Poseidon himself, that he gave us these powers to protect ourselves and to use them to protect those who were unwanted, like the infants that, traditionally, were left by the seaside to die by uncaring or unwilling parents. It was a practice that has a centuries-long history – if not millennia.” She tutted and shook her head. “A disgusting practice,” she said, “but one that our society has relied on for as long as history itself.”

“Humans are disgusting,” Jen huffed, looking down at her hands, which clenched in the fabric of the blanket that had been draped across her lap.

“No,” Lilia said, her voice firm enough that Jen snapped her head up to look at her. “Well…yes,” Lilia conceded. “Many of them were. Those practices began specifically to rid the world of their baby girls. It’s the reason that our society is matriarchal, why so many more of the landborn mermaids are female and why there are so few males.” She shrugged. “Or, at least, there were so few of them. I have no clue if that’s changed.” She looked to Jen and Agatha.

Jen shrugged. “Not by much,” she said. “Though we did get a pair of twin boys, like, fifteen years ago. Really rare.”

“Sixteen years ago,” Agatha corrected. “They’re my students this year. Billy and Tobey.”

“Tommy,” Jen corrected.

“Whatever,” Agatha said, waving her hand. “They’re not the only boys in their class, but they’re definitely a rare commodity. Billy isn’t interested in girls, though, so…”

“That’s not going to bode well for him,” Lilia said.

Agatha furrowed her brow. “You think so?” she asked.

“I don’t know if it’s changed, but when I was a girl, we were encouraged to mate with the males if we were so inclined. But there were a pair of boys who fell in love with each other and…” She cleared her throat and shook her head. “They were not treated well.”

Agatha frowned at that. She had never heard of this prejudice. In the Pod, it didn’t seem like there was any particular attitude about who could or could not be together. She was never discouraged from choosing another female as her mate. She never knew a couple who was discouraged from being together, though she knew that it was preferred sometimes for mermaids of different genders to be together for the sake of making more Sirens.

She would hate for Billy to feel as though he couldn’t choose a mate that he could be happy with just for the sole purpose of birthing children underwater. It was a ridiculous notion! Nobody chose who they fell in love or wanted to mate with!

“I’m sure they’ll be fine,” she said, but she was uneasy suddenly. “It’s not like that anymore.” She looked at Jen. “Is it?” she asked.

“I don’t really know,” Jen admitted. “I haven’t heard much, but most of my students are girls. I think I only have one boy. He has a female mate already.”

“The leaders have been trying desperately to increase their numbers in the Pod for years,” Lilia said. “Since before even I was born.”

“Whoa,” Agatha said.

“Shut up,” Lilia huffed, throwing a pillow directly at her face with startling accuracy.

Jen snickered next to her. Agatha hit her over the head with the same pillow. Jen smacked it away to the ground, glaring at it.

“Why is that rock so soft?” she asked.

“We don’t have time for that right now,” Agatha scoffed, looking back at Lilia. “Please continue. Why did you leave?”

“Because I no longer wished to kidnap children,” Lilia retorted.

“Kidnap?” Jen asked. “No. Sirens save children. Don’t they?”

“We’re supposed to,” Lilia said. “We were supposed to use our voices to call out to the unwilling mothers, who would have abandoned their children to die, anyway.”

“But you didn’t?” Jen asked, furrowing her brow in confusion.

“I thought I did,” Lilia said, avoiding both hers and Agatha’s gazes. “I used my song to call out to the mothers who did not want their children, as I had done for centuries, as I had learned to do. Even after it wasn’t as common a practice, mothers still brought their newborn babies to the shore to be taken away by the tide. I thought for sure that we were saving these children, giving them a better life than the one they would have ashore.”

“You were!” Jen insisted. “You did! You saved our lives. We would have been dead if not for you.”

Lilia shook her head. “You were never in danger, Jennifer,” Lilia said. “Your mother…she never would’ve…” There was something like shame covering her face now, her eyes filling with tears. “She wanted you.”

“What?” Jen asked, frowning. “Lilia, what are you talking about? She didn’t want me. Why would she put me in the tide if she wanted me?”

“She didn’t place you on the tide,” Lilia said. “Not until…not until my song called out to her ears. It hypnotized her.”

“What are you talking about?” Jen huffed. “That’s not how it works. The song is only meant to reach the unwilling mothers…isn’t it?” Lilia didn’t avoid Jen’s laser-focused gaze, but she also didn’t confirm nor deny what Jen had said. “Lilia?” Lilia didn’t even blink. “Lilia, please!” Lilia’s eyes filled with tears, and she swallowed thickly. “Did my birth mother want me?” Jen asked, her voice low and breaking in the middle of her sentence.

Lilia opened her mouth, but nothing came out as the tears began to run down her cheeks, and she shook her head. She sniffled, swallowed.

“Did she?” Jen demanded after too much time had passed. “Please tell me. Please?”

Agatha knew that, were she able to, Jennifer would have already gotten down to her knees to beg at Lilia’s feet for the answer. And she couldn’t blame her, not at all. It was an earth-shattering revelation. All the landborn mermaids had grown up knowing that the reason they’d been brought to the Pod was so that they could be raised with the knowledge that somebody wanted them, that they were loved by their adopted families.

It was one of the few honors and privileges that landborns had over the seaborns.

But if even that wasn’t the whole truth…

“Please, Lilia,” Jen practically sobbed, reaching out to take Lilia’s hand and squeezing it. “Please, tell me the truth.”

“I can’t,” Lilia answered, her voice choked with tears. “I can’t tell you the truth, Jennifer.”

“Why?” Jen sobbed. “Why not?!”

“Because I don’t know,” Lilia choked out. She swallowed thickly and squeezed Jen’s hand before she could pull back. “I swear I don’t. It actually wasn’t until your mother that I even had a suspicion…”

“My mother?” Jen asked. “What about her?”

“Do you know,” Lilia asked, “that you and Agatha were the last children I res—took from the shores of your islands.”

“We’re from different islands?” Jen asked.

“Of course,” Lilia said. “There are so many islands with what we once thought were unwanted children. We didn’t ever want to focus on just one.” Her eyes hardened slightly. “That’s what they told us, anyway.”

“They?” Agatha asked, looking over Jen’s shoulder at Lilia.

“The leaders,” Lilia said. “The older Sirens, too. The elders.”

“But they were wrong?” Agatha asked.

“Worse,” Lilia said, practically gritting her teeth. “They were liars.”

Jen and Agatha shared a look. There were tears dripping down Jen’s cheeks still. She turned back to Lilia.

“You still haven’t told us exactly what they lied about,” she pointed out.

“Well, I didn’t realize that anything was off until I noticed your mother’s hesitation to place you in the sand,” Lilia said. “I had seen hesitation before, of course. So many years doing this, how could I not have?” She sniffled and reached for a tissue from the box on the coffee table, swiping at her nose. She didn’t let go of Jen’s hand and turned back to her with her features softened. “Your mother, though,” she continued, “she struggled to let go of you.”

“What do you mean?” Jen asked, searching Lilia’s eyes.

“She did what all the other women did,” Lilia started. “She walked down the beach with you in her arms. You were wrapped in this little pink blanket. There was a bow in your hair. You were sleeping so soundly, but she looked exhausted…dazed.” She took a deep breath. “Her eyes were wide and her lips were tight, like she was in pain.” She looked at Jen. “You look like her,” she said. “Like your mother. You have the same eyes as her, the same nose and chin.” She reached up to stroke Jen’s face, swiping a tear away with her thumb.

“I look like her?” Jen asked, her voice barely recognizable.

“Yes,” Lilia confirmed. “She had significantly more hair than you do now,” Jen snorted and rolled her eyes through her tears, “but you do look just like her.” She took another breath and continued: “She was struggling to walk down the beach. At first, I thought it was just because the sand was so uneven. But she wasn’t tripping. She just…couldn’t walk forward.” She paused, furrowing her brow as she looked down. “I should have known even then that something wasn’t quite right. I just thought she was struggling the way I’d seen other mothers struggle: they almost always cried as they set their children to the waves.”

“And you didn’t think that was odd?” Jen asked, moving out of Lilia’s grasp, bumping Agatha in the process. “That women were crying as they sent their children off to die.”

Lilia shook her head. “We were trained not to question it,” she said. “Many women didn’t have any other options. They were often forced to give up their children, so crying really wasn’t that far out of place. I saw nothing odd about it. Even as she sobbed placing you down, blanket and all, into the water.” Lilia’s eyes filled with tears again. “But then she got to her knees. And she started to mumble something against your cheek and clutched the little pink dress you were wearing.” She took another breath as Jen listened, her face glistening with tears. “I fully expected her to change her mind, to pick you back up and carry you back to wherever she came from. But it was like she was in pain as she left you there, dragging her feet all the way back up the beach, looking back over her shoulder, tripping over herself the entire time while you were engulfed by the sea. I plucked you out of the water when you began to cry, placed you against my shoulder…” She swallowed thickly. “I rocked you while I waited for her to come back.”

Jen blinked at that, her mouth gaping slightly. “You waited for her to return?” she asked. “Really?”

“For as long as I could,” Lilia confirmed. “But you were shivering in my arms. I needed to get you somewhere you could be warmed and start your transition into your new form.” She took a deep breath. “If I knew then what I know now, I would have walked you back up that beach myself. I swear it.”

“What do you know now?” Agatha urged. “You still haven’t said.”

“That my powers don’t work the way I’ve always been told,” Lilia said. “That they are far stronger and more…manipulative than I’ve always been led to believe. That what I’ve been doing for most of my life isn’t saving unwanted children from unwilling parents – I’ve been depriving mothers of the babies that they very much do want.” Lilia’s bottom lip started to tremble at that, her tears finally spilling from her eyes as she shook her head, guilt flushing her face. “And I am so sorry, Jennifer. I am so very sorry. I had no idea, I swear.”

Jen pulled away from Lilia, folding her arms across her chest as she rested against the back of the couch, staring straight ahead as the news sunk in.

She was not an unwanted child, as she had been led to believe. Her mother had struggled to abandon her. She had loved Jennifer and wanted to keep her.

Jennifer had two parents who loved her.

She had older brothers who loved her.

She had lived a good life under the sea. She was a healer, a doctor. She was well-respected in her community, in the Pod.

But she was never supposed to be there. She was supposed to be raised here, on land, by a mother who found it painful to let her go, who was influenced by magic and yet still fought against every bit of it in an attempt to keep it – but still lost in the end.

Agatha reached for her hand in a move that would have – and did – shock Jen. She didn’t resist, though, as Agatha squeezed her fingers. She squeezed back as she let the tears flow, allowing the sobs to wrack her body.

Lilia just watched her, her face looking almost as broken as Jen’s, the guilt written in the crinkle of her eyebrows as her fingers played with the hem of her skirt. She looked down at her lap every now and then, tears dripping from her eyes and soaking into the material of her skirt and shirt.

“I’m so sorry,” she said on repeat. “I am so, so sorry.”

Eventually, Jennifer stopped crying, but her head was starting to loll against Agatha’s shoulder. She must have been exhausted just from the journey, but the crying certainly couldn’t have helped, either.

And starving.

“When was the last time you ate?” Agatha asked her, voice low and gentle.

Jen shrugged. “I had a little bit of eel from home earlier,” she said. “I shared it with Scratchy though.”

“Anything else?” Agatha asked. Jen shook her head, and Agatha looked up. “Do we have anything to give her?” she asked. “Before she goes to bed.”

“Of course,” Lilia said, nodding. “Let me fix her some of the leftover pasta from the other night. She can eat it in bed.”

“Bed?” Jen asked, glancing around. “Bed where?”

“In the other room,” Agatha told her. I was staying there, but you can’t go up the stairs, so I guess you can take it. I can find somewhere else to sleep.”

“I don’t want to take your bed,” Jen murmured. “I can figure it out.” She let out a long, loud yawn, nuzzling more against Agatha’s shoulder. Despite being a full six inches lon—er, taller than Agatha, Jen felt small as she curled her body toward Agatha’s, pulling her knees up against her chest like she’d had them before, on the beach. That fuzzy thing she was wearing on her head tickled Agatha’s cheek, but she didn’t move away.

Lilia returned with two warm plates of food and the tray that she had used for when they ate their dinner on the couch, watching old movies over the last few days. Or the time Agatha had eaten in bed, her legs sore from her first session with Rio out of the pool. Lilia had sat next to her in the bed as they ate their dinner, telling her stories about her adventures to the surface while she was a Siren.

She wondered how many things Lilia had neglected to tell her about those journeys.

“Can you take these to the bedroom, doll?” Lilia asked. “I’ll handle Jennifer…”

“Don’t touch her,” Agatha said, a little more sharply than she had intended. Lilia’s eyes widened at her tone. “I’ve got her,” Agatha said. “I can help her.”

Lilia nodded. “Okay,” she said, quietly. “That’s fine. I’ll just…bring these into the bedroom.”

Lilia scurried off with the food while Agatha helped Jen stand up, practically carrying her as they stumbled after Lilia toward the first-floor bedroom. Jen’s face stayed pressed to Agatha’s shoulder, which left her pretty hunched over though she didn’t seem to mind it. Agatha could feel when a fresh wave of tears began. Her fists clenched on the material at Jen’s hip.

She and Jen weren’t overly affectionate friends. They hugged, sometimes, but that was rare for them. Moreso, they just existed near each other from time to time. They celebrated their big moments together and comforted each other in the best way they could when one of them was having a rough time, but rarely did that include any physical closeness unless one of them wanted to purposely annoy the other – or it was very serious.

This was definitely the latter.

Agatha was able to get Jen to the bedroom well enough, even helping her to sit up in the bed with the pillows fluffed up behind her back. Lilia had left their food at the end of the bed. Agatha reached for it as soon as she had Jen settled.

She handed Jen a fork for her spaghetti, and Jen just stared at it.

“What the hell is this?” she asked, looking at Agatha.

“A fork,” Agatha said, twisting her pasta and bringing it to her mouth. It tasted just as good as it had the other night, though she knew that most warmed foods didn’t. Lilia really was a good cook. She eyed Jen next to her, who seemed to be struggling with the concept of the tool in her hand still. “Jen, I’ve brought forks back from shipwrecks before.”

“But you said they were for hair!” Jen huffed. “I obviously had no use for them then!”

“Well, obviously I was mistaken,” Agatha snorted. “They’re for eating.”

“Why do humans need tools for eating? They have hands the same as we do.”

“It’s messier that way,” Agatha said. “They have sauces and toppings on their food. It’s not just eel and fish and kelp and all that. It has way more flavor.” She twirled more spaghetti on her fork. “Follow my lead,” she said. “It’s not that hard.”

Jen watched as she stabbed her pasta, following along with every move Agatha made, twisting the fork to gather the noodles and then scooping them up and into her mouth. The second the food touched her tongue, Agatha could tell that she was having something akin to a religious experience as she began to shovel more and more food into her mouth.

“Slow down,” Agatha said. “You can choke just as easily on land, you know!” She huffed. “Easier, even. It’s not going anywhere. Take it easy.”

“It’s so good though!” Jen said, through a mouthful of spaghetti. “Why is it so much better than fish?”

“Because, as I’ve said, it has flavor,” Agatha laughed. “Lilia adds some kind of sand to it to make it taste better. I don’t know what it is, but it smells really good when she’s cooking it. Makes me immediately hungry.”

She twisted more of her spaghetti and leaned into the plate to eat before reaching for the drink Lilia had left her, washing everything down.

Jen reached for hers, as well.

“How do I chew this?” she asked, looking at Agatha as she tipped her cup this way and that, watching as the liquid inside moved.

“You swallow it,” Agatha said. “It’s water. Try not to breathe it.”

“Why would I try to breathe it?” Jen snorted. “I’m not an idiot.”

“Go fuck yourself,” Agatha breathed against the rim of her glass.

“What was that?” Jen asked, just before she brought the cup to her lips.

“What? I didn’t say anything,” Agatha said, taking a long sip of water.

“Freak,” Jen huffed, taking a sip of water.

Agatha noticed with no small amount of satisfaction that she choked a bit on the water though she tried to hide it by clearing her throat as soon as she pulled the cup away from her lips.

When they were finished, Agatha took both plates, complete with silverware, into the kitchen, placing them in the sink.

Lilia and Marisol were talking as they, too, ate leftovers. They each also had some kind of red liquid in strange-looking cups. Any other day, Agatha would have asked about it, but she was still annoyed with Lilia after what she’d just told them, so talking about weird cups really wasn’t top on her list of priorities right then.

Instead, she padded up to the older woman, who was already looking up at her with a frown on her face.

“Agatha,” Lilia started, her voice barely above a breath it was so low.

“No,” Agatha said. “Not right now. I don’t want to hear your excuses or apologies.” She swallowed thickly. “I just want you to tell me one thing. No more lies or half-truths.”

“Anything,” Lilia said, wiping her mouth with a napkin. “Anything you want to know, just ask.”

“Did my mother want me?” Agatha asked. “Like Jen’s mother, I mean. Did she…did she struggle to give me up?”

Lilia’s jaw clenched at the question, anger flashing across her face. Not at Agatha, though. Her eyes actually softened after a second while looking at Agatha. But there was a noticeable clench in her jaw and in her fingers around the glass in her hand. She placed it down on the table and opened her mouth to speak.

“Agatha?” Jen called from her room across the hall. “Where’d you go?”

Agatha took a deep breath, gritting her teeth slightly as she glanced back toward the bedroom. She turned back to Lilia.

 “We’re going to continue this,” she said. Then she looked at Marisol, who had been completely silent toward her the entire time they’d been home. “I would also like to talk to you,” she said, “if you don’t mind.”

“I’ll be here,” Marisol said. “Whenever you’re ready.” She sipped at the red drink in her hand, and Agatha nodded.

“Okay,” Agatha murmured. “Give me a little bit.”

Jen was still sitting up as Agatha re-entered the bedroom, but her eyes were beginning to droop now with her fatigue.

“Getting tired?” Agatha asked.

Jen nodded. “I’ve been swimming for the better part of the last twenty-four hours,” Jen reminded her. “And whatever it was that you just fed me feels heavy in my stomach – in a good way.”

“Yeah, good food can do that sometimes,” Agatha agreed, echoing a sentiment that Lilia had shared after her first full meal on land. “Okay, well let’s get you to the bathroom and then you can go to sleep.”

“The what room?” Jen asked as Agatha started to help her up, pushing the blankets off of her and tugging Jen’s feet to land on the floor.

“The room where we do our business,” Agatha said, using the word that Lilia had used the first time she explained it to her.

“What business?” Jen asked.

“Peeing, Jen,” Agatha said. “You’re going to learn how to pee on land.”

“That sounds ominous,” Jen said, stumbling as Agatha practically dragged her toward the bathroom.

“It’s not,” Agatha huffed, shutting the door behind them.

After spending far too long explaining how and why humans needed a special room to urinate and do other things, Agatha got Jen cleaned and back to bed, pretty much tucking her in. She made to leave, but Jen’s hand reached out, clutching hers and tugging her back, quite forcefully.

“Don’t go,” she begged. “Please. Not until I fall asleep anyway.”

Agatha took a deep breath and nodded, turning back and climbing over Jen. “Fine,” she said. “I’ll stay. Just until you fall asleep though, okay?”

“Okay,” Jen murmured, relaxing into the pillows. “Why are human beds so much softer than ours?”

“Because they can’t literally float while they sleep, I guess?” Agatha said with a shrug. “I wondered the same thing my first night here. There are a lot of things that are different up here than they are down there.”

“Better?” Jen asked.

Agatha though of the food she had enjoyed the last few days, and of the music she got to hear, the clothes she got to wear. It was all still so new and strange, but she liked it. She liked wearing the colorful fabrics and eating the decadent food. She liked dancing to the music and even just hearing it without moving her feet at all. She liked feeling the sun on her skin without the water acting as a barrier, even when it burned her. She liked feeling the sand between her toes when she and Lilia took their shoes off at the beach.

She liked having toes, period.

She liked having people who didn’t roll their eyes every time she asked a question, like she was too curious for her own good. She liked that Lilia answered every question she had before she ever even voiced it. She liked that Rio did the same.

Rio…she really liked Rio. She liked how patient Rio was, how willing she was to share her knowledge with Agatha. She liked that little spark she got in her eye that lit up her entire face when she was explaining something to Agatha. She liked her weird taste in ice cream and her strange relationship with the man who worked in the ice cream shop. She liked that she had a good relationship with her mother (although Agatha was a little bit suspicious how much Rio knew about her mother’s true origins) even though she was actually somewhat jealous of it.

That didn’t mean that Agatha didn’t miss the ocean…somewhat. She missed…teaching? Her students? They weren’t all little nerds like Billy, but she did miss them. They weren’t horrible kids. They just…weren’t as excited to learn as he was. And she missed the shipwrecks she used to explore. They were wells of knowledge.

She also had Scratchy. Scratchy, she could at least still see because he was allowed to come to the surface of the water, but she couldn’t really give him her leftover eel if she never caught any.

Could sharks eat spaghetti?

Agatha had no idea.

Were humans willing to leave him alone, or would they be like the guards and try to kill him for his meat? Would it be safer for him if she went back down to the Pod, where he could live just outside of its walls?

“In some ways, yes,” Agatha decidedly answered. “In some ways, no.”

“Do you think I’d like it up here more than down there?”

“I don’t know,” Agatha answered honestly. “Maybe. We’ll see how you feel tomorrow.”

“At the fair?” Jen asked. “Seriously, what even is that?”

Her voice was getting quiet, her words slurring a little bit as her eyes drooped and she sunk deeper against the pillows and mattress. She let out a soft snore before her eyes even fully shut, but soon enough she was asleep.

Agatha stayed there for a while, just watching her.

As soon as she was certain that Jen would not stir, she extracted herself from the bed and padded back to the door, turning off the light and closing it gently behind her.

Marisol and Lilia were still in the kitchen, but their plates were on the drying rack, along with Agatha’s and Jen’s. They were leaning against the kitchen island, still sipping their red drinks.

When Agatha entered, Lilia turned fully toward her, placing her glass on the countertop.

“So,” she said, “what would you like to know?”

Chapter End Notes

idk where the trauma came from I'm just 🧍‍♀️

Chapter 13

Chapter Summary

Agatha learns more about the night Lilia saved her

TW for mentions of Evanora Harkness

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Chapter Thirteen

The night air was cooler than the afternoon had been by a significant amount as they stepped outside onto Lilia’s patio. It overlooked the ocean over the fence.

Patio.

Fence.

These words would have meant nothing to Agatha just a few days ago. That they were what humans called the most mundane things in their lives was…incredible to her. There were four folding chairs out there for them, too, arranged in a semi-circle facing the ocean.

Agatha took one chair on the end, while Marisol and Lilia left a gap between them and her, giving her space. They said nothing for a few moments, just listening to the waves crashing against the shores in the distance. Agatha shut her eyes and felt the warm island breeze on her face as the sun was starting to set. When she opened them again, the sky’s orange and purple hues were fading to deep indigo and navy.

The moon was mostly full. Agatha marveled at it for a moment.

This was another thing that the surface had over the ocean.

“So?” Lilia said, after a long moment.

Agatha took a deep breath, turning toward her.

“What questions do you have for me, doll?” Lilia asked, her voice tinged with anxiety, as well as her eyes. She swallowed thickly.

“Where do I come from?” Agatha asked. “Which island did you steal me from?”

“You were not ‘stolen’,” Lilia retorted, “and I actually, coincidentally, found you…here. On this island.”

Agatha’s eyes widened at that, her heart beginning to hammer in her chest. This was where she had been born?

“Is my mother still here?” she asked. “My father?”

“I don’t know who your father is,” Lilia admitted. “I wondered about that, too, after I learned who your mother is. But I could never quite tell.”

“They’re not together?” Agatha asked, furrowing her brow.

“Most happily married women don’t give their children to the sea,” Marisol commented.

“Well, we don’t really know how many women gave their children to the sea willingly, do we?” Agatha retorted, raising her eyebrow at Lilia.

“No,” she agreed, “we don’t.” She looked down at her lap, rightfully ashamed.

“So,” Agatha said, “who is my mother? Where can I find her?”

“Agatha,” Lilia sighed, “trust me when I say that you don’t want to find your mother.”

“Why would I trust anything you have to say to me?” Agatha huffed. “When you literally stole Jen—”

“I didn’t want to!” Lilia snapped, her eyes narrowing angrily at Agatha, her voice rising a bit in volume and pitch. “I didn’t want to steal Jennifer or any other child. I couldn’t control it!”

“You couldn’t control the powers that you yourself claim you’ve had for hundreds of years?” Agatha asked, skeptically. “Really?”

Lilia clenched her jaw and looked away. “They told us lies about our abilities,” she said. “My mentor told me that my song would only draw out the mothers that would have harmed their children anyway. If they didn’t give them to the ocean, they said, then they would have harmed them in some other way. At least this way, they were still alive. Better alive and in the Pod, they’d say, than dead somewhere else. We needed the help in our population, anyway.”

“What exactly do your abilities do?” Agatha asked. “If they don’t draw unwilling mothers to the shore, what do they do?”

Lilia took a deep breath and furrowed her brow. “That was never quite clear to me,” she said, “after taking Jennifer. I knew that her mother hadn’t wanted to give her up. That much was clear. She was struggling against my magic the way I had never seen another mother struggle. She had dressed Jennifer the way no other woman had. In over three hundred years, I had never seen a baby come out in more than a thin blanket or towel. The mothers that brought their children to be taken by the sea did not care that they would be potentially drowning their babies. They wanted to leave no trace that could lead back to themselves. Jennifer’s blanket, however, had her name on it.”

She sighed, looking off in the distance like she was remembering something quite fondly. “That’s where I got her name,” she said. “After I brought her down to the Pod. She was still wrapped in that blanket, her little hands clutching it. They took it off of her the second I arrived at the nursery. They tossed it aside like trash, then they asked me what her name was. I said ‘Jennifer’ because I knew we had never had one before. They didn’t even ask where I had gotten the name, and I didn’t have to tell them that her mother had given it to her. Her mother who loved her, who didn’t want to leave her…”

She swallowed, thickly.

“I decided after that that I was just going to leave, escape to the surface. I had known other Sirens and mermaids to do the same, but I never understood why. Sure, walking on land and congregating with the humans was fun. I enjoyed their food and music and the way their society changed every decade or so, in significant ways, but I hadn’t truly considered integrating myself into that society until…”

“Until Jen,” Agatha said.

Lilia nodded. “Until Jennifer,” she confirmed. “She was meant to be my last baby. I couldn’t give her back to her mother, anyway.”

“Why not?” Agatha asked.

“They never taught you about this?” Lilia asked. “About our powers?”

“It looks like they never taught you about your powers, either,” Agatha pointed out. “So, no, they didn’t teach me about your powers.”

Lilia snorted. “Fair,” she said, nodding. She cleared her throat. “Siren song magic has a way of making a woman…forget she ever gave birth. They forget that they gave birth and that they brought their child to the shore to float off into the night. The second that baby leaves their arms, they become somewhat of a blank slate.” Lilia took a deep breath. “The second Jennifer’s mother let go, though she fought hard not to, she started to forget about her baby.”

“What about the others?” Agatha asked. “The people around her? Surely somebody must have noticed that she’d been pregnant. Didn’t Jen have a father or siblings?”

“I have no idea,” Lilia admitted. “If she did, they would have forgotten, too.” She swallowed thickly. “My powers don’t only affect the mother; they also affect the entire community. They forget that there was ever a new baby on the way – at least the one that is taken away.”

“But,” Agatha said, “they must have prepared for her, right? What about the nursery?”

“As far as I can tell,” Lilia said, “they just…take it down. They rid themselves of any trace of a baby that they’ll never see again. By the time they’re aware, there is no trace that they ever gave birth.”

“That’s…fucked up,” Agatha said, standing up, her fists clenched at her side. “That is so fucked up! How could you do that!?” Lilia reached for her, but Agatha backed out of her reach entirely, going toward the fence, looking out at the ocean. “And then you did the same thing to me!”

“No!” Lilia said, her voice a bit harder than Agatha had been expecting. “That situation was entirely different!”

“How so?” Agatha huffed, turning back to her. “You stole me, didn’t you?! Just like you did Jen? You admitted yourself that you wanted to adopt me, to raise me as your own, didn’t you? You would have raised me down there, with all those thieves?! Mother of the fucking year!” She took a deep breath, turning cold eyes on Lilia. “You should have just left me with my mother. You should never have sang your song for her and—”

“I didn’t,” Lilia said, her voice practically a whisper.

“What?” Agatha asked, her brow furrowed. “What do you mean, you didn’t?”

“I didn’t sing my song for your mother,” Lilia clarified. She swallowed thickly. “I wasn’t coming here to look for any new children to…rescue.” She curled her fingers as she said the word. “I was going to settle here. I was done with the Pod.”

“But you said that my mother placed me on the shore,” Agatha said. “Why would she do that if…” Her face paled as realization dawned on her. “She was actually trying to kill me. On her own.”

She nearly fell right there by the fence, but as her knees gave out, she felt hands on her. She turned her head to see Marisol had stood up and caught her. The older woman’s face was soft as she helped Agatha back to her seat, setting her down and stroking her shoulder comfortingly before backing off.

“You’re okay, querida,” Marisol said, softly. “You’re okay.” She sat down, her face still soft as she regarded the younger woman.

“No, I’m not,” Agatha said, fighting past a lump that was quickly forming in her throat. Her eyes ached with tears that she refused to let fall. She blinked them back as she turned back to Lilia. “My mother…tried to kill me. Without a Siren song to influence her?”

Lilia nodded, slowly. Sadly.

“I’m so sorry, tesoro,” she said. “Your mother is not a good person.”

“B-but even bad people love their children,” Agatha argued. “Don’t they?”

“I’ve never known her to love any child,” Lilia said. “Or anybody, for that matter.”

“So, I was just what?” Agatha huffed. “An inconvenience to her? Something she could toss away like trash?”

Tears spilled down her cheeks at that. Lilia’s own tears mirrored them.

“I was just trash?” Agatha asked, her voice tinier now, broken. Her bottom lip trembled.

“Querida,” Marisol said. “It was not your fault. Not at all!” She reached out, clutching Agatha’s hand. Agatha attempted to pull away, but Marisol held on, tightening her grip, but not in an uncomfortable way. Her hand was soft, her grip somewhat…comforting. “That woman is not your mother. She is simply the woman who gave birth to you. You have two mothers who love you very much.”

“No, they don’t,” Agatha said, her chest aching. “My mothers…they cannot stand me. I haven’t even spoken a word to either of them in over five years!”

She tore her hand away from Marisol finally and stood up, starting to pace. “They always said I was more trouble than I was worth. I was too curious for my own good and a troublemaker. I wasn’t worth loving.” She stopped, the tears streaming down her face. “I’m not worth loving.”

She dropped to her knees in the grass, her body shaking with sobs.

It wasn’t long before two sets of arms wrapped around her, two voices whispering in her ears, two hands stroking her hair and her shoulders, and two arms wrapping her tightly in their embrace. Agatha struggled weakly against them, but they held tight, continuing to whisper words to her in languages she didn’t understand.

Lilia and Marisol held her like that for a while, continuing to stroke her hair and her back and her arms until Agatha practically melted into their embrace, her body sagging against their hold, her cheek falling to Lilia’s shoulder as Marisol held most of Agatha’s body in her own lap, her arms wrapped more loosely around Agatha’s frame.

Agatha’s entire body felt heavy, both from exhaustion and from the knowledge that she had been so utterly unwanted and unloved, as an infant, that her mother had legitimately attempted to murder her without the manipulation of a magic song.

“Why would she do that?” she murmured, mostly to herself, but she lifted her head to look at Lilia as she asked the question aloud. “Why would she try to kill me? I was just a baby.”

“As I said,” Lilia sighed: “your mother is unwell. She’s…”

“Evil,” Marisol finished for her. Lilia scoffed, but she didn’t disagree. “I had to deal with that woman for four frickin’ years while Rio was in high school, and I—”

“School?” Agatha asked, her eyes widening as she turned back to Marisol. “She…she’s a teacher?”

Marisol just stared at her, wide-eyed. She glanced up at Lilia as though she’d just made a major misstep, had said something she wasn’t meant to say.

Agatha turned back to Lilia, questioningly.

“Is my mother a teacher?” Agatha all but demanded. “She’s allowed around children?”

“Teenagers,” Marisol corrected. “Not that it matters,” she muttered when Lilia glared at her.

“She’s not a teacher,” Lilia sighed. “She’s a…principal.”

“Principal?” Agatha asked, furrowing her brow.

“Somebody who oversees the teachers,” Lilia explained. “She deals with any issues that may happen in their classes at the school.”

“She made my daughter’s life a living hell all throughout high school,” Marisol grumbled. “Puta.”

“Won’t argue with that,” Lilia grunted as she stood, pulling Agatha with her.

Agatha’s legs were unsteady, almost like they had been a week ago. Lilia brushed off the dirt from her knees, and Agatha let out a hiss.

“Oof,” Lilia said, “that’s going to bruise. No blood, though, which is good. Be careful with this body, kid; it’s the only one you get.”

She guided Agatha to sit back down and pulled her own chair a little bit closer. As soon as Agatha was out of her lap, Marisol did the same.

“How did my mother make Rio’s life ‘a living hell’?” Agatha asked, looking at Marisol. “What did she do?”

“The bitch is homophobic,” Marisol answered before Lilia could say anything. “She treated my daughter like shit just because she liked girls.”

Agatha furrowed her brow, looking between the two women. “Homophobic?” she asked. “What does that mean? She’s afraid of…homo? That means same? What does Rio liking girls have anything to do with that?”

Lilia took a deep breath and patted Agatha’s knee. “Love up here isn’t like what it is down in the Pod,” she said. “People have opinions on women who are attracted to other women and men who are attracted to other men. It’s not the default.”

“I know that it’s not the default,” Agatha argued. “For mating and procreation, those with different internal biology must come together to make progeny. That’s a basic lesson we learn as children, but what does that have to do with a phobia?” She turned to Marisol. “Was my mother afraid of Rio?”

“Ha!” Marisol snorted. “I wish that bitch was afraid of anything. She shoulda been afraid of me.” She let out a puff of air from her nose. “No. She wasn’t afraid of Rio. She hated Rio.
