She hated my baby.” Her jaw clenched. “And, for that, I hated her.” She grimaced looking at Agatha. “Sorry, querida.”

Agatha shrugged. “She tried to kill me: hating her is warranted.”

Marisol let out a tiny giggle at that, patting Agatha’s hand. “You’re funny,” she said. “Rio likes funny.”

“Marisol!” Lilia hissed.

“What?” Marisol said, shrugging. “It wouldn’t be the worst thing in the world.”

“That’s not what you were saying a few hours ago,” Lilia pointed out, “when you were complaining about how much Rio talks about Agatha.”

Agatha felt her cheeks go hot at that revelation. “Rio…talks about me?” she asked.

“Madre de dios,” Marisol said under her breath. “Rio would kill you for that, you know that?” she said to Lilia.

“No, she wouldn’t,” Lilia snorted. “I’ve embarrassed her in way worse ways.” She cackled. “Remember her first date with that girl back in eighth grade. What was her name?”

“Rosa,” Marisol said. “She didn’t talk to you for weeks.”

“Wait,” Agatha said. “Can we go back to Rio talking about me for a second?” She looked at Marisol with pinkened cheeks. “What, exactly, was she saying about me?”

Marisol snorted, shaking her head. “Oh, nothing much,” she said. “Just that you were making such incredible progress and blah, blah, blah. She spoke for so long that I had to tune her out after a bit, but she could go on for hours about how extraordinary you are and this and that.” She rolled her eyes, affectionately. “She’s always bragged about how well her patients were doing, though. You should hear her talk about Yelena.”

“Who?” Agatha asked, her voice unintentionally sharper as she felt a stab of jealousy.

“Don’t fret, querida,” Marisol said, patting her cheek. “She harbors no feelings for active patients. That’s why she asked you on a date tonight.”

“She what?!” Lilia gasped, looking between them. “Is that what had you in a pissy mood when you showed up tonight?”

Agatha frowned at Marisol. “You don’t want us to go on a date?” she asked.

“Well, I didn’t,” Marisol admitted. “Not if you’re going back to the Pod. I don’t want my baby getting her heart broken again.”

“What made you change your mind?” Lilia asked.

“Who said I did?”

“I know you,” Lilia huffed. “You changed your mind.”

Marisol took a deep breath and nodded. “I did,” she confirmed.

“You did?” Agatha asked, looking at her with wide eyes.

Marisol nodded. “Yeah,” she said. “You…seem like a good girl, Agatha Harkness.” She grinned as she said the last name. “And it seems as though you’re starting to put down roots here. Any thought of staying on land?”

Agatha’s heart started to race at that question, her pulse thrumming in her neck at the thought of…staying. Would she be able to do that? Would she be able to leave everything she once knew for…what? Rio? A woman she’d known less than a week. A human? Was that insane?

“I don’t know,” she admitted. “There’s a lot to consider. I don’t even know what I’d do up here! I don’t even have that little thing with my face on it that you have.” She looked at Lilia.

“And ID,” Lilia supplied for her. “I can get you one. I know a guy. I can get you a birth certificate and teaching credentials, too, if you want to be a teacher.”

“You can?” Agatha asked, blinking at her.

“I can,” Lilia confirmed. “You might need to learn a lot more before you actually apply to a teaching job, though. You don’t know as much about the surface as you do about mermaid society, but I have a feeling that’s not going to really be an issue for you, is it?”

Agatha’s heart flipped at that. She loved to learn. Part of what made her so outcast in the Pod was her insatiable curiosity and her yearning for knowledge. She wanted so much knowledge all the time. She had never understood why that was such a bad thing.

Was it encouraged up here? Learning? Could she really find acceptance for it? Finally?

“I’ll be honest,” Agatha said, “the more I think about it, the more attractive it seems.” Then a thought occurred to her. “Would I have to teach under my mother? Would I have to interact with her knowing what I know now while she believes me to be dead?”

“Not necessarily,” Lilia said. “You don’t have to work at the high school level with the older kids. You could be a middle school teacher, or you could even work with the younger children in the elementary school. You could shape their young minds, teach them how to read and write and do simple mathematics.” She reached out, stroking Agatha’s jaw. “You can do anything you set your mind to, tesoro.”

Agatha took a deep breath, her body relaxing as her face pressed against Lilia’s hand, struck with a sudden wave of affection for the older woman.

“I’ll think about it,” she said.

“Sleep on it,” Lilia agreed, patting her cheek. “You look exhausted already. I saw you dancing earlier,” she glanced at Marisol, “with Rio.”

“Oh?” Marisol asked, giving her a tiny teasing grin. “You and my baby were dancing? Did you have fuuun?” Her voice was just as teasing as she reached out to pinch Agatha’s cheek.

Agatha swatted her hand away, standing up. “Ugh,” she said. “You two are annoying.”

“We’re moms is what we are,” Lilia said, standing up with her.

“You got children I don’t know about?” Agatha asked, raising her brow.

“Dozens,” Lilia said, nodding. “Hundreds, even. All the lost souls who escape the Pod and come through here are my children.”

“Escape the Pod?” Agatha snorted. “It’s not that hard.”

“You say that now,” Marisol said. “Just wait.”

Agatha didn’t like the sound of that, but she was far too sleepy to worry about it now. The sun had long set while they were talking and her eyes hurt from crying. Her brain hurt from all the knowledge she had gained from their conversation. And her heart hurt from what that information did to it.

She was exhausted, like Lilia said.

“I’m going to bed,” she said, rubbing her eyes.

“Do you want me to make up the spare room upstairs for you?” Lilia asked. “Since Jen has taken your room.”

Agatha thought about it for half a second, then shook her head. “I can share with Jen,” she decided. “I don’t want her waking up alone, anyway.”

“You’re so sweet,” Marisol cooed, pouting her lips.

Agatha rolled her eyes, but she was blushing again.

What was it about the Vidal women making her blush all the time? It was exhausting.

“Whatever,” she grumbled. “Goodnight.”

“Buenas noches, querida,” Marisol sing-songed at her.

“Goodnight, tesoro,” Lilia said, stroking her shoulder as Agatha passed.

Agatha offered her a soft smile as she headed toward the sliding glass doors that led into Lilia’s kitchen. She opened them and made to step inside, before pausing and turning back.

“Dr. Vidal?” she asked.

“Please,” the doctor retorted with a snort. “Call me Marisol.”

“Marisol,” Agatha echoed, nodding. “Does Rio…does she know that you’re a—”

“No,” Marisol said, quickly. She swallowed thickly, looking down as if ashamed of that face. “She does not.”

“Why?” Agatha asked, furrowing her brow. “Why wouldn’t you tell her—”

“I have my reasons,” Marisol said. “I just…I think it’s for the best.”

Her tone left no room for argument, so Agatha just simply…didn’t. She just nodded and turned back toward the kitchen, stepping inside.

“Agatha,” Marisol called after her.

Agatha paused, waiting.

“I’d prefer it if you didn’t tell her, either,” she said.

Agatha looked back over her shoulder. “It’s not my secret to tell,” she said, before shutting the door and making her way to bed.

Chapter End Notes

Agatha, my baby...

Chapter 14

Chapter Summary

Rio as a nervous little dork for her date

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Chapter Fourteen

Rio woke up feeling…good.

No, better than good.

She felt…excited. There were butterflies in her stomach that she hadn’t felt in years. She felt the giddy need to giggle.

Rio didn’t giggle.

Well, okay, she had been known, on occasion, to let out a laugh that could have been misconstrued as a giggle.

But that was rare.

Which is part of what made this so exciting.

Agatha Harkness made her want to giggle.

And that felt almost dumb.

Ugh, who was she?

Rio reached for her sketchbook on her end table and picked up her pencil, as well. She had been sketching the night before. After she said goodbye to Lilia and her mom and Agatha (giggle) she had taken a detour down the dock and sat down, pulling the tiny book from her back pocket, as well as a pencil that could just barely be called that anymore from having used it nearly to a nub.

Rio never wasted her pencils if she could help it. They weren’t anything special – she didn’t waste money on fancy charcoal or artist pencils when she didn’t consider herself much of an artist – but she was pretty good friends with Ron, who worked the counter at the stationary store. He gave her discounts that he definitely wasn’t supposed to give her, but he’d been a friend of her dad’s.

Santiago Vidal had also been an amateur artist who drew some of the fish and birds they saw at sea. He had taught Rio how to draw a shark for the first time when she was seven. From there, her interest in drawing not only sharks but also other sea creatures grew. She had sketchbooks full of whales and dolphins and crabs and lobsters and every kind of fish they’d ever caught. The evolution of her art was clear throughout these drawings. She had gotten better over the years, for sure, but she still wasn’t a professional artist, nor did she have any desire to be.

That being said, her sketch of Agatha wasn’t too shabby.

She had felt creepy sketching it at first, like she was some kind of obsessive stalker creeping on an unsuspecting victim. But it’s not like she was about to make wallpaper out of the sketch or anything. She had sketches of other people in her life. She had sketches of Alice and Lilia and her mom and her dad…

Rio’s chest ache as she thought of the last sketch she’d ever made of her dad. She’d drawn his face a little fuller than it was after all the chemo had failed to cure him, making his cheeks look more sunken in and sallow. She’d made his eyes wide and full of that mirth they’d had her entire childhood with him. She’d broadened his shoulders and made his smile nice and wide, with that little gap between his front teeth that she’d inherited.

She thought about that sketch, how she had framed it and gifted it to her mami after her father’s funeral, how she’d held her while she sobbed in Rio’s arms, how Rio had refused to cry because she had to be strong for Marisol. Strong like her father. She had held her mother as tightly as she could bear while also holding back the tears.

Rio sniffled and shook her head, wiping the image of her mother’s crying from her mind as she focused on the tiny sketchbook in her hand.

She had made quick sketches of Agatha’s face the night before, along with a few of them dancing like idiots around the dance floor at the plaza. The sketches weren’t great, but they were good enough that anybody who knew them would recognize whom Rio was attempting to depict.

She’d also drawn a shark.

It wasn’t her fault this time, though!

They weren’t always on her mind, no matter what Alice said. She had just seen one swimming around, pretty close to the shore, when she’d been sitting on the dock. She’d caught sight of it out of the corner of her eye. It was tinted yellow and had scars and a few holes in it.

Rio’s heart had clenched at the sight of the poor thing. It had been through a lot. She had made a quick sketch of it as it swam in slow circles right in front of her, poking it’s head out every so often so that she caught its big blank black eyes and gaping mouth.

Sharks always looked like they had anxiety to Rio. This one looked like he was constantly in the midst of an existential crisis.

He was so cute.

Rio felt as though she had met this shark before. Something about his coloring and the scars that lined his back were familiar to her.

But she’d seen a lot of sharks. She’d drawn pretty much all of the ones she’d seen, too. Maybe that was it. Just an overabundance of her drawing sharks had led to her familiarizing her with them a little bit more than what was accepted as normal.

At least according to Alice.

But she was a hater, so…

Rio ran her finger over the shark sketch, smudging the pencil a tiny bit, before returning to the sketch of Agatha, laughing with her eyes crinkled at the corners. Her smile was so wide and her lips were plump and her cheeks were round while her jaw was squared and…Rio felt those butterflies causing a ruckus in her stomach again and blushed, falling back to the bed to bury her face in her pillow.

She was pathetic.

Agatha wasn’t going to stay.

She had a whole life somewhere else. She was a teacher somewhere. She probably had friends and co-workers who were worried about her! She couldn’t just…stay. Not for Rio, a woman she’d met just a week prior.

Still, Rio found herself excited about their date.

She had resisted feeling anything more than fondness for Agatha for the last week. She had remained professional despite her growing feelings for the other woman. She had tried to keep her distance as she helped Agatha learn how to walk again. She had encouraged Agatha to keep speaking so that she could regain the ability to talk. She had ignored the goosebumps and shivers and heat in her cheeks whenever they touched, trying not to let herself feel too much…

And she’d somehow failed.

Rio didn’t know what was wrong with her.

She had never felt this way about a patient before. She had never let herself feel this way about a patient before. And it had been so easy to disconnect from them in the past. She’d always kept it in her mind that her patients were entirely off-limits, whether gay or straight, single or married, attractive or not…Rio was good at separating herself from them. She was very good at boundaries.

And there were some patients who were more than willing to cross boundaries over the years. Rio didn’t let them. She’d prefer to stop seeing them altogether if they couldn’t respect that.

She had been very lucky…up until now.

With Agatha, it just felt…different.

Rio didn’t how to explain it or why it was happening with her, of all people, but there was something so…intriguing about her. Something that Rio felt drawn to…

“Idiot,” Rio groaned into her pillow, punching it. “Idiota. Pinches cabrona.” She sighed deeply, shaking her head as she pushed herself back into a seated position and reached again for the sketchbook. Then she searched the blankets for a minute before she found that little nub of a pencil, ignoring the marks it left on her green bedspread as she went back to her sketch, cleaning it up a little bit before moving on to shading it. She defined Agatha’s nose and added a little bit of light to her eyes, making them brighter with her eraser.

Her eyes were so blue…they almost glowed.

Rio bit her lip as she shaded Agatha’s mouth, adding a little definition to those teeth. Such a beautiful smile…she didn’t want to forget it when she was gone.

Rio had changed her outfit three separate times by the time she finally made it to the plaza – and, even then, she very nearly turned back around.

At first, she had put on one of the more formal shirts she owned. One with a collar and sleeves. A polo. She had a few of them that she wore mostly for work when she had one of her more finicky clients (re: old and cranky), but she quickly vetoed that the second she looked at herself in the mirror. She looked like she was on her way to work.

She didn’t want their first date to feel like work.

Your only date, a voice in her head had said. Rio physically waved it away, frowning.

Whether or not it would be their only date was immaterial to her. It had to be. She wanted to enjoy herself. And she really wanted Agatha to enjoy it. If it was the only time they could really spend together before Agatha left to go home, well…Rio was going to make sure it was memorable, at the very least.

So she replaced the polo with one of her punny shark shirts. It was just a picture of a cartoon shark against a white background that said, “Hey, Chums!” with a big, toothy grin.

As soon as Rio saw it in the mirror, she all but tore it off of her body, tossing it back in the general direction of her dresser.

“Stupid,” she muttered to herself. “So dumb.” She shook her head.

The next thing she tried was a simple tank top. It was olive green and the material was thin. Paired with her denim shorts and boots, she looked…decent. Casual. Fine.

Rio didn’t hate it.

She made her way into the bathroom and grabbed a hair tie, gathering her short hair into a messy ponytail with a few loose strands hanging out over her face and grinning at herself in the mirror. She looked like a dork.

Rio groaned and shook her head, taking a deep breath as she reached for the last pair of daily contacts. She got one in her right eye, but when she reached for the left one…

“Shit!” Rio huffed, trying to grab it before it went completely down the…

Yeah, it slipped down the drain.

“Fuuuuuuck you,” Rio groaned, closing her eyes as she pressed her forehead to the sink counter. Of course this would happen today. Why not?

She looked back up at her reflection and nearly started crying.

When she didn’t have her contacts or glasses on, she felt as though she could see just fine. Sure, she couldn’t exactly read that well, but why did she need to be able to read? She was a fisher and a physical therapist. She wore her contacts when she was on the job most of the time. But, even when she didn’t, she could at least see most things, even if they’re a tiny bit blurry.

When she had just one contact in, however, the difference between her two levels of vision was stark. It made her dizzy, and she had to close her eyes again.

“Dammit,” she murmured, opening her right eye and taking the contact right back out. There was no way she would be able to walk through the fair like that.

Unfortunately, the difference between 20/20 vision and her default vision was stark enough that now it was entirely too noticeable even after only having the contact in for a total of about twenty seconds.

Everything was fucking blurry.

Rio groaned (again) and stomped into her room like a grumpy toddler, snatching her glasses off of the bedside table and slipping them onto her face, sighing when the room around her came into focus.

She didn’t hate wearing her glasses. Not like she had when she was fourteen and first diagnosed with astigmatism and they were her only option because of her parents’ shitty insurance. She’d had a pair of thick, black-framed, square glasses that made her look like the nerdiest dork on the island. She had refused to wear them at first, but then her parents gave her ‘incentives’ in the form of a cell phone, an annual pass to the local aquarium so that she could spend as much time as possible looking at all the different sharks (her favorite pastime as a teenage dork), and a National Geographic motorized shark model, which she had had her eyes on as a possible Christmas present that year.

Her mother had promised to buy it for her early if she wore her glasses for the entirety of every school day for at least a full month. She promised that she’d check with all of her teachers to make sure that she was keeping up her end of the bargain.

Rio, ever the obedient dorky child obsessed with sharks, kept up her end of the bargain without fail. She got the shark model, aquarium pass, and cell phone over a month before Christmas.

For Christmas, she got LEGO sets, which she spent the entirety of her holiday break putting together with her dad.

They still lined the shelves in her apartment.

Thankfully, after a while of the big, dorky glasses, Rio was able to switch to a leaner frame in her favorite color: green. They didn’t feel as heavy on her face, and she didn’t get teased nearly as much for wearing them.

Kids were assholes, though, so the teasing didn’t stop entirely. But she at least had a girlfriend or two in high school – girls who were very interested in nerdy girls with glasses, though they weren’t as impressed with all the fun facts Rio knew about sharks.

Their loss.

Rio wondered what kind of girl Agatha had been in high school. Would she think the glasses were charming, or would they be an ick for her.

Only one way to find out…

Rio found one of the pairs of shark socks that Lilia had bought for her two birthdays ago and slipped them on, chuckling to herself as she stared down at them on her feet for a moment before stepping into her boots and lacing them up. If she couldn’t wear a shark shirt…well, at least her favorite animal would be repped, somehow.

God, she was a dork with a capital ‘D’.

She just hoped Agatha was the kind of woman who could appreciate that about her.

By the time she left her apartment, it was ten to six; she was going to be late.

Agatha had spent the majority of her day in the plaza, staring out at all the families having fun on rides, walking around the little stalls, and dancing to the music that Rio’s friend was playing with her band. The small children were the most energetic of the dancers, especially when Alice played certain upbeat songs that they seemed to know all the words to.

Agatha’s heart swelled with affection for them as she thought about the little ones in the Pod, how they giggled and raced each other, swimming around her and the other adults as they had fun. She remembered Billy and Tucker being that small at one point. Her other students, too.

She wondered if she’d ever have a child that small herself. She’d been encouraged to adopt one of the infants brought back to the Pod, but now…she didn’t know if she could raise a child that she knew had been stolen.

Agatha didn’t know how many Sirens were like Lilia: ignorant to the true nature of their most potent powers. How many of them knew exactly what they were doing and did it, anyway?

But what was she going to do on land? Teach? In the same community where her own birth mother apparently bullied teenagers for loving who they loved? How would she react to Agatha if she ever met her? Would she do the same? Would she treat Agatha cruelly just for being with other women? Would she continue to harass Rio if that was Agatha’s partner?

Did Agatha want to put Rio through that again?

Not that they would be together, of course. This was only their first date.

Their only date, probably.

She had told Jen about Rio, about their upcoming date and about what Lilia had told her about her own mother, how she was going to kill her even without the help of a Siren song.

“Are you sure she was telling the truth?” Jen had scoffed, disbelievingly, as Agatha helped her put on the outfit she’d bought at the thrift store.

“Why would she lie?” Agatha asked. “She already told you the truth about your mother.”

“And look what happened!” Jen retorted. “Do you think she wanted that reaction twice?!”

“Marisol corroborated,” Agatha pointed out. “Not because she was there but because she actually apparently knows my birth mother. She’s kind of a bitch. Oh, that’s a female dog, by the way.”

“What is?” Jen asked.

“A bitch,” Agatha explained. “It’s a female dog.”

“What’s a dog?” Jen asked, furrowing her brow.

“Those little fuzzy things that were walking around on four legs,” Agatha said. “There were a few in the plaza.”

“I thought those were called cats.”

“No. Cats are a bit different. Smaller and quieter, I think. They make different sounds than dogs.”

“Wow, you really learned a lot up here, didn’t you?” Jen asked.

Agatha nodded in agreement. “And there’s still so much more to learn,” she said. “I’ve been reading the books in Lilia’s library and there’s just…so much about this world I never knew. She told me that I was welcome to come back whenever I wanted to read more, too.”

“You can’t,” Jen said. “They’ll find out!”

Agatha had bristled at that. She’d never liked being told what she could do, let alone what she couldn’t do.

“So what?” she’d huffed. “What are they going to do? Exile me? At least I have somewhere I could go.”

“What if they went after Lilia, though?” Jen asked. “They all think she’s dead. They named a child after her, Agatha! How angry do you think they’ll be when they find out that she’s still alive and has simply abandoned the Pod?”

“They can’t really do anything to her either, can they?” Agatha snorted, stepping into the overalls that Lilia had bought her. The straps had rainbows on them and they attached just above a pocket on her chest. When she looked at herself in the mirror in their bedroom, she loved what she saw. She felt at home in this outfit.

She felt at home on this island.

“We don’t know what they’re capable of,” Jen huffed. She was wearing a pink shirt and a skirt from Agatha’s shopping ship a few days prior. “We didn’t think they were capable of mass kidnapping either, remember? What if they send somebody for her? What if they kill her?”

Agatha’s heart dropped at that.

As angry as she had been with Lilia the night before, she didn’t want the older woman to die. Lilia was the adult she had needed when she was young and being told that her curiosity was wrong, that who she was was a problem that needed to be solved, that she was going to get herself killed one day. Or worse.

Lilia would have probably encouraged that curiosity. She would have nurtured it. She would have answered all of Agatha’s questions without a doubt. The thought of anything bad happening to her…

“If there were any danger of them actually finding out, Lilia wouldn’t have extended the invitation,” she said, confidently. “And Lilia seems like she could handle her own, anyway.”

“Does she?” Jen huffed. She was obviously still bitter, but Agatha had noticed that she had softened somewhat since the night before. She might not have been fully speaking to Lilia, but she accepted the food Lilia had served her for lunch.

Ravenously.

“How did she learn to cook so damn well?” Jen had huffed through a mouthful of something Lilia had called pesto chicken pasta. She groaned as she ate more. “And why does human food taste so good.”

“Variety,” Lilia answered, coming into the kitchen with her own plate. She’d eaten in the other room to give Jen a little bit of space. “It’s the spice of life. All we had to eat down in the Pod was raw fish and eel and seaweed.” She made a face. “It wasn’t horrible, but it certainly wasn’t this.” She held up what was left on her plate, which was just a few smudges of the green sauce. “I started learning to cook well before I officially moved up here, however.”

Jen furrowed her brow. “Why?” she asked, forgetting that they weren’t on speaking terms.

Lilia visibly brightened at the question, but Agatha noticed that she was still holding back, trying not to show it.

“Because,” she answered, scooping more of the dish onto Jen’s plate, to her delight, “as a Siren, I was meant to integrate myself into society. I needed to know how to walk and talk and live amongst the humans despite being born and raised in the sea. They really hammered that in.”

“Hammered?” Agatha asked as Jen stuffed her mouth with more pasta and chicken, nearly choking. Agatha patted her back, but her focus was still on Lilia, her curiosity overpowering her concern for her friend.

Jen would be fine.

“A hammer is a tool,” Lilia explained. “We use it to build things. I’m surprised we never actually had them in the Pod, but I guess when one uses magic for pretty much everything, a hammer is unnecessary.”

“Magic?” Agatha snorted. “We don’t use magic for everything.”

“Well, you don’t,” Lilia said, “because you don’t have any, as a landborn. But the seaborns are a different breed. They use magic for everything.”

“I thought only Sirens have magic,” Jen said through a mouthful of food.

“We do,” Lilia confirmed, “because we’re sea-born. But our magic is quite different from the magic of the leaders and elders who were also born at sea.”

“How is magic ability determined?” Agatha asked.

“That is a very good question,” Lilia said. “One that I don’t quite have a definitive answer to, yet.” She started cleaning up the kitchen, scooping the leftovers from her pan into a container to place it all away into the fridge for a later meal. “Back before I first left the Pod for good with Marisol,” she said, “I had asked around to some of my confidants. Fellow Sirens that were older than even myself. Some that were younger, too, but children of elders who held different magic.”

“And what did you learn?” Agatha asked.

“Nerd,” Jen snorted, licking at her plate.

Agatha elbowed her in the ribs, but she didn’t take her eyes away from Lilia, who rolled her eyes affectionately at the two girls.

“I learned that training a seaborn in a certain type of magic takes away their ability to do other types of magic, but we’re all born with the capability to do every type of magic.”

“What are the other types of magic?” Agatha asked.

“Well, there’s Protection magic, for one,” Lilia said. “It’s one of the reasons humans cannot find the Pod even though we can.” She shrugged. “When we’re in our mermaid forms, at least.” She opened the fridge and stuck the leftovers inside, then turned to start washing the pan. “There’s also Potion magic, which is what we typically use to make the necklaces for transformation for the infants that we res—ahem,” she flushed under Jen’s hard gaze, “take,” she corrected, apologetically, “and for the necklaces we use to disguise ourselves amongst the humans.” She reached under the collar of her yellow blouse and pulled out a necklace that looked similar to both Jen’s and Agatha’s own. It was amber in color, and it shimmered even in the low light of the kitchen. “I actually learned a little bit of that in my training, for emergencies.”

“Is that why you were able to make me a necklace to return to the Pod?” Agatha asked, looking at the necklace Lilia had started working on the first day she arrived. It had been sitting in a pool of…something that smelled spicy, buried under a mixture of herbs and salt.

“Yes,” Lilia said. “Not that you need that one, anymore, now that Jennifer has kindly brought your own back to you. I knew you were a purple girl from the moment I saw you.”

“Do the colors mean anything?” Agatha asked.

Lilia walked over to the necklace that was still marinating in the leaves and salt water. She plucked at the chain, pulling out the stone to show them. It was pure white.

“Why does it look like that?” she asked as Lilia brought it closer. Jen also took an interest, tilting her head as she considered the stone that Lilia had laid in Agatha’s hand.

“It’s selenite,” Lilia informed them. “This is what all the stones are made from. This is how they all start out. White little chunks of rock. Nobody really knows why they change color, but within a year of wearing them, they just…do.” She shrugged. “I’m still trying to figure out why.”

“Maybe it’s just because they’re our favorite colors?” Jen suggested. “Or the color of our tails?”

Lilia shrugged. “I have no idea,” she said. “Maybe it’s random. Maybe not. Purple is one of the rarer colors, though.” She winked at Agatha, whose eyes widened.

Now that she mentioned it…

“Agatha?”

Agatha jumped, blinking down at Jen, who was back in her wheelchair, waiting on her.

“Where did you go just now?” she asked. “And why do you keep zoning out?”

“I was thinking about the stones again,” Agatha replied, her face flushing as she pulled hers out of the chest pocket on her overalls. The color looked a little duller than she remembered. Maybe it was starting to lose color.

Would that make it less effective?

“Why?” Jen asked. Her own stone was tucked into the pocket of her skirt. Agatha had caught her sticking her hand in her pocket several times just to make sure that it was still there.

She didn’t want to get stuck up here like Agatha had been, after all. Though Jen seemed to be getting used to living on land.

She still couldn’t walk, but she’d caught on to the use of the wheelchair pretty easily. She didn’t need Agatha or Lilia to push it for her now.

“Just thinking about it,” Agatha responded. “Just wondering why mine is purple and yours is pink and Lilia’s is yellow.”

“Well, Lilia said her tail was yellow,” Jen said, so I’m certain that’s all it is. They just match the colors of our tails.” She turned the chair toward the door. “Now, come on. You promised to take me around the fair for a bit before you abandon me for your date.”

“Don’t be bitter,” Agatha snorted. “And it’s probably not a date. We’re just…hanging out. We did that yesterday, too.”

“Uh-huh,” Jen said, “and Lilia said you two almost kissed yesterday, too.” She waggled her eyebrows at Agatha, who reached out and tugged her beanie down over her eyes before walking away. “Hey! You bitch!”

“Ladies, no fighting please,” Lilia said, coming down the stairs as she put on a pair of earrings. Agatha had noticed that Lilia was partial to jewelry. They also wore it in the Pod, mostly to show off some of the precious stones they found or the shiny little metal pieces that merchants had found and fashioned into fun shapes. It was one of the few forms of self-expression they had besides their hair and the occasional scraps of clothing they’d don. Lilia’s earrings and necklaces and bracelets were all interesting colors and shapes, far more interesting than anything that could be found in the Pod.

“Agatha started it,” Jen said, immediately turning back into the tattling five-year-old she used to be when they first started their schooling.

“Liar!” Agatha shot back.

Jen stuck out her tongue.

Lilia just stared at them with a disapproving look, one eyebrow raised. They quieted, their shoulders hunching slightly.

“Now,” Lilia said, “do you two think you can behave in the plaza on your own for a bit or do I have to accompany you the whole way there?”

“We’re not babies, Lilia,” Agatha huffed. “We’re thirty years old!”

“Yeah,” Jen agreed. “We can behave ourselves!”

“I have my doubts…” Lilia said, slowly. “But okay. I will trust you. But Marisol will be there, and she’ll tell me if you cause any trouble.”

“What’s Marisol going to be doing there?” Agatha asked.

“They asked her to be the medic on-call,” Lilia said, “just in case something bad happens and somebody needs assistance.”

“Does that usually happen at these ‘fair’s?” Jen asked.

“Sometimes,” Lilia said. “Not often. But we have quite the elderly population on this island. Humans’ bodies tend to fail them as they get older.”

“Does your body—”

“No,” Lilia said, glaring at Agatha as she gave her a cheeky smile. “And I’m not old for a Siren, even in my human form. I will outlive every old windbag in this bitch.” She reached into her pocket. “Now stop being a cheeky little shit or you won’t have a penny for ice cream or whatever rides you were planning on going on today.” She held up a small wad of bills.

Agatha immediately pressed her lips together. She didn’t know as much about the surface as she would like, yet, but she knew that she couldn’t get really anywhere without even just a little bit of money – and usually more than just a little bit, even just for ice cream.

“What’s ice cream?” Jen asked. “What’s that?”

“Shut up,” Agatha huffed, elbowing her in the shoulder.

“Quit that!” Jen growled, poking her between the ribs. It was something she used to do when they were kids and still did just to annoy Agatha. It made her squawk just as much now as it did when she was six.

“Girls,” Lilia said in a dry voice.

“Sorry,” they said in unison. Agatha tucked her hands into the pockets at her hips and Jen folded hers in her lap. They had the decency to look contrite.

Lilia just sighed and handed Agatha the cash. “That’s eighty,” she said. “Don’t overspend it, please. The rides should each cost no more than two dollars per person. Don’t spend more than ten dollars at any of the stalls. Don’t buy any food other than the ice cream. Marisol and I are going to buy you dinner at the diner.”

Agatha raised her hand.

“Yes, Agatha?” Lilia said, unable to keep herself from smirking just the tiniest bit.

“I think I have dinner plans,” she said. “With Rio.”

Lilia’s smile spread a little bit wider. “Oh,” she said, “that’s right.”

“She’s abandoning me,” Jen added, with a pout.

“Poor you,” Lilia tutted. “But you’ll have a few hours to spend together, at least, so maybe you’ll have some fun, after all.”

“As much fun as I can have,” Jen huffed, “in this stupid chair.” She rolled past Lilia toward the door, ignoring the little whispered “goodbye” Lilia gave her.

Lilia turned a frown toward Agatha, who shrugged. “She needs a little more time,” she said. “But Jen never really holds a grudge for that long.”

That was a lie. Jen was the queen of holding grudges.

But Lilia didn’t need to know that.

Besides, Agatha was hoping that she could spend the next couple of hours talking Lilia up to Jen. Agatha could be very manipulative when she wanted to be.

No, not manipulative, she thought.

Convincing.

Not much better.

She let Lilia give her a small hug and offered a reassuring smile of her own before following Jen to the door and opening it for her. Jen raced right out, but Agatha turned back to give Lilia a tentative smile and a thumbs up. Lilia just waved in return, a sad look on her face.

Agatha took a deep breath and stepped out into the sun.

It was four-thirty and the plaza was already bustling with activity. Little kids were running around, holding colorful clouds on sticks and giggling. Agatha’s eyes widened at that as she watched them go. Sometimes, one would stop and tear off a piece of cloud, shoving it into their mouths with a happy smile as they parents or friends hurried them along.

Agatha stopped one girl with a cloud the size of her head.

“Excuse me,” she said. “What’s that called?”

“Cotton candy!” the little girl chirped before taking off after her friends.

“Cotton candy,” Agatha echoed. “Whoa, that looks really good.”

“Lilia told us not to eat,” Jen reminded her. “Except for the eyes cream.”

“The what?” Agatha snorted, looking down at her.

“The eyes cream,” Jen repeated. “Don’t act like you don’t know what that is! You’ve had it before! I haven’t!”

“It’s ice cream,” Agatha laughed. “Not eyes cream.”

“What’s the difference?”

“These,” Agatha pointed to her face, “are eyes. Ice is that thing that Lilia put in our sweet tea at lunch.”

“Why would you get so excited about eating that?” Jen huffed.

“That’s not what ice cream is,” Agatha groaned. “Ice cream is so much better! They have oyster ice cream here.”

“I…what? What does that mean?”

“It means it tastes like oysters!” Agatha exclaimed, raising her hands and gaining some attention from the people around them, to which she paid no mind. “Which is better than even mint chocolate chip!”

“What the hell is mint chocolate chip?” Jen asked, furrowing her brow.

“Oh, you have so much left to learn.” Agatha snorted. “Come on.”

She grabbed the handles of Jen’s wheelchair and started pushing her toward a stall in front of the ice cream shop, where Scott and one of his workers – the gangly teen boy from the other day – were serving two lines of people. He gave Agatha a wave as she joined his line. She waved back.

“You know him?” Jen asked.

“That’s Scott,” Agatha replied. “Rio doesn’t like him.”

“But we do?”

“He has the ice cream,” Agatha said. “We tolerate him.”

“…okay.”

Jen ended up loving the oyster ice cream.

“It tastes like home,” she said, a little misty-eyed. “Why am I leaking again?” She sniffled, swiping furiously at her eyes and looking at Agatha as if she had caused this.

Agatha had just shrugged as she scarfed down her own ice cream cone, not wanting to make a mess like she had the last time.

But then came a whole new problem.

Brain freeze.

That’s what Marisol had called it, anyway, when she’d come to Agatha’s side after she dropped the remnants of her cone after her head felt as though it were going to explode and she let out a pained shout.

“Don’t eat your ice cream too quickly, querida,” she said. “It’ll freeze the nerves on the roof of your mouth.”

“Why?” Agatha groaned. “Why do humans serve food that both burns us and freezes us?”

“Because it tastes good,” Marisol had laughed. “Here, just take your thumb and press it to the roof of your mouth.”

Agatha did as she was told, pressing the pad of her thumb to the roof of her mouth and waiting. After a few seconds, the pain in her head diminished and she breathed a sigh of relief, her tensed shoulders relaxing.

“Much better,” she said, looking at Marisol. “Thank you.”

“Anytime, querida,” Marisol replied, affectionately. “Have fun, you two.”

Agatha frowned at the splattered ice cream cone at her feet.

“I’ll try,” she said, genuinely holding back tears at the ruined treat. She hadn’t even reached the mint chocolate chip part of the cone.

“Here,” Jen said, holding out her own. “Take it.”

“What?” Agatha asked. “Really?” She reached cautiously for the ice cream. “You don’t want it?”

“The oyster one was good,” Jen said, “but whatever that green stuff is hurts my tongue. I don’t like it.”

“Really?” Agatha asked. “You’re missing out.”

“Poor me,” Jen deadpanned, then she furrowed her brow. “What’s that sound?” She turned her wheelchair toward the stage, where Alice was currently singing into the microphone, some song about losing her love and trying to win her back. People were dancing and nodding their heads to the beat. A bunch of little kids were bouncing around in the middle of the dance floor while she smiled down at them and bounced on her own toes. She was dressed in a dark shirt with a collar and short sleeves. There was something swinging around on her neck, too. It had stripes in orange and white. Her hair was up in two buns on either side of her head.

The second Jen’s eyes landed on her, Agatha could see her entire demeanor shift, her cheeks darkening.

“Who is that?” she asked, her voice barely above a whisper. Agatha almost couldn’t hear her.

“That’s Alice,” Agatha said. “She’s a friend of Rio’s.”

“She’s…very pretty.”

Agatha looked back at Alice. Like Rio, she had deep brown eyes and black hair, but that was where the similarities ceased. Where Rio was a tiny bit taller than Agatha, Alice was significantly shorter than both of them – and especially shorter than Jen. Her smile was just as wide as Rio’s, but there was no gap between her front teeth. She had higher cheekbones, as well.

Yeah, Agatha could see that she was attractive, naturally, but she wasn’t Rio. She could absolutely see what Jen saw in her, though.

She glanced back at Jen, whose amber eyes were tracking every movement Alice made across that stage, her lips parted just slightly.

Agatha gave her a knowing grin.

“Would you like to go and watch the pretty girl perform?” she asked.

“Shut up,” Jen grumbled, swiping at the ice cream cone in Agatha’s hand. Agatha pulled it away with a grin.

“Well?” she said. “Would you?”

Jen glared at her. “Yes.”

“Let’s go,” Agatha said, still licking her ice cream as she led Jen to the stage area.

They ended up spending the next hour watching Alice perform. Jen was enraptured the entire time, her cheeks darkening every time Alice so much as glanced in her direction. Agatha watched her in amusement as she finished off her ice cream.

She tried to get Jen to go look at some of the vendors’ booths that were lined up adjacent to the stage or even to check out some of the rides on the other side of the plaza, but Jen was fully focused on Alice and her band.

And it seemed like Alice had noticed her, as well. Agatha could see the pink flush that spread across the other woman’s cheeks every time she and Jen locked eyes. Agatha might have teased her if she didn’t fully recognize that look in her eyes from just the previous evening.

“You sure you don’t want to do anything else?” she asked Jen. “I have about fifteen minutes left until I need to meet Rio. And Lilia should be here soon to bring you to dinner.”

“I don’t want to go to dinner with Lilia,” Jen huffed, finally tearing her gaze away from Alice at the sound of the other woman’s name. “She kidnapped me.”

Agatha opened her mouth to defend Lilia, but, before she could, she felt the bench seat she was sitting on shift a little bit and turned to see Rio seated next to her, grinning shyly in her direction. Agatha felt her own cheeks start to burn now and felt a soft nudge in her side.

She turned to glare at Jen, who was giving her a cheesy grin.

“Hey,” she said, turning back to offer Rio a friendly grin in return.

“Hey,” Rio greeted back. Her eyes flickered to Jen. “Who’s this?”

“Oh,” Agatha said. “Nobody. Just Jen. Friend of Lilia’s.”

“Oh, of course,” Rio said, reaching around Jen to offer her hand. “Nice to meet you,” she said. “I’m Rio. Rio Vidal”

“Hello, Rio,” Jen said. “I’m Jennifer. Jennifer…” She furrowed her brow, glancing at Agatha for help.

“Kale,” Agatha blurted out, remembering the salad that Lilia had made for her a few days ago. It had something called kale in it. Kale was absolutely disgusting, but it should work as a last name. At least for now. It wasn’t like Jen was going to have it for very long, anyway. “Jen Kale.”

“Yes,” Jen said. “My name is Jen Kale.” She gave Agatha an odd look, but her smile widened as she looked back at Rio. “Nice to meet you. Agatha tells me you’re friends with,” she looked back up at the stage, her eyes lighting up with warmth as her voice became breathier, “Alice.”

“Since school,” Rio confirmed. “She’s a pretty good musician, huh?”

“Yeah,” Jen sighed, grinning up at Alice as she bounced around the stage. “Really pretty.”

Agatha smothered a snort with the back of her hand. She caught even Rio biting her lip to keep from laughing as they all watched Alice come to the end of her set. The shorter woman picked up a towel from the edge of the stage and wiped the sweat from her face as she got a round of applause.

“Thank you all so much!” she said. “Let’s give it up for Coven! Big round of applause for my lovely band!” There was a lot of applause for the band. “Amazing!” she said. “Well, I’m going to take a short break so that I can hydrate and feed myself, but I will be back soon to get the party truly started! In the meantime, please feel free to visit our vendors or even get on a ride! The lines are pretty short right now, so you should get in while you can! I’ll be back in twenty!”

She placed the microphone back on the stand and turned to her bandmates, clasping hands and giving hugs before she turned back toward the audience and hopped down from the stage.

“Hey, Alice!” Rio called out, holding something out that Agatha hadn’t even realized she was holding.

“Popcorn!” Alice exclaimed like an excited child. “Sweet!” She practically skipped over to Rio, taking the back of popcorn from her and shoving a handful of the puffy white stuff into her mouth, her cheeks puffing up as she chewed.

Agatha wrinkled her nose in disgust and looked over at Jen, whose eyes were practically alight as she continued to stare at the other woman, who was doing a little happy dance as she ate her treat.

“You are really down bad, huh?” Agatha asked.

“I’m down what?” Jen asked, her eyes barely leaving Alice as they flickered to Agatha and back. “What does that mean?”

“I’m not fully sure,” Agatha said. “I heard some teenagers say it yesterday when they were making fun of their friend for having a crush on some boy or something. I don’t really know how that became the phrase for that.”

“Kinda how you’re down bad for Rio?” Jen retorted, grinning knowingly.

“Shut up!” Agatha grumbled, elbowing her in the shoulder. Jen elbowed her back, and Agatha flicked her in the ear before scooting away on the bench.

“You’re a child,” Jen huffed, but her face brightened as she turned back to Alice just in time to watch as the other woman approached her with a shy grin.

“Hey,” she greeted, her cheeks pink, “my name’s Alice.”

“I’m Jen,” Jen said, swallowing thickly. “Really nice to meet you.”

“Really nice?” Agatha murmured under her breath, teasingly.

Jen sent her a sharp look and Agatha held up her hands in mock surrender as both she and Rio snickered, watching as Alice sat on the bench next to Jen’s wheelchair. She offered her the bag.

“Would you like some popcorn?” she asked.

Jen smiled and took a small handful, shoving the whole thing into her mouth like she’d seen Alice do just moments ago. Next to her, Rio practically choked on thin air. Agatha turned to furrow her brow at the other woman as she covered her mouth, coughing and snorting, obviously amused.

Okay, so there was something that Agatha wasn’t really understanding, but she didn’t mention it. Instead, she just let out a forced laugh of her own as she turned back to watch Alice stare down at Jen, her cheeks just as pink as before as she watched her eat the puffy white treat. She looked absolutely enamored.

Just as much as Jen did.

Just then, Agatha spotted Lilia and Marisol walking in their direction. Marisol spotted Jen and Alice first and leaned in to point them out to Lilia, who nodded, saying something that Agatha couldn’t quite make out, but then they were heading straight for them. Agatha jumped to her feet and excused herself for a second as she ran toward them, holding her hands up to stop them.

“Hey,” she said. “Hold on.”

“Wow, you’ve really got a hang of the running thing, too, now?” Marisol hummed, impressed. “Good on you, querida!”

Agatha flushed a little bit, pleased by the compliment. “Oh,” she said, looking down at her feet in the royal purple converse she’d paired with her overalls. “Thanks!” She cleared her throat. “Anyway, I know you were planning on bringing Jen out to dinner at the diner, but, um, I don’t think she’s quite comfortable with that just yet. She’s still a little miffed about the whole ‘kidnapping’ thing, you know? Besides, I think she and Alice are…” she glanced back at where Jen and Alice were tossing popcorn at each other and attempting to catch it in their mouths as Rio held her phone up in front of her face, laughing at something on it, “…bonding? Courting? I have no idea. But I think they’d rather not be interrupted right now.”

Both Marisol and Lilia glanced around Agatha to see the two women giggling as they pelted each other with kernels of popcorn, their faces each melting into a soft smile. Lilia even rolled her eyes, affectionately.

“Alright then,” she said. “We’ll go buy ourselves dinner while you all do your little ‘courting’ thing. Have fun, doll!” She leaned in, pressing a kiss to Agatha’s cheek. “Treat my niece right, you got that?”

Agatha nodded, turning to Marisol, who was still looking past Agatha’s shoulder.

“Go wait for me at the diner,” she told Lilia. “I gotta say something to Rio real quick.”

Lilia turned a suspicious gaze on her.

“I’ll be right there!” Marisol said. “But the dummy isn’t wearing her contacts.”

“How can you tell?” Lilia asked.

“Look at how much she’s squinting right now.” Marisol tutted. “I need to go knock some sense into her.”

Lilia let out a cackle. “Alright,” she said. “You go ahead. I’ll place our orders.”

“Thanks, Lils,” Marisol said, affectionately, waving to her as she followed Agatha back to the small group they’d formed. “Lilia was right,” Marisol said, her voice low as she followed Agatha back to the dance floor. “You better be good to my baby. I don’t want any broken hearts tonight.”

“I would never hurt Rio,” Agatha replied immediately. “I…I really like her.”

“I know you do,” Marisol sighed. “But that’s not really enough if you don’t intend on staying.” She paused. “Have you thought anymore about it yet?”

“I’ve thought of nothing but staying,” Agatha answered, honestly. “I still just don’t know.”

“Well, figure it out as soon as you can,” Marisol pleaded. “Because I know my baby: she falls hard and she falls fast, but her heart doesn’t heal quite so easily.”

Agatha nodded in understanding.

“Mami?” Rio grinned over the top of her phone. “What are you doing here?” she asked. “I thought your shift ended already!”

“I can’t say hello to my only baby before I go grab dinner?” Marisol scoffed, opening her arms. Rio felt no embarrassment as she ran straight into her hold, letting out a soft grunt as Marisol’s arms wrapped so tightly around her that they squeezed the air from her lungs.

Thankfully, she was used to this.

“You can always say hi, Mami,” Rio huffed, pressing a kiss to her mother’s temple. “But why are you here, really?” She pulled back to give her mother a suspicious glance. Marisol Vidal was definitely there with an ulterior motive. Rio could see it in her eyes.

Then her mother flicked her between her eyes.

“Where are your contacts?” she demanded.

“I ran out,” Rio replied, pouting as she pulled back and rubbed the spot between her eyes.

“Okay, so then where are your glasses?” Marisol demanded, folding her arms over her chest. Rio was a full four inches taller than her mother, but she still felt like a little kid under that disapproving ‘Mom’ look.

Her shoulders slumped as she reached into her pants pocket and pulled out the wire-framed glasses that she was meant to wear whenever she didn’t have contacts to help her see. She held them up for Marisol’s approval.

“I’ve got them right here,” she murmured, keeping her voice low.

“That’s nice,” Marisol replied, at a volume that was definitely audible to those around them – including Agatha and Jen. “Put them on before you get a headache. I don’t wanna hear any complaints from you at the end of the night. Claro?”

Rio let out a sigh at that. “Sí, Mami,” she said, opening the frames and slipping them onto her face. Her frown deepened as Marisol gave her a satisfied grin.

“Mi bebé, tan guapa,” she cooed, pinching Rio’s cheek.

Rio couldn’t help but snort out a laugh as she batted her mother’s hand away. “Mamiiiiii,” she groaned, pouting again.

“Fix your face,” Marisol chastised lightly. “It’ll get stuck like that.”

“Then I’ll just have a terminal case of Blue Steel,” Rio said, pulling another face, putting her hand up to hold her chin between her thumb and index finger.

Marisol snorted and shoved her shoulder lightly. “Be good, mija,” she said. “You big goof.”

“Te amo, Mami,” Rio called out after her as Marisol walked away toward the diner. Marisol blew her a kiss.

“Te amo, mi alma,” she called back.

Rio was still smiling as she turned back to her friends.

“What’s that?” Agatha asked, motioning to her face.

Rio’s face went hot. She honestly hadn’t expected Agatha – or anybody, for that matter – to mention the dorky-looking glasses so quickly. She pushed them up her nose as she walked toward them. Jen and Alice were still giggling as they shared the popcorn Rio had bought for Alice, but Agatha’s gaze was fully on her now.

“My glasses,” Rio replied, trying to sound casual if just a tiny bit bashful. “I ran out of contacts this morning, so I am cursed to look like a dork for the rest of the weekend until I can get more from the pharmacy on Monday.”

“You don’t look like a dork,” Agatha replied, giving her a bit of a lopsided grin. “You look like…Rio.” She shrugged, her cheeks turning pink as she finished her sentence. “You look cute.”

Rio’s grin must have been equally as bashful. She could feel the heat of a thousand suns in her face. She scratched the back of her neck and didn’t miss the way Agatha’s ocean-blue eyes tracked the flex of her biceps.

Okay, the tank top had been a good choice.

Agatha didn’t know what it was about the glasses that Rio was wearing, but she really, really liked them. They sort of magnified Rio’s eyes, making the brown irises even bigger and the little flecks of green in them even more apparent. There was even a little gold there when the sun hit them just right.

She and Rio had left Jen and Alice at the dance floor, talking throughout Alice’s break, still sharing that same bag of popcorn. They’d barely even glanced up when Agatha and Rio said goodbye. Agatha left Jen with a twenty to get something to eat, but Alice had said that she’d be the one to treat her, waving them both off.

Agatha had simply shaken her head with a snort as she and Rio headed off in the direction of the rides and games, where Rio bought them each a paper bracelet that had some kind of…picture? Agatha had no idea, but Rio used her phone to scan the code on each of their bracelets.

“This should get us on rides for the next couple of hours,” she told Agatha. “I know they’re mostly kiddie rides, but they’re kind of fun.”

Her cheeks were pink again. Agatha felt those stupid flutters in her stomach. She tried her best to ignore them, but they increased in intensity as Rio grabbed her hand and linked their fingers together, lightly tugging her in the direction of a ride that had a lot of strange creatures on it. There were poles going through each animal.

Rio and Agatha showed their wristbands to a teenager attending the ride, who gave Rio a cheesy grin as he scanned each of their wrists.

“Nice to see you looking well, Miss Agatha,” he said.

Agatha furrowed her brow. Was this one of her students? It couldn’t be! He had legs! And while he was just mildly familiar, she didn’t really recognize him all that much.

“This is Edison,” Rio said.

“Not my name,” Edison interjected.

“He works on my boat with me,” she explained, “when he’s not abandoning me to earn some extra cash at a carnival.”

“Alice abandoned you, too,” Edison pointed out. He smiled at Agatha. “My name is Eddie, by the way.”

Agatha didn’t really understand the difference between the two names, but okay. “Nice to meet you, Edy,” she said as Rio tugged her onto the ride, ignoring his protestations and Rio’s snorts.

“Pick whichever horse you want,” Rio said.

“Horse?” Agatha asked, looking up at an animal that had a much longer neck as well as a noticeable hump on its back.

“Well, in a manner of speaking,” Rio snorted. She tugged Agatha around the ride, where there were even more unusual animals. Agatha had seen pictures of horses in the books Lilia let her read – none of these were horses. She did, however, recognize an elephant, a giraffe, a lion, a dolphin, and a—

“Shark!” she gasped, racing toward it.

“Aw, no fair!” Rio practically whined. “I would have loved that one!”

“You snooze, you lose!” Agatha taunted, lightly. She struggled to get on the creature as it was a little higher up than she could reach.

She suddenly felt warm hands on her waist, lifting her up to sit on the strange, colorful thing on the shark’s back. She looked back down to see Rio with her hands on her, giving her a shy grin as she helped Agatha get situated. Then she wrapped something around her and fastened it into place before backing up to sit on the dolphin next to her. She wrapped her own belt around herself.

“Ready?” she asked as Eddard passed by, tugging on the belts. He said something into the strange, bulky cellphone in his hand before giving them a thumbs up and heading back to his previous spot at the gate to the ride. He stood in front of a machine.

“For what?” Agatha asked, suddenly feeling nervous.

“Just wait,” Rio said, eyeing Edwin. “Three…two…one!”

The ride came alive with some kind of jaunty tune, the lights above their head going wild as the shark under Agatha’s body started to fall, then rise as they lurched forward. She let out a surprised laugh and clung to the pole in front of her, holding on for dear life so as not to fall off.

Next to her, Rio laughed. But not at her.

Her laughter was somewhat unhinged, but it wasn’t mocking or mean. It was full of joy and mirth, her eyes alight as they went faster and faster, the wind whipping through their hair. Agatha’s was down that day, free from the braids that Lilia had been putting them in for her physical therapy to keep it out of her face. It was wild as it flapped around behind her. Some strands of coal black hair fell from the messy bun that Rio’s hair had been pulled into as well. Agatha grinned sideways at her, laughing just as hard as Rio, as her fingers clenched around the pole going straight through the shark’s body.

By the end of the ride, they were both messy-haired and giggling like children, their cheeks pink and smiles goofy as they came to a stop.

“What’d you think of that?” Rio asked. “Fun, right?”

“Very fun!” Agatha agreed. “Can we go again?”

“Absolutely!” Rio exclaimed, waving to Edmond as he came around to help some of the younger kids off of their animals.

“You guys going again?” he asked, approaching them.

“Yeah,” Rio said. “Is that alright? Or is the line too long now?”

Eddie turned to look out toward where a line had formed around the perimeter of the ride. “Nah,” he said, “I think you’re good. Just let me scan your wristbands first.”

Agatha and Rio held out their wrists for him, and he gave them the okay before heading off to start letting more people in.

“You want to switch?” Agatha asked.

“Switch?” Rio asked, furrowing her brow.

“Yeah,” Agatha said. “You haven’t had a chance to ride the shark yet. I can take the dolphin.”

The look Rio gave her made the flutters in Agatha’s stomach stretch up to her heart.

Rio might have been in love.

Was it too soon to say that? Probably.

Did she care? Not at all.

But she wouldn’t say it out loud yet, lest she scare Agatha away.

She slipped off the dolphin and helped Agatha down from the shark, helping her onto her new creature on the merry-go-round before hopping up onto the shark like a kid at Christmas hopping up on Santa’s lap with a whole list of things to ask for.

She knew she looked like a big old dork grinning from ear to ear as she waited for the ride to begin, but she didn’t care. Because Agatha was giving her a smile that wasn’t teasing or mocking or mean by any stretch of the imagination.

It was straight-up adoring.

And that was all that mattered.

Chapter 15

Chapter Summary

Fair Date Part 2: Electric Bougaloo

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Chapter Fifteen

Rio was correct: the rides at the fair were very fun.

Agatha found herself giggling like she hadn’t since she was a small child. Maybe not even then! She couldn’t remember ever laughing so hard as they went from ride to ride, the two oldest people there without any children holding their hands like the other adults.

They got a few odd looks from the others around them, but Rio paid them no mind, so Agatha didn’t either. They went on a ride that swung them around in the air for a couple of minutes and made Agatha feel as though she were flying. Then they went on a ride that looked like an eel. Agatha and Rio both had to press their knees against their chests as it was clearly made for smaller bodies, but they raised their hands and cheered as they went over the little hills, anyway. The children giggled at them, but again nobody mocked them.

Then there was a ride that wasn’t really a ride so much as Agatha and Rio controlling little boats – except these boats were on dry land. Agatha didn’t really know how the mechanics worked. They each got their own. Agatha got a purple one and Rio hopped into a green one. They each had two levers that one could push or pull. Agatha didn’t really understand how it all worked, so she ended up going around in circles for the entirety of the first time she rode it, not able to stop until the ride itself was powered down.

Agatha sat there, dizzy, for a long moment until Rio was at her side, asking if she was okay through her giggles, which she was very obviously attempting to suppress.

“I’m okay,” Agatha replied, dazedly. “I’m fine.” She blinked at Rio’s grinning face and found herself snorting out a laugh as well. “Can we try that again?” she asked.

“Of course, querida,” Rio said, flushing slightly when she realized what she’d just said. Agatha didn’t point it out, but she felt the flutters in her heart again, and she had to bite her lip to keep herself from giggling like a lovesick teenager.

They waited in line for a second time, and this time Agatha did a bit better on the ride. She was able to go in straight lines and turn, but she still spun for a bit just because it was fun.

Rio did the same, her glasses practically falling off her face when the ride stopped. They both giggled at that.

They stumbled dizzily away from the rides after that, instead heading toward what Rio referred to as games. There were more creatures there, except these ones were soft-looking and hanging above their heads.

Agatha saw one with a toothy smile, whiskers, and a pair of long, floppy ears hanging down next to its face. Its coloring was odd for an animal (deep violet) but Agatha kind of loved it.

“You like that?” Rio murmured in her ear as they walked, hand-in-hand past the game with the…creature. “You want me to win that for you?”

Rio’s voice was lower than usual and sent shivers down Agatha’s spine as her lips practically brushed the shell of her ear. She swallowed thickly, turning back to Rio and finding her mere inches away. She nodded, the tips of their noses practically brushing.

Rio gave her that grin again. “As you wish,” she said.

She turned away from Agatha and grinned at the man overlooking the game, which consisted of…well, she didn’t really know. There were guns, she saw, which she had read about in one of Lilia’s books. But the guns didn’t look dangerous. They were clear and obviously filled with water. They were also attached to a long counter via hoses. Straight ahead of each gun was a red disk with a hole in the center. Above that were those creatures again – horses – all on top of one another, all facing to the right. They were all different colors and they had numbers written on them.

Rio let go of her hand to pay the man standing above them before she sat down on one of the stools and grabbed her gun. A few others stopped to also pay and play the game. There were a few kids that tugged their parents’ hands, dragging them over to either pay the man the money for them to play the game or to play the game for them.

In the end, there were about a half dozen players.

“On your marks,” the man said, in a loud booming voice, as he flicked a couple switches on the counter behind him. “Get set…” He looked around at all the players. Rio held her gun steady, leaning forward. “Go!”

Some kind of jaunty tune played loud over them, drowning out pretty much every other nearby sound. Then there was a loud boom and the horses started to move forward as a stream of water came out of every player’s gun.

Agatha watched in awe as Rio shot the water straight at the hole in the middle of her own red disk. The horse numbered 11 – her horse, Agatha surmised since the same number was on her gun – raced forward, outpacing the other players to get to a little sign that said ‘Finish Line’. Rio’s horse was white with brown spots and the rider on the back wore a green hat. Agatha’s heart galloped just as quickly as the horses did as she watched them go. She found her breathing getting shallower, but not necessarily in a bad way.

She was excited. She found herself clapping her hands and cheering Rio on as she hit her target with startling accuracy.

“Neck and neck!” the man said into a microphone. “Speckles is up front, but Blue is gaining on him.” A gray horse with a darker mane was just an inch behind Rio, but Rio paid it no mind as she seemed laser-focused on winning.

Which she did.

A bell rang above their heads when ‘Speckles’ reached the finish line, and Rio raised her arms in victory, letting out a whoop.

“Victory for Speckles!” the man said. “Congratulations! You can pick any of the prizes hanging right here in front of you.” He motioned to the creatures with floppy ears and a few others that looked like snakes with legs or, vaguely, like dogs.

“Go ahead and pick one,” Rio said. “Anyone you want.” She gave her a wide grin.

Agatha grinned back and pointed at the purple creature. Rio nodded at the man who used some kind of long stick to unhook the stuffed creature from the ceiling and lower it down to her.

“There you go, little lady,” he said. “You got yourself a champion right there.”

“Thanks, Mr. Coulson,” Rio snorted, taking Agatha’s hand again. “Nice to see you.”

“You too, Rio. You be good!” The older man waved at them as they left.

“You know him?” Agatha asked, allowing herself to be tugged along.

“He was my high school English teacher,” Rio informed her. “Pretty cool guy. Bit of a goof, but a good teacher.”

“Why is a high school teacher working here?” Agatha asked.

Rio shrugged. “Summers can be tough for teachers, money-wise, can’t they? Some just need a little extra income to get through the hotter months until they can go back to their classrooms.” She furrowed her brow at Agatha. “Aren’t you a teacher?” she asked. “Don’t you take extra jobs in the summer?”

“I teach during the summer, too,” Agatha said, which was the truth. Besides a week here and there throughout the year, most students were in school most days, which meant that Agatha was in school most days, teaching them. Their schooling only ended when they were assigned their more permanent positions and could start training for said jobs.

Agatha had gone to school until she was sixteen, when she was first assigned to be a scout, only to be reassigned as a teacher when she proved that she couldn’t abide by the guidelines set for her.

Still, she had finished her formal schooling earlier than most in her age group. Earlier than Jen, even. Jen hadn’t started her healing training until a year later. Agatha could always hold that over her head.

“Summer school?” Rio asked. “Bummer. I had to do summer school once, but it wasn’t my fault.” Agatha tilted her head curiously at Rio, which she must have mistaken for skepticism because she laughed. “Really!” she said. “I did not belong there, but my asshole principal—”

“Your principal?” Agatha asked, remembering what Marisol had said about the principal at the local high school.

Her mother.

Rio let out a groan. “Yeah, her,” she said. “She was kind of…a raging bitch. No offense to your principal if they’re not like that, I mean. She just…didn’t like me.”

“Why not?” Agatha asked even though she kind of already knew why not.

“Because I was a lesbian,” Rio answered. “Still am, I mean.” She chuckled to herself. “But she really didn’t like that about me. She did everything she could to take out her hatred for the gays on me.”

“The gays?” Agatha echoed.

“Yeah, I know,” Rio said, “she was ridiculous. I’m glad to be out of high school and away from her.” She smiled down at Agatha. “So,” she said, “what are you going to name your bunny there?”

“Oh,” Agatha said, absorbing the name she’d called it. “I have no idea.”

The bunny wasn’t as soft as it looked, but it had a pair of big, black, fathomless eyes that reminded her of somebody.

“Scratchy,” she said, decisively.

“Scratchy,” Rio echoed. “Good name. Mucho gusto, Señor Scratchy.” She picked up one of the stuffed creature’s arms and shook it like she was shaking somebody’s hand.

Agatha snorted and rolled her eyes, affectionately.

Then her gaze snagged on something a few feet away.

“What’s that?” she asked, pointing to a large, rectangular box that had pictures of people’s smiling faces on it.

Rio followed the line of her finger and grinned.

“Holy crap!” she said. “A photobooth! I haven’t seen one of those in years!” She looked back at Agatha. “Wanna try it?”

“Um, sure,” Agatha said, still studying the pictures that littered the outside of the box as they approached it.

 There were pictures in sets of four. The same two or three people making funny faces or smiling brightly or, in the case of one pair of a man and a woman, kissing. Agatha felt her cheeks go hot pink as Rio put a few dollar bills into a flashing slot on the outside of the box before guiding her inside.

Señor Scratchy, unfortunately, was too big to fit inside with them, so Agatha left him right outside the little piece of fabric that separated the inside of the box from the outside.

Rio slid in first with Agatha right along after her. The space was so tight that Agatha was practically half on Rio’s lap, their feet tangled together. She felt – and saw – her cheeks turn red on the screen in front of her. There were more examples of what to do that flashed across the screen, as well as the words “Press START When Ready”.

Rio reached up, loosening her hair from the tie that been holding it up and running her fingers through it. Then she removed her glasses, squinting a bit at the screen then at Agatha, before giving her a grin as she tucked them back into her pocket.

“Ready?” she asked. “They’re gonna go pretty quickly, so think about what poses you wanna do and just go for it, okay?”

Agatha thought about what she wanted to do. She thought about which poses she wanted to copy. She could see them very clearly in her mind, her heart racing as she glanced at Rio’s face in their reflection on the screen.

“Ready,” she said, somewhat breathlessly.

Rio reached forward and pressed ‘START’ on the screen.

“Okay, first up,” she said as a countdown from three began on the screen. “First one is always just a smile.” She gave a big, gap-toothed grin to the screen. Agatha followed suit, not missing the way Rio’s eyes flitted in her direction just as the screen flashed.

The next time, Rio held up two fingers and smiled directly at the screen, but her arm wound its way around Agatha’s waist, pulling her more closely. Agatha grinned even more widely, scrunching up her face a bit for a goofier smile as she felt Rio’s fingers prodding at her tummy. She let out a tiny snort as the screen flashed for a second time.

For the third picture, Agatha glanced sideways at Rio, who still had her arm wrapped around her waist. In turn, Agatha wrapped her arm around Rio’s shoulder and pulled her toward herself, making the split-second decision to place her lips against Rio’s cheek, watching in delight as Rio’s mouth gaped open and her eyes widened just as the screen flashed.

When Agatha pulled back, she turned to look at Rio, who was staring at her, wide-eyed. Agatha couldn’t resist looking down at her lips before quickly looking back up into her eyes. Her heart was hammering in her chest and the flutters were nonstop as she leaned in, both of her hands now cupping Rio’s cheeks, pulling her face closer and leaning in.

Neither of even noticed the flash of the screen as their lips connected, Rio’s hands grasping Agatha’s wrists as if to keep her in place. As if Agatha would ever dream of pulling away when Rio’s lips were as soft and warm as they were. She found herself doing the opposite, in fact, as she sunk deeper into Rio’s embrace.

The other woman’s grip tightened on her wrists as she tilted her head a bit, deepening the kiss. Agatha let out a soft, involuntary moan into her mouth, her fingers sinking more into Rio’s dark tresses as she pressed forward. She felt her lungs begin to scream as they begged for air, but it seemed more important to keep Rio’s lips on hers than it did to breath.

Rio, on the other hand, soon pulled back with a soft gasp.

“Sorry,” she said. “Sorry. Needed…air…breathe…wow.”

She pressed her forehead against Agatha’s, their noses brushed as their breath mingled between them. Agatha was taking the chance to breath in some fresh air, as well. Well, fresh air mixed with Rio’s breath, which smelled sweet.

“Yeah,” Agatha agreed, after a long moment, her lips turning up in a tiny grin. “Wow.”

Rio grinned at her and leaned in again, their lips just barely brushing as another voice broke through the little moment they were having.

“Yeah.” This voice was deeper. “Wow.”

Rio’s eyes popped open like cartoon and she glanced over Agatha’s shoulder. Agatha turned to see the figure of somebody tall and broad on the other side of the thin sheet separating them from the outside. She cautiously pulled it to the side to see a man standing there, holding a little girl on his waist and covering her eyes as she fought to see around his hand, giggling like it was a fun little game they were playing.

“Sh—uh, shoot,” Rio said behind Agatha. “Sorry!”

Her hands fell away from Agatha’s wrists and the two of them quickly scrambled out of the booth, Agatha reaching for her bunny as Rio snatched the paper that came out of the little slot on the side of the box.

“No worries!” the man called, chuckling. “Love is love and all that!” He was flushing as he slid into the booth, his little girl craning her neck to watch as Rio and Agatha fast-walked away from it, her little hand waving at them. Agatha waved back with a tiny grin, and the girl giggled as her father closed the curtain on them.

When she turned back toward Rio, she found the other girl already looking back down at her, an awed grin on her face as they continued to speedwalk to…wherever it was they were going. Neither of them was really paying any attention until they bumped into another man.

“Oop,” Rio said, practically bouncing back from the bigger man’s body. “Sor—Dr. Banner?!” she exclaimed.

The taller man gave her a wide smile. “Rio Vidal!” he said. “How’ve you been, kiddo?”

Rio glanced down at where hers and Agatha’s hands were laced together once again and blushed as she looked back up at Dr. Banner. “Can’t complain,” she said.

Dr. Banner looked between them, his grin growing by the second. “Looks like it,” he said, giving her a wink before turning to Agatha. “Nice to meet you, Miss…”

“Agatha,” Agatha said. “Agatha Harkness.”

“Agatha Harkness,” Dr. Banner echoed, nodded. “Good name. Scottish, I think.”

Agatha nodded. “Okay,” she said, blankly.

Another stray firework went off in the distance, lighting the plaza up with a purple glow that just barely caught the two figures moving further and further away.

“Um…hey,” she said, staring down at the shark. Her favorite animal. Potentially one of the most dangerous fish in the ocean. He stared at her, wide-eyed and mouth open, exposing the sharp ridges of so many teeth.

“Don’t be scared,” Agatha said. “He’s friendly.”

Rio turned to her with furrowed brows. “How did you find this out?” she asked. “How are you friends with a shark?” She frowned. “How come shit like this never happens to me?”

Agatha snorted at that, then she frowned, looking down.

“Scratchy and I go way back,” she said, stroking his back, her hand running down the length of his body. He rubbed against her legs again, doing the same thing to Rio as he made another circle around their bodies. “I know this is…odd,” she said, “and I have a lot to explain, but, for right now, can we just…stay like this?” She still had one arm wrapped around Rio’s middle, their bodies still pressed together. “Please?”

Rio took a deep breath and nodded, pressing her lips to Agatha’s head as the shark – Scratchy, apparently – continued to circle them.

“As you wish,” she said, wrapping one arm around Agatha’s shoulders and using her opposite hand to reach down, stroking the shark’s back as he bumped against her knee.
